The Red Page
DEAD HEART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What if my heart should die and I did not
know!
If I saw the nodding dahlias come and go
With no delighted start —•
Went cold in September's sun where the
swallows dart
Close to my face
In their aery race —
Whom, whom should I curse for my dying
heart?
If, in the windy pools along the shore,
The sun its silver dapples made no more!
If the slow-falling shuffle of the sea
Made no unspeakable yearning move in me!
And, when the dawn came loaded with new
fate
For flowers and clouds and men, should my
high gait
Spurn in its pride the hedges that I pass?
The white geraniums near the green lawn
grass?
Did I spurn the jewels that a wet night
leaves
Swung from web-wheels that the spider
weaves —
If I lost my faith in a late sun ray,
If the glory of daffodils slipt away;
If l . looked on romping dogs without a move
Saying that ins that there was nothing to
love;
If I dreamed I saw
What revealeth now, but saw it not, and awe
Stole from my soul through some constituent
flaw!
If dark gleams of the cliffs where turns the
creek
Harbored but darkness and no gnomes that
speak
Truth and delight!
And oh, the night!
What if I lost
The steel-blue color of a night of frost!
If the gleam of lighted windows on the hill
Found this heart steady still; I
If a tall white house on a pine-clad rise
Probed a cold heart through unresponsive
eyes,
If the dawn-trot of an early market load
Broke me no joy from out its hidden road,
From the song of its sleepy men —-
t should gladly die then.
FURNLEY MAURICE.
Wilde
“You’ll dine your man in Paris to-mor-
row,” remarked the Solicitor-General to
Edward Clarke, Wilde’s counsel, at the con-
clusion of the third trial of Oscar Wilde.
But Wilde’s counsel, waiting for the return
of the jury, was less hopeful. And Wilde
went to gaol for two years.
The opportunity is now given, by the pub-
lication of Oscar Wilde: Three Times Tried,
a big volume, giving, with some necessary-
suppression of detail, the full report of the
three trials in which Wilde figured, not only
of judging whether Wilde was guilty, but
whether the evidence submitted Vas suffi-
cient for that verdict. The conclusion, if a
lay critic may venture his opinion—-the con-
clusion that as a juryman this critic would
have reached —is that Wilde was undoubt-
edly guilty, but that the evidence was
not strong enough for a conviction. That
conclusion the jury of the second trial evi-
dently reached, since the jury was unable
to agree. Wilde was sent to gaol by Wow-
sers.... but Wilde deserved gaol. “Onfie in
six or seven years,” wrote Macaulay, “our
virtue becomes outrageous.”
J*
This, according to the author, is the first
accurate account of the trials; and it gains
in picturesqueness by indicating the manner
in which the accused and the witnesses com-
ported themselves in the box. The proceed-
ings were instituted by one Queensberry, the
father of Alfred Douglas, both bizarre speci-
mens of England’s aristocracy. The father,
maddened by his son’s intimacy with Wilde,
left a card for the latter at the Albemarle
Club. This Was handed to Wilde by the
porter. On it was written Wilde’s name and
a statement, not that he was guilty of a cer-
tain crime, but that he was “posing” as
such a criminal. Wilde had no choice then
but to sue Queensberry for criminal libel.
Queensberry was a short man with a round
face, clean-shaven except for a streak of
red whisker, and, a drooping lip. His plea
was justification. He accused Wilde of defi-
nite acts of immorality. And as the case
proceeded revelation follpwed revelation,
and the plaintiff found himself in the posi-
tion of defendant. His cross-examination
led to admissions that could not be over-
lopked. It was an unfair cross-examination
in part, since it dealt with Wilde’s works,
and even with a story that had not been
written by Wilde, but had merely appeared
in a magazine, afterwards suppressed, to
which Wilde had contributed. But the
damning part of the cross-examination re-
vealed the possession in the hands of the
defendant of a body of painful evidence. As
always, stolen letters came into the wrong
hands. Wilde’s justification of one of these
letters was that it was a prose poem, and
his tenancy of the witness-box was the occa-
sion for a brilliant flow of impudent and
delightful paradox. And Wilde’s counsel,
with the acquiescence of his client, with-
drew, agreeing to a verdict of Not Guilty.
Wilde’s arrest naturally followed.
It should be noted that Wilde would have
had plenty of time to leave the country had
he wished to do so; but apparently he was
so confident of being acquitted that he was
determined to remain and face any charges
that might be made against him. It is to be
regretted that Wilde was not persuaded by
his friends to go abroad —at least, for a
time —until the violent prejudice which had
been imported into the case, and worked up
against him by the baser section of the
gutter press of London, had calmed down.
However, he was informed by a journalist
that a warrant had been issued for his
arrest, and he waited at the hotel for what-
ever developments might arise.
J*
At the time of his arrest two of Wilde’s
plays were being performed in London, The
Importance of Being Earnest and An Ideal
Husband. The last night of the latter play
had already been announced, but the former
continued running for another month, the
author’s name having been removed, with
delightful hypocrisy, from all advertisements
and programmes, both in London and in
America. It may be mentioned that the
offence of which Wilde was charged had
become a crime only in 1886, when an
amendment so constituting it was inserted
in a bill dealing with immorality. Prior to
1886 this offence was not a crime—nor is it
a crime to-day in several European coun-
tries.
It will be noticed that from the moment
of his arrest Wilde was treated as guilty by
almost everybody, especially by the lower
class press, which did its best to stir up
prejudice against him. It is stated that he
was all the time treated more harshly than
an ordinary prisoner would have been.
The charge was one of misdemeanor, not
of felony. Yet Wilde was refused bail while
on remand. It is stated, however, that it
was obvious from the first that the Treasury
had been anxious to avoid overloading the
case against the accused, rather than press
the charge. During this second trial Wilde
had changed in a startling degree. His face
was haggard and grey, his cheeks seemed
fallen in, his hair unkempt, and his general
mien was one of great depression and sudden
age. Later on, a sickly pallor seized
upon his countenance, and he betrayed a
great deal of nervous activity.
J*
With him in the dock was Alfred Taylor,
charged with him with conspiring. The
indictment contained 25 counts, all of them
amounting only to misdemeanor. During
the course of this trial Wilde, asked to ex-
plain the meaning of a poem by Alfred
Douglas, made that wonderful speech in his
own defence which has been declared to be
“the finest speech of an accused man since
that of Paul before Agrippa.” As literature,
it is, in Robert Buchanan’s words, indeed
“marvellous.” It is said to have (hrilled
every one in the court. Unfortunately,
judging by the evidence, it was not true. In
the witness-box, under the shrewdest cross-
examination, Wilde acquitted himself with
dignity, and his willingness to enter the
witness-box stood in his favor with the jury.
The jury disagreed; but the weakness of
the case may be seen from the fact that out
of the 25 counts in the original indictment
two were struck out by the Judge, three,
were abandoned by the prosecution, on three
counts the two defendants were found not
guilty, and on the rest the jury returned no
verdict. The prosecution was unfortunate
in having to rely for their chief evidence on
disreputable persons, most of them black-
mailers ; but it is difficult to see, in a case
of this sort, where any but this sort of tes-
timony could have been found. Wilde was
this time released on bail while awaiting
what was practically his third trial. One
of his sureties was a clergyman, Stewart
Headlam, who stated that he had under-
taken the responsibility on public grounds.
He felt that the public mind had been pre-
judiced before the trial began. It is said that,
though at this time Wilde had been found
guilty of no offence, he was refused admis-
sion at more than one hotel on the night of
his release, and that he was followed about
by a gang of roughs.
In the next trial the defendants were
separately tried. Parker’s case wqs taken
first, and he was found guilty on two counts.
Wilde’s counsel had protested against his
client having to wait till Parker’s case had
been settled, on the ground that a verdict
given against Parker would prejudice his
own client’s case; but he was overruled. Of
course, had Parker been acquitted, Wilde
would have had the benefit of- that verdict.
It was Wilde’s own counsel who had asked
that the two defendants should be tried
separately, but Edward Clarke was beaten
by the prosecution deciding to take Parker’s
case first. It is probe ole that had Edward
Clarke got Wilde’s case taken first, it would
have been extiemely difficult to obtain
Wilde’s conviction. It was, however, in
Wilde’s favor that the counts alleging con-
spiracy with Taylor had been withdrawn at
the previous trial, and that the jury at
Parker’s trial had been unable to agree on
other counts which would have implicated
Wilde.
Wilde was found guilty on seven counts
—all except one. Sentence,in Parker’s case
had been deferred; and each, after a scath-
ing address irom Uie Judge, was sentenced
to two years’ hard labor —the severest sen-
tence that the law allowed. Analysing the
verdict, the author says, “Wilde was con-
victed on the evidence of two blackmailers
and some hysterical servant girls. The
former had been described as belonging to a
gang of the vilest type of men produced by
great cities; the evidence of the latter, his
Lordship had declared, was not fit to hang a
dog upon.” The author also finds fault with
the -unnecessary virulence in the tone of the
Solicitor-General, who conducted the case
for the prosecution.
However,
Wilde was guilty.
The Rea l South Africa
According to Ambrose Pratt The Real
South Africa (Bell, per Dymock’s Library)
is situated in the suburbs of Hades.
“The vast bulk of the sub-continent is a
barren, pest-ridden wilderness, where only
the black man can live or hope to thrive.
One passes across thousands of miles of
monotonous veld, and sees no cultivation, no
stock worth owning, no sign of comfortable
or civilised habitation. The British are
walled and cooped up in the mushroom
cities: the Boers are scattered sparsely
through the lonely plains. The British
think only of winning riches from the
mines. The Boers, absolutely ambitionless,
think only of wanning a meagre livelihood
from the sour and reluctant soil: and both
races alike, corrupted and enervated by
their long and uninterrupted dependence on
the blacks, ‘laze’ along, do no work save
overseeing work, and prosper, or exist, as
they prefer, on the slavish toil of the down-
trodden aboriginal inhabitants... .On the
veld....the blacks are everywhere, and con-
tact with them brutalises and depraves the
whites. The landscape is either dreadfully
monotonous or appallingly grand, but. always
the eye wanders in a miserable, baffled quest
for a sign of trees.... Dust storms sweep
across the plains with every wind-whirling
maelstroms of blinding sand and powdered
soil —and this even in the heart of the rainy
season of the year. They call the herbage
that covers these plains grass....lt grows
in far separated clumps—bundles of wire
and weeds. South Africa has been settled
for several hundreds of years: it has been
fought for and bled for by many peoples:
to-day its to,tal white population scarcely
exceeds 1,200,000 souls..,.The natives are
so physically vigorous and mentally virile,
their numbers are so vast, thei-f rate of
natural increase is so great and rapid, and
their desire to exceed the bounds of the
white man’s caste prejudice is so keen that
it is staringly obvious that their aspirations
must be restricted and repressed as a con-
dition precedent to the preservation of white
supremacy The whites at one and the
same time hate, fear, despise and use the
blacks. They cannot do without them.
South African civilisation is a marble palace
built upon a quagmire of black, heaving
bodies. Shake or disturb the essential foun-
dation of cheap, colored labor, and it would
crumble on instant... .Christianised negroes
have churches of their own, and did any
black venture to invade the portals of the
white man’s house of worship he would not
merely be driven forth; his impertinence
would be rewarded with the whip. Mil-
lionaires and mechanics view the negro
through cognate sets of spectacles. The
millionaire wants plenty of unskilled
cheap labor. The mechanic wants a mono-
poly of the skilled labor market, and, being
lazy, he also wants cheap black industrial
valets to perform the rougher portions of
his work The blacks are given all the
unskilled labor in the land to do, and be-
cause they do it at the call of caste, that
labor is proscribed from white performance.
Thus the class of ‘noor whites’ originated.
Every town and city has its unlisted legion
of such men. They are unskilled laborers.
They would be glad to do unskilled work in
any other land: but in South Africa they
would not if they dared, and dare not if
they would, for the punishment of their
offence would be eternal ostracism from their
kind... .Factories there are almost none.”
A pleasant picture! Ambrose Pratt’s
skilled journalist’s pen fills in the dark de-
tails. South Africa, is a black man’s coun-
try. Climate, disease, the ratio of increase
between black and white, will keep it so.
At present the blacks outnumber the whites
within the Union by four to one —and there
are millions of blacks just outside. A car-
penter walks to his job with a Kaffir behind
him carrying his bag. Each white girl, going
to school, is followed by a Kaffir carrying her
school-books. And the Kaffirs are not like
the Australian blacks: they are a capable
and ambitious race —skilled and dexterous
craftsmen. The whole race is infected
with a craze for education. There are
more black than white children attending
the public schools.
And right in the centre o| the Union lies
the blacks’ fortress of Basutoland, impreg-
nable, held by a third of a ihillion Basutos,
armed and unconquered. The land is full of
savings banks, its exports are rapidly increas-
ing, the kraals are giving place to well-built
houses, picked scholars are every year sent
abroad to be educated; the color-line is
sternly drawn at the borders against the
wuite man, the farmers use steam ploughs
and harvest by machinery, factories ar>
being built. And the Basutos are soldiers
by tradition and are armed with modern
weapons. And what is the Union going to
do with this black volcano in its midst V
The real Boer is pictured as a dirty re-
trogressive waster. The death-rate of the
miners in the Rand is dreadful; but Pratt
supplies statistics and official reports for his
conclusions. And South Africa has practi-
cally no indigenous literature. And the
sub-continent knows drought as, happily
Australia has never known it.
The one cheerful aspect in this dark pic-
ture is Pratt’s account of the municipal
Socialism of the cities. Each city owns all
its civic utilities, and the experiment of
municipal Socialism has everywhere been
so successful that even State bakeries are
in existence, while municipal dairies and
food production and distribution are
authoritatively advocated as the next steps.
Yet South Africa has never had room for a
Labor party. It is governed by Fat— and
Fat, haying the sense to see that Private
Enterprise was, as usual, bringing along his
commanding officer, General Exploitation-
decided to experiment with municipal con-
trol. “Thus the world has been afforded
the anomalous and amusing spectacle of a
purely capitalistic State outdistancing in
Socialism the Socialists themselves.”
Wanted—A November Sonnet
The entries for the competition for the
best sonnet on our Australian October must
beach this page by November 3. Meantime
sonneteers may be trying their pens ou
November. Half a guinea will be paid for
the best sonnet on our November, provided
that entries are addressed to this page and
received by us by December 6. Competitors
will note that we do not desire sonnets that
end in a riming couplet.
• __
In protest against the action of The Cir-
culating Libraries’ Association, the league
of London subscription libraries that has
tak-_. upon itself to censor books, this page
will, from time to time, give publicity to the
books that have been banned. The Associa-
tion states: “We are tradesmen; our cus-
tomers expect us to supply them only with
matter suitable for family reading; parents
will complain if they find in their daughters’
hands books which they consider unfit for
the young; and we, being tradesmen, must so
conduct our business as to avoid giving our
customers ground for complaint.” A curious
idea these tradesmen have of the purpose of
literature. They still believe authors write
for the Young Person—who, by the way,
must not be confused with the Flapper. The
method adopted by these libraries is to
classify the books at their own discretion,
placing in “Class B” some which are not
“offered.” These books do not appear on
the printed catalogues, are not displayed on
bookstalls, shelves or counters, or sent out
in parcels selected by the library authori-
ties. If. perchance, a customer, having dis-
covered the name of a censored book, asks
for it, it is, apparently after some delay, sup-
plied. “Thus,” says The Athenaeum, “the
associated libraries hold and wield at their
pleasure a weapon with which they can
seriously injure persons engaged in produc-
ing those wares in which they themselves
are dealers.” The only way to counteract
this attempt on the part of a trust of trades-
men to censor fiction in order to preserve
the alleged virginity of the minds of Young
Persons is to give a free advertisement to
such censored books. The most recent of
the banned books are: The Woman Thou
Caves t Me (Hall Caine), The World’s
Daughter (Cyril Harcourt), The Devil’s
Garden (W. B. Maxwell), Sinister Street
(Compton Mackenzie). And this page will
erdeavor to review all such censored works
—with, of course, the obvious exception of
Hall Caine. There are some books that are
beyond criticism —on this page.
A new edition of Lost England, by
Beckles Willson, in the compact shilling
“Useful Knowledge” series (Hodder and
Stoughton, per George Robertson, Sydney),
calls attention to the striking fact that
England is gradually disappearing. The
good ship, England, has been scuttled, and
she is slowly, but inevitably, settling down.
All round her coasts marine erosion is going
on. “For hundreds of miles on the English
coasts are buried once prosperous towns and
villages and mighty forests?” Despite re-
clamations, the balance against England is
hundreds of square miles lost to the sea.
Yet there is a compensation in the disap-
pearance of the vast tract of land between
Land’s End and the Scill.y Isles, for this
was the Lyonesse of tradition, and many a
poet has niade a good living out of Lyonesse.

Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

TRAGIC END.
Sudden Death as Result of Burst
Varicose Veins.
A COMMON OCCURRENCE.
<*• The above is not an unusual headline S»
in the daily newspapers, and in nearly
every instance the tragedy could have been avoided,
except that traditional belief in man’s incompetence
had been responsible for misguiding the victim at a
time when the proper relief could have been availed
of and all danger avoided.
The Booklet on Varicose Veins and Ulcers published
by the Vecsey Varix-arium, 3103 Ash Street, Sydney,
if carefully read by any sufferer from this unrelenting
complaint, will quickly convince the reader that the
old belief is founded purely on superstition, combined
with the failure of the old school of medicine to cope
with the trouble.
Varicosity is a subject discussed by many, but
entirely misunderstood, and it remained for an
enthusiastic and persistent layman to discover the
treatment that would eradicate it. In this booklet
the subject is fully explained, and more can be
learned about the disease by an hour’s perusal of
this valuable little booklet than can be learned from
weeks of delving into medical tomes.
Every person who is afflicted should at least have
a copy. It is most enlightening, and gives useful
hints on how trouble may be avoided, even though
treatment for the elimination of the trouble is not
undertaken.
Scores of successfully treated cases are cited to
prove the arguments put forward, and it is only rea-
sonable to suppose that with old traditions knocked
on the head, and modern remedies given a chance,
that what can be done for one person can be as
readily done for another.
If space permitted, dozens of letters like the fol-
lowing could be quoted here: —
Mrs. P. A. Kelly, Nareeb Nareeb, Vic., wrote
recently: “I did not write you further, as I had
paid my full fee for treatment, and found myself
cured at the end of the course, as you promised
should be the case. Also, I desired to wait a while
to see if the results justified your claims as to their
permanency.
“Nothing now remains to show where the bad
veins were except a slight discoloration of the skin
at the spots where the patches were so bad, and I
can only thank you sincerely for my great cure and
wish you continued success in your splendid work.”
One noticeable feature amongst sufferers from this
complaint is the strong tendency of the afflicted per-
sons to put off seeking assistance, principally because
in the primary stages there does not appear to be
any danger, and later on from advice obtained
amongst friends “that there is no cure.” It is these
causes that send so many old people to the Institute
when they hear finally of the Vecsey Institute method
—poor old sufferers who have for years never known
a minute’s respite from aggravating, and, in thou-
sands of cases, agonising pain.
Could the fresh victims of the complaint only see
a few of these, and realise what is in store for those
who delay, wild horses would not restrain them in
their desire to get all the information available on
the subject.
The Vecsey Varix-arium offers every assistance,
free of charge, to those who would learn all about
this wonderful method, and no sufferer should be
without their book on bandaging and the care of
varicose veins. A copy will be posted free to any
address upon receipt of a 2d. stamp. (2804)

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Smith’s Life-Work.
The miserable bodysnatcher !**— Professional inter-
I tection made by attempted Australian statesman,
during working hours and in the ordinary way of
business.
A large, dull man was Caesar Smith
Who represented Monolith.
He believed in ev’ry party myth ;
Each had the loyal aid of Smith.
His vote was never once in doubt;
Few were the things he talked about.
For Caesar Smith had quite a lack
Of notions. So he just sat back
And looked as wise as any owl;
He was a slow but wary fowl.
The years went by, but Caesar Smith
Scarce said a word for Monolith.
Meanwhile he cut vile Labor’s throat
By sawing at it with his vote.
’Twas noble work, but Caesar he
Gained by it scant publicity.
One had to search through Hansard for
The pars wherein Smith used to pour
His pint of knowledge. Monolith
Got little chance to read of Smith.
Till he began to specialise
In interjections—sudden cries
Of Lib’ral rage, designed to blast
And ridicule on Labor cast.
A heap of shots went wide, but Smith
Shed glory on glad Monolith
When he designed one graveyard taunt —
A fierce gibe guaranteed to haunt
A man throughout one passing year,
And tickle ev’ry Liberal ear.
Then Smith sat back. His work was o’er,
And Monolith could ask no more.
He’d done his best. He did not fret;
That was as near as he could get
To stateinanship. For years he drew
His handsome and unfailing screw.
There came no kick from Monolith ;
They knew they’d got the lot of Smith.
N.S.W.
INCURABLE UNDERTAKER.
Perjury.
He (Judge Box) regretted to say that the crime
of perjury was far too prevalent in Victoria. It was
a dreadful crime. —Daily paper.
Truth is hard and rusty; Life’s a heavy job;
Hear the springs and axles scrape, and
squeak, and sob ;
Make the wheels go smoothly and the miles
slip by
With an application of the oily lie.
Stop the waste by friction. Ease the en-
gines’ strain ;
Broken parts can never be replaced again.
Heed no moralising. Wink the other eye.
Lubricate where needed with the easy lie.
Death’s foul blow is softened —Fate’s worst
wounds are naught
When the kindly liar anodyne has brought.
First aid for the wounded! Someone’s
always by
Ready with a potent, chloroforming lie.
N.S.W.
PERJURER.
A Prison Dialogue.
“Stone walls do not a prison make,
Nor iron bars a cage” ;
Your hallowed soul shall yet awake
To claim its heritage.
Yet this I know, oh, poet queer:
Stone walls and bars still keep me here.
Your body does not count for much,
So long as thought is free.
Now, who amongst us all may clutch
That straw of liberty?
What use that thoughts in Heaven dwell,
If feet still tramp the narrow cell?
In fancy you may see the skies,
The mountains and the trees,
The morning, golden with surprise,
The shimmer of the seas.
Whate’er avails the landscape fair
When we are breathing prison air?
And you have mem’ry at your side —
The thoughts of days agone
Like loving servitors abide,
And leave you hoping on.
But there are also bitter things —
Ah, Memory too deeply stings!
And you your freedom will regain,
With strength to live anew
Your dreams —the dreams that were so vain
Ere Folly fettered you.
Ah, strength! Ah, strength!—its hollow
sham!
For strength has made me what I am!
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.
His Visioning.
He walked the narrow and the noisy street,
And ever as he walked the dream
recurred :
His soul dragged slowly on with shackled
feet,
And dirge and requiem alone he heard.
All big with promise rose the glorious dawn ;
All dark with broken hopes the sun went
down,
An outcast soul in sombre gloom withdrawn
He wandered lonely through the brazen
town.
At night (his cares forgotten) regally
He treads on purple, crowned with dreams
of bliss.
Large laurels wreath him; yea—sublimely
he
Rides triumphing through proud Perse-
polis.
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Corresponacnts to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and > to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone py name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER G. 1013.
THE PROPHET OF LEAST
RESISTANCE.
There have been large prophets and small;
fat prophets (a few) and lean prophets
(very many) ; slow prophets, like Moses,
who had to get an agent to speak for him;
and flying prophets, like Elijah, who ran
half across the kingdom in front of Aiiab’s
chariot at the breaking of the drought.
There have been thick prophets, and
prophets whose tale was very thin. Morose,
hairy prophets have been known who lived
in caves and were suspected of a fly-whisk
or caudal appendage; and as a set-off the
world has produced cheerful prophets who
joined in the annual picnic of the congrega-
tion along with Bunstruggle, the High
Priest, and Plate, the deacon. Also there
has never any lack of the prophet who
took the line of least resistance, and the
name of one“-of them in the present genera-
tion is HiGGfe. Speaking in the House of
Representatives a few days ago—it was the
dnr when the inconsequent Ryrie called him
'•You miserable body-snatcher”—about the
absurdity of providing for the defence of
Australia, this seer sowed the bad seed as
follows: —
1 propose to prove that we are going too fast, par-
ticularly in the matter of naval expenditure. Certain
people take the view that the awakening of China is
fraught with danger to Australia, and that we are
also in danger from Japan. 1 believe, however, that
we have no reason to anticipate danger from Japan.
In the first place, that country, 1 read, is over-
burdened with debt. Authorities tell us that an
attacking force must be five times as strong as a
defending force. That was about the position in the
case of the South African War. The Boer forces con-
sisted of about 50,000 soldiers, and Great Britain
had to send out 250,000 to defeat them. That war
cost the Old Country £.211,000.000. Is Japan in a
position to send an attacking force of 250,000 men
to Australia, or to find £211,000,000 to provide for
the cost of an Australian invasion ?
Then, again, I would ask whether it is likely that
the civilised races of the earth would allow Japan to
take hold of Australia. Have not Germany, France
and the United States of America, as well as Great
Britain and other nations, interests in Australia
which they would hasten to protect the moment they
were threatened ? I propose to show why we have
no right to incur this large expenditure in respect of
naval defence. Capitalists— most of them in London
—consisting not only of Anglo-Saxons and Celts, but
of people of all nationalities who have settled in
England, have interests in Australia, as well as in
other parts of the world, and they should be called
upon to pay the money necessary to maintain the
Navy if they want it.
All of which is very comforting, but —!
And again but — !
The first element in this consoling theory
is that Japan is overwhelmed with debt,
therefore it can’t attack Australia. Yet
Japan owes only £5 per inhabitant, and
though it is a poor country it is steadily
growing richer. Also there are ways of
getting out' of debt in a great emergency.
Austria, after it had carried the heat and
burden of the Napoleonic wars, went insol-
vent twice, and the final clean-up was at the
rate of about 2d. in the £. Russia solemnly
filed its schedule in the ’forties of last century.
Some of the State Legislatures of the U.S.
simply told their creditors they would see
them hanged first, and paid nothing at all.
The creditors are still hanged first. Spain
repudiated at least two debts bodily, and
compounded on one or two more, and once
it didn’t pay the composition. And there
are many other examples, including that of
Italy, which once levied so much income-tax
on its creditors’ dividends that the dividends
fell very considerably. A country with a
sufficiently good army and navy and a suffi-
ciently bad conscience can always get out
of debt if the circumstances call for a big
effort. There isn’t the slightest reason to
suspect that Japan or any other existing
Great Power would contemplate these
drastic old methods, still it isn’t well to rely
too much on a nation’s debt (a debt of £5
per head) as a guarantee of its peaceful-
ness. To get down from the wholesale to
the retail, the _ late Henry Parkes was
almost always in debt, but his peacefulness
was an unknown quantity.
Then, again, there are States which go to
war most cheerfully when they are most
dead broke. When the original Napoleon
first came to the front he had charge of a
force that was almost naked and shoeless,
ill-fed, ill-armed, wholly unpaid, and backed
by a Government which nad practically
given up printing paper money for want of
funds with which to buy the paper and the
ink. For many years he not only made war
pay for itself, but made it show huge profits.
He not only stole the land and money of the
conquered States, but he embezzled their
pictures, statues and furniture. The Mah-
ratta Confederacy in its great days always
expected its generals to make conquest pay,
and also to declare a dividend when they
came home. It struck the beginnings of
ruin when it sacked its greatest general for
failing to declare the usual dividend, and put
the Chancellor of the Exchequer in his place.
If it hadn’t carried the principle of cheap
war to an extreme it might be a flourishing
State to-day. It is a common saying that
Turkey’s chronic impecuniosity never pre-
vented it going out and killing somebody.
When it was poorest it had the greatest
desire to feel in the pockets of the dead for
any loose assets. Japan is a country which
conducts war on fairly inexpensive prin-
ciples. It eats and drinks cheaply, builds
its ships cheaply, and pays amazingly low
wages. It has 230.000 men always under
art"", and they could probably live as easily
in Sydney as in Tokio. It has a great array
of ships, and they could probably get coal
at Newcastle for a lower figure than at
Kobe. There are certainly difficulties in
campaigning with little money, but the thing
has been done, and it may happen again.
The Boer war, on which Higgs, M.H.R.,
bases his theory that an attacking force
must be five titnes as strong as a defending
one, is a tired and threadbare circumstance
nowadays. Because Australia sent a few
contingents to the Boer scrap, certain poli-
ticians have a habit of regarding it as the
whole history, narrative and embodiment of
war —and it isn’t. Who the “authorities”
may be who support his amazing view
Higgs doesn’t say, but it would be inter-
esting to know their names. Assuredly in
the successive conquests of England by
Romans, Saxons, Danes and Normans the
invading force was the smaller. The British
conquered India and Burma with mere
handfuls. The Saracens built their empire
against immense odds. The Japanese
weren’t anything dimly resembling five to
one when they seized Korea, nor were the
Germans in their invasion of France in
1870. The “authorities” from whom Higgs
thinks he got his comforting information
appear a dubious lot. and on a rough esti-
mate three-fourths of the evidence of his-
tory is against them. Certainly in a rare
case like that of the South African annoy-
ance. when the assailant is a tortoise and
the defendant is an ostrich for speed, and a
very hard-fighting bird at. that; when there
are no bloated coastal cities to defend, and
one slouch-hatted rider can defy all the
fleets on all the seas; and the wealth of the
country is so widely diffused that there is
no one supremely vulnerable spot—in such a
case it may require even a great deal more
than five-fold odds to effect a conquest. But
the instance is one in a thousand.
*■ *, * • * *
Finally, there is the comforting assurance
that we owe so much money that our old
friend Three-Ball must rush to our assist-
ance—just as he didn’t rush worth a cent
when Russia and Austria and France and
Turkey and Greece and Spain and Portugal
and Peru and Guatemala and Honduras and
Argentina, also Virginia, North and South
Carolina, Georgia, Florida. Alabama, Mis-
sissippi, Louisiana and Arkansas, to say
nothing of Jeff Davis’s Confederate States
and Hayti and other institutions either
broke, burst, compounded or repudiated.
Certainly an heroic attempt was once made
to collect a bad debt from Mexico, and the
attempt ended in the execution, of one em-
peror and the downfall of another. The
facts of the case are: (1) Tiiree-Bael
doesn’t care for fighting. It pays him better
to write off the bad debt and keep on at lus
business of lending money on the bill of
sale. (2) Though Australia owes money to
Great Britain, France, Germany, the
United States and other countries, still it
probably owes money to only one person out
of a hundred in these countries, and the
other 99 have as much objection to shedding
their blood in defence of their capitalist as
Higgs, M.H.R., has for shedding his gore
on behalf of the Van Diemen’s Land Co. or
the Daniel Cooper estate. If some hostile
person wrecked the U.S. Meat Trust’s in-
terests in Queensland quite a number of
U.S. citizens would rather throw up their
hats than go to war. (3) Governments
hate to take unlimited risks in defence of a
limited amount of money and they loathe
distant adventures, because they never know
which immediate neighbor will bite them in
the tail while their forces are away. All
these are reasons why we can’t rely too
strongly on our good creditor, Tiiree-Ball,
to defend us. Australia owes a lot of
money, but it would require to owe ten
times as much before it could depend on an
overwhelming force of armed pawnbrokers
to come to its assistance. And if it owed
ten times as much it would be broke, and
the pawnbrokers would .be more likely to
fight against us than for us.
And the Prophet of Least Resistance,
Higgs, M.H.R., reasons on another false
basis, though it is certainly a very common
basis. If a powerful enemy, such as Japan,
tried to annex this thinly-peopled and ill-
defended country at a time when Britain
was otherwise engaged, it can’t be imagined
that it would commence operations by
threatening all our foreign creditors, scat-
tered among the Great Powers of the world,
with confiscation of their Australian inter-
ests. That theory rests on the assumption
that our only possible assailant is an armed
and embattled lunatic asylum. The hostile
person would be much more likely to begin
with a strenuous assurance that all foreign
property would be rigidly respected, and
that the interest on the foreign debt would
be paid in full, even if it was necessary to
enslave Higgs, M.H.R.. and set him to earn-
ing money as an animal of burden in the
shafts of a cart to provide the funds. The
hostile person would, in fact, put his name
on the back of our bill, and thus claim to
have rather improved the security, and a
lot of our creditors would probably consider
the claim valid. He might placate foreign
capital by promising to cut the tariff down
a good deal, which, with cheap Asiatic
labor, he could do without suffering any
injury. He might make promises of rail-
way concessions. Then he could do a good
deal in the way of allaying the fears of the
local capitalist—who isn’t a frenzied fighter
in any case —by expounding to him that his
interests would be respected if he kept quiet,
and that he might even be rewarded, after
the annexation, with an unlimited right to
oppress Higgs, M.H.R., whereas if he re-
sisted, the very worst might happen to him.
These promises might not be all kept in the
end, but there would certainly be no lack of
friendly assurances while the trouble was in
progress.
It is because of considerations like these
that The Bulletin believes in a strong
local defence, strengthened by an influx of
food white population. It doesn’t regard
Britain as a reliable bulwark; that country
is too far away, and has too many responsi-
bilities of its own. and some of these re-
sponsibilities are liable to blow up at any
moment. It has no faith in the vision of
the international pawnbroker coming with
arms and banners and the unexpired pledge
ticket to our defence. There is a shred of
plausibility in the proposition that the work-
ing class shouldn’t be called upon to fight,
and that the big capitalist, as the man with
the largest interests, should carrv the gun.
But if he is entrusted with the gun he may
fire it backwards. Again, unless the big
capitalist is a verv different individual from
the one pictured by ITtggs and his friends,
he wouldn’t carry anything unless driven to
it. His first idea, if the matter was left in
his hands, would probably be to make the
best possible terms for the protection of bis
own investments, and to ask the enemy if he
would kindly enslave the Trade Unions, and
rpgard their forced services in the light of
an indemnity. For these and other reasons
this paper has its eye on Higgs as one of
the prophets whose feet are set on the prim-
rose way—which way is mostly downhill
•with a big drop ’at the end of it.
G. H. REID AND HIS MISSION.
G. H. Reid has come to Australia intent on
getting his one-time colleague, Prime Min-
ister Cook, to give him a further term as
High Commissioner. It will be a calamity
if he succeeds. What Australia wants in
London is a young and energetic man with
the capacity and vitality to push its inter-
ests commercially. The need is particularly
urgent since there is a whole herd of rival
countries, all well represented, pressing tire-
lessly for the means to develop themselves.
The re-appointment of Reid will signify five
years lost to this country in a commercial
sense: and it will mean a further term of
humiliation for those who want to see Aus-
tralia represented in London with a certain
amount of dignity.
* * * *
The Australian who gets all his knowledge
of the High Commissioner and his office
from the local daily papers, and rarely looks
at a London publication, can form no opinion
that is worth anything of the light in which
G. H. Reid is regarded outside the Common-
wealth. The correspondents of various
morning papers are member's of hisrpstaff.
They are excellent and picturesque'irriters.
But in the nature of things they can’t ex-
pose their chief when he errs, and they are
bound to turn the limelight on him whenever
there is an excuse for doing it. The only
other public evidence as to his usefulness (or
otherwise) is furnished by returned travel
lers, most of them fervent liberals, who have
been his personal friends for years and have
been assisted socially by him in England
Thev would not be human beings if thev
testified to his detriment. The consequence
is the spreading broadcast of the grotesque
illusion that Reid has been a stupendous
success in London and an asset of great value
to his adopted land. The facts are depres-
singly otherwise. He began his London
career with a couple of blunders, which in
the eyes of Britons of position were un-
forgivable. The venue was the Royal
Academy banquet, which is the oratorical
function of the London year. The first five
minutes of his address were devoted to a
fulsome eulogy of Edward VII. it i s
imperishable convention of London Society
that the King’s name shall not be mentioned
publicly, except in connection with baldly
formal toasts. Just as in an Indian native
regiment the white officer can do no good
with his men if he is ignorant of their
racial and religious prejudices, so in Eng-
land the overseas Commissioner, or pro-Con-
sul, or ambassador labors under a hopeless
handicap if lie does not know the finer points
of the social game. It may appear ludi-
crous, but there it is. Reid went on to ac-
claim the Royal Academy, and, apropos, he
inquired in pained tones: Why not an
Acauemy of Industrial Art? lie had not
heard, apparently, that such an institution,
had been in existence at South Kensington
for many years. The knowledgeable people
who were not scandalised were amused, hut:
all agreed that persons holding ambassadorial
rank did not do such things.
From then onwards the press ceased
to regard Reid as other than a sort
of joke. One other move, and one
only, was made by Fleet-street to give
his reputation a chance of arising from
its ashes. The occasion was an address
which he was billed to deliver on Australia.
It was felt that he was bound to say some-
thing of worth on this subject. Most of the
big dailies arranged to give him full reports.
Whether his nerve was destroyed by his
primal failure, above mentioned, or whether
he has nothing new or stimulating to say
about the Commonwealth, will never he
known. Anyhow, he weighed in with such
a string of elaborately-guarded platitudes
that the papers did not give him more than
a dozen lines. Since then, despite the non-
sense about his prominence and popularity
that has appeared in the Australian press,
he has been, in every serious sense of the
word, a business and political cipher in
Britain. He suffers incurably from that
slackness which makes life’s spade-work re-
volting to" him. In fact, it is his
detestation of the studv of details in any
shape or form that has been to blame for
most of the errors into which he has crashed.
The disaster that befel him recently in Ger-
many is a striking case in point. As part
of his emigration campaign he formed the
idea of alluring German emigrants to Aus-
tralia. To this end he had shoals of attrac-
tive pamphlets printed in German and circu-
lated through the Fatherland —circulars, ex-
plaining what a uniquely fascinating and'
rich and healthful land Australia was. Not
till the damage had been done did he dis-
cover that, in Germany, where every unit:
of the population is needed for defence pur-
poses, it is an offence to do anything calcu-
lated to unsettle the mind of any German
in such a way as to dispose him to leave his
country. The German authorities won’t in-
terfere, of course, if a citizen wants to_ emi-
grate, but they draw the line at permitting
foreign agents to persuade him to do so. There
were all sorts of diplomatic difficulties to be
surmounted before the High Commissioner’s
office was suffered to withdraw its egregious
literature and promise not to offend again.
George Reid is 69 years old, and a tired
man at that. Providence gave him the
brains to be a big man, but always he has
been too indolent and too ready to grasp at
the immediate benefit —or what looks like the
immediate benefit —and let principle go. The
only real qualification he had in the latter
part of his life for the job to which there is
imminent peril of his being re-appointed was
his capacity to deliver after-dinner speeches.
The London Morning Post reported" the
other day that after some big function*'or
other the High Commissioner for the Com-
monwealth called for “three cheers for the
whole blooming Empire.” Also, being asked
by a pressman what he had done to make
the King laugh so on the same .occasion, he
explained that he had persistently addressed
the monarch as “your Honor.” This variety
of cheap, not to say bankrupt wit, goes down
fairly well at festivals where the majority
have been transformed into temporary lati-
tudinarians by generous supplies of food and
wine. But in any other environment
such waggeries produce a deplorable effect.
In arty case the life of a Commissioner for a
toilsome country like Australia cannot
bo one long after-dinner speech. There is
all sorts of other work to be done. In the
case of G. H. Reid it has been, and must
always be, neglected. His Department puts
to shame, in the matter of muddle and the
like, the War Office itself. Nothing of any
business value is ever accomplished or ever
will be. since, constitutionally, the cluet
is too tired. Australia never gets an adver-
tisement through him that is not a flagrantly
w T rong sort of advertisement. The Common-
wealth needs a representative who will
make fewer jests and do more work; one-
who is young enough to acquire fresh ideas
and energetic enough to carry them out:
and no perverted sense of loyalty to aD
old political ally should keep Cook from
looking for such a man.

Extraordinary Announcement to Agents.
Christmas Edition of
The Bulletin, 1913.
PLEASE TAKE NOTICE that the
CHRISTMAS NUMBER of “THE
BULLETIN” will be an EXTRA
and SPECIAL ISSUE.
Date of Publication: December 13, 1913.
This SPECIAL NUMBER will con-
tain high-class Stories, Verse and other
Literary Contributions from foremost
Australasian writers.
The Illustrations will be supplied by
the popular Bulletin artists, and
others in the front rank of the artistic
world.
The Ordinary Issue of THE BULLETIN
will be published on the usual date.
Agents are requested to note
that, as the Christmas Number of
“The Bulletin” is now going to
press, the orders that reach us from
now on will be treated in order of
priority.
and the official dignity is to hang on to its
gauds and its remuneration. The purpose
of the other crowd is to acquire the gauds
and the remuneration. Parties being so
evenly balanced it is impossible, under the
present system, to do any real work, because
the scramble for the honors and the hono-
rarium occupies all the time. The great
business of the Cook party is to get some
kind of a Bill —almost any kind of foolish
or insane Bill —through the House of Repre-
sentatives, so that its rejection by the
Senate may furnish reason for a dissolution
of both Houses, by which the Liberals may
profit a little and can’t possibly lose much.
The urgent business of the Fisher party is
to prevent any Liberal Bill getting through
the House of Representatives and going up
to the Senate, so that there may be no
deadlock and no double dissolution, with a
possible diminution of the Fisher majority
in the Senate. The Opposition had a moment
of great joy when it shut up the Australian
Legislature for a day through Page, the
Opposition Whip, forgetting that, in case of
the irrepressible Bruce Smith being absent,
he had prdmised to attend and “make a
House.” It had another hour of exuberance
when it defeated the Government owing to
certain Government supporters being away
helping to bury Knox of the Hat. Some
arrangement about “pairs” had broken down,
in a rather confused fashion, and the burial
of poor old Knox enabled a snatch vote to
be secured against the Cook Government. It
didn’t damage the Government, and it didn’t
hurt Knox, who will never wear his Hat
any more, but the country’s business was
hung up once more. Yet the success wasn’t
wholly one-sided. The Cook push came into
its own on the night when Higgs was sus-
pended for reflections on the political morals
of his opponents, while the brilliant and
eloquent Ryrie called him a “miserable
body-snatcher,” without producing the body
he had snatched. The country’s business
was again suspended, of course, while Higgs
was being removed, but the Ministry, which
had been having a rather bad time, enjoyed
its half-second of glory. The only affair
which doesn’t get any satisfaction or tri-
umph or glory out of all this riot is the
poor old country. Presumably it has some
work which it wants done, else why should
it pay all these politicians to work for it?
It is aware that for every measure
there must be an honest and fairly intelli-
gent majority one way or the other, if the
majority could vote according to its con-
science, without taking any account of party
interests, and without fear of putting its
crowd out of office and letting the other
crowd into office. If a Government was
elected for the life of a Parliament, and
was fixed for that term, then Page, M.H.R.,
would find no joy in securing a victory while
some of the Government men were attending
Knox’s funeral, and Ryrie would have no
mirth or satisfaction in calling out “body-
snatcher” when the Opposition force was
reduced by one through the suspension of
Higgs, whose body was then being snatched
by the Sergeant-at-Arms. One of the worst
features of Party Government is the way it
leads members to make remarks about other
members’ bodies.

The Position in Maoriland.
Maoriland, under the influence of the
I.W.W. (Independent Workers of the
World), has an industrial trouble on hand
which involves more conversation than any
other trouble with which it has been
afflicted for nearly a generation. The I.W.W.
is a sort of international organisation of
industrial anarchists, and it sees red all the
time. Through seeing red too long its head
has apparently taken fire. It is allied with
all manner of downtrodden peoples in remote
lands, and it uses their form of language,
just as if Maoriland were bossed by a Czar
or over-run by Macedonian brigands. Pos-
sibly it lacks a sense of proportion, or of
time, or space, or something. In short, the
I.W.W. represents the irreconcilables —the
rebels in the hills. Its motto is: “To Hell
•with agreements,” and that makes negotia-
tion with it difficult. The general view
which it seems to hold is that no faith need
be kept with employers, just as Alva held
that no faith need be kept with Protestants,
and William lll.’s Parliament considered
that the worst kind of treachery to Catholics
was honorable and justifiable. The I.W.W.
isn’t a very great institution in Maoriland,
but, like the handful of desperadoes who
made the French Reign of Terror, it wields
a very considerable influence by reason of
its vigor, just as an infuriated bulldog may
do when it attacks a procession. The big
unions in Maoriland have developed a tend-
ency to withdraw from the Arbitration
Court, so that they won’t be bound by legal
awards, and may rush into battle at any
moment. That is the I.W.W. attitude.
The men who are running the big strike at
Wellington are under an agreement not to
strike, and the contract has still a few
weeks to run, but the paper bond doesn’t
seem to amount to much. The idea of en-
tering into a new paper bond under the
rules of the Arbitration Court —which prob-
ably wouldn’t amount to much more than
the old one —was being flatly repudiated by
the strikers at the time of writing,
and in consequence diplomacy is hung
up. There is much talk about the em-
ployment of troops and special constables
on one side, and of the upheaval of 10,000
or 15,000 armed toilers on the other, but so
far people haven’t been killed, or towns
destroyed, or trains wrecked. The Premier
hasn’t been slaughtered, as was President
Madero of Mexico; nor has the leader _ of
the insurrection fled for refuge to a foreign
warship,. like General Felix Diaz, of the
same worried country. As for bailing up
foreign tourists and putting their feet in the
fire to make them disclose the location of
their valuables, nothing of the sort has yet
been attempted. There has been much froth
on the row at Wellington, and comparatively
little beer underneath —at. least, compara-
tively little of what Mexicans and Mace-
donians and Mirdites and Mohammedans
wduld call beer. Still the trade of
Wellington has been held up, and the
■capital is beginning to find its vic-
tuals expensive. And the trade of other
ports seems likely to be held up. For a;
countrv which owes so much money abroad,
and which must keep on exporting wheat
and wool and meat and . cows -skins
and bulls’-innards and > ox-tail and rab-
bits and pigs’-trotters in order to pay
the interest bill, the partial stoppage
of trade is serious. Amid all the
clamor and the shouting it is rather difficult
to understand what the row is about, but it
is a nice row to contemplate from a dis-
tance.

Concerning the Defence of the Pacific.
At last Maoriland’s naval policy, or what
purports to be its policy, is announced. The
main point about it is that there have ap-
been serious disagreements between
the Admiralty, the Government of M.L.
and James Allen, Maoriland’s Minister for
Defence. In the long years of the Seddon-
Ward control, a cash subsidy to Bull-
cohen’s navy was the settled policy; but,
in spite of this payment, the Admiralty,
while playing up to Maoriland’s display of
loyalty and fireworks, did not take the small,
far-away geographical dots seriously. As a
matter of hard fact, J. Bull was not in-
clined to take Australia seriously either,
until he saw that Australia was considering
whether it should take him seriously any
longer. Both Australia and Maoriland have
all along been more or less contemptuously
“managed” by the dear Smotherland. As
history goes, it is only yesterday that a then
coming man in the public life of N. S.
Wales, W. C. Wentworth, declared that
unless England’s treatment of the country
became more sympathetic he would urge the
colonists to revolt. Now, in this year 1913,
it seems clear that J. Bull is rattled as to
the defence of his scattered empire. He
has become so rattled, in fact, that he has
deserted Australasia as far as naval defence
is concerned. In 1909 a conference was
held in London between the Admiralty (or
British Government) and the “overseas
dominions.” It was then decided that a
certain policy should be followed, which
policy had for its basis, as far as the out-
lying branches of the empire were con-
cerned, a naval unit on the East Indies
Station, one in Australia (constructed and
manned by Australia), and one in Chi-
nese waters, the last-named to also
look in on Maoriland. The pawnshop bat-
tleship New Zealand was to be the flag ves-
sel of the China squadron. Hardly
had the delegates to that conference un-
loaded their optimism before Bull went back
on his word. The East Indies unit became
a mere dream or hallucination, the battle-
ship New Zealand was “borrowed” for Eng-
lish waters, and the partly-China-partly-
Maoriland unit, it now appears, is to con-
sist of the wreckage of what was hitherto
known as the Australian squadron—a most
unlovelv lot of vessels. Hereabouts we get
back to Maoriland’s part in the business.
When the Massey Government came to
power about 12 months ago the naval de-
fence scheme of the Loanly Islands was so
ridiculous that James Allen, Minister for
Defence, was hurriedly despatched to Lon-
don to protest. En route, Allen saw Sena-
tor Pearce, at that time Australia’s Minis-
ter for Defence, and, apart from supplying
more than a hint of Allen’s own views, the
meeting was significant. The Bulletin
will await in vain for a denial from James
Allen that he (and presumably the Govern-
ment he represented) was at that time
anxious for a White Pacific scheme of local
defence, meaning thereby a linked policy em-
bracing Canada, Australia and Maoriland.
With that in his mind, Allen went to Lon-
don and apparently was thwarted. If not,
he made, or the Massey Cabinet has made,
an unholv mess of things. The central fact
is that on his return from that special expe-
dition there was a mysterious silence as to
what exactly had happened. The policy
that Allen should have had in his gripsack
was .0. It was next hesitatingly announced
that a further communication from the British
Government was expected “shortly.” From
that it was to he deduced that Winston
Churchill and Co., despite the enthusiasm
of their expert advisers, were not now
enamored of local fleets. Why, how or whaf-
for remains to be seen. Anyhow. James
Allen —who was practically pledged to local
fleets, with a general organisation in the
Pacific, and who was, moreover, borne up
with sympathy for Australia’s scheme—has
been politelv shelved. It was impossible to
go back on him in the altogether, as it wer«,
so Premier Massey now announces that un-
less more satisfactory news drifts in from
London. Maoriland may start to build its
own defence ships next year or thereabouts.
Meantime. Bullcohen will lend M.L. a
training ship, and a naval reserve is to be
formed. Beyond that he is apnarently not
prepared . to go. Maoriland wanted more ;
but in response to its request for two war-
ships of the British type in return for an
increased subsidy of £150,00 annually, he
last month said :
At present they [ships of the Bristol type] would
be superfluous in Maoriland waters, as there are no
possible enemy’s ships of equal speed to be dealt with
there.
The statesman who is so unalert to the de-
fence isolation of the White minority living
under the dark shadow of Asia would be
better employed drawing corks.
The Striker in Brass Buttons.
A London cable mentioned a week ago that
“the officers of the P. and O. service intended
to discontinue their services until their
grievances as regards inadequate pay, inade-
quate provision for old age, and excessive
overtime were adjusted.” The Company
denied the story next day, but officers who
were in Australian ports admitted the truth
of it, and finally the following message
arrived :
The notices of resignation, from the service of the
Peninsular and Oriental S.N. Co.- —unless salaries,
hours and conditions generally are improved—in-
clude those of all the officers except the commanders
of six vessels, including the Malwa.
So, apparently, the belated kick of this
flagrantly sweated toiler has transpired at
last. The P. and O. and Orient mates,
or officers, as they prefer to be called, have
to sustain much frill for little money. Fifth,
fourth, third and even second officers are
paid pittances that an Australian mechanic
would sneer at loudly. The chief officer —a
middle-aged mariner, _ with an appalling
amount of responsibility on his shoulders
from time to time —may get up to £3OO a
year. The skippers earn between £6OO and
£909 per annum. > And there are no pen-
Now consider the other side of the
picture. The mate of a liner requires to be
an adept at his calling; also, a man of
character and of a certain amount of educa-
tion. Most of them have graduated from
training-ships such as the Conway, where
the fees are at least as high as those of an
English middle-class school. They have to
possess what are known amongst commercial
travellers ana the like as the “manners of
gentlemen.’’ Although, since the wreck
of the China, they have been discouraged
from fraternising with the passengers, yet
are they expected to answer queries regard-
ing the likelihood of the ship foundering, or
the possibility of the sea-serpent coming over
the side, in a style other than that of the
frank, hearty, blasphemous sea-dog of tne
old blue, or bilge, water school. In fact, the
better imitation a mate of the mercantile
marine can give of a naval officer, the more
acceptable he is to the directors of passen-
ger-carrying vessels. His security of
tenure is of the frailest. If a liner
grounds the man in charge is doomed, how-
ever little he may have been to blame. In
the case of the China, above-mentioned, all
sorts of persons who had nothing to do
with the unpleasantness were fired into the
chill waters of unemployment. The powers
of the directors of certain companies—the
P. and O. amongst them —are almost as
autocratic in regard to their officers as those
of the English Jockey Club are in regard
to professional horsemen. And they are not
left in idle abeyance. For example, a year
or so ago the chief officer of one of the big
I*, and O. ships on the Australian line went
ashore at Colombo after dinner. Now there
was specie aboard, and the skipper was also
ashore. Immediately on the ship’s arrival in
London the Chief was incontinently sacked.
That is to say, for one trifling lapke he lost
the results of years of faithful toil, and the
possibility of grasping the only position that
is worth while in the Service. Is it any
wonder that the distressed mariners are
fighting for a fairer deal with the only
weapon that presents itself? It is a mat-
ter of financial life or death to thd majority.
Not all of them, by any manner of means,
can look to become commanders. Failing
that, their best hope of evading an old age
of insolvency consists in wedding some wan-
dering heiress. But heiresses aye scarce.
Moreover, as a result of the higher educa-
tion of women, and similar pestiferous inter-
ferences with the established order of things,
the glamor of the maritime uniform has
ceased to be the matrimonial asset to its wearer
that it was. Furthermore, however beauti-
ful the mate may appear in the costume in
which he stands over the Lascar and in-
structs the latter, in what purports to be
his (the savage’s) native tongue, on the
question of brass-polishing, or what not —
however alluring he may be in such circum-
stances. he has a tendency, when driven to
don shore-clothes, to look hopelessly un-
glorious, not to say provincial. The result
is that the heiress gives him the chuck the
moment she sees him off his own quarter-
deck, and life for him becomes sable as
night, in a cash sense, once more. The
only solution of an intolerable position is a
fair living wage all round.
A Minor Political Matter of Some
Importance.
Should Wade blunder enough to shove the
present N. S. Wales Government back into
power, the latter is pledged to accomplish
one item which doesn’t draw any limelight,
but is, nevertheless, exciting sundry stock-
owners. After backing and filling 'for about
three years, the Government is Committed
(as far as politicians are ever committed) to
the abolition of Pastures Protection Boards.
It is intended to hand over some of their
work to the shire councils, and place the
purely veterinary and stock-inspection busi-
ness under control of the Stock Department.
The boards are about the last survival of the
propeny vote; hence, of course, the handing
over of any part of their business to the
common or garden ratepayers of shires, spells
ruination —as the Tory stockowner reads it.
Seeing that at present, in most i districts,
practically the same crowd are found on
both Shire councils and boards, the fear
seems exaggerated. But, whatever it means,
the handing-over of the stock-inspection
duties to the Stock office is a sound pro-
posal. The present system is rotten in prin-
ciple and liable to be exceedingly dangerous
in practice. It is the inspector’s duty to
keen an eye on diseased stock and outbreaks
of disease; but seeing that his bosses are the
men who own the stock that may be dis-
eased, and are in the country where there
may be an outbreak of disease, a zealous
officer may have the unpleasant duty of
bumping against his own directors-; and an
officer who isn’t so zealous is liable to let
things slide if his directors’ interests seem
to be threatened by action. Hence the wis-
dom of having these men independent of
local stockowners and responsible to their
own department. There is a rather horrible
casualness about stockowners where animal
disease is concerned, and there is no reason
why the public should have to risk the con-
sequences.

The Crowded Cornstalk Madman.
The old bad mad-house days are evidently
still abroad. Maoriland has been holding a
Commission of Inquiry into alleged cruelty
to patients in an asylum at Sunnyside, Can-
terbury. Plain manslaughter in the case of
warders has been more than hinted at, and
general neglect has been openly charged.
The denials on the part of the authorities
have been emphatic, but the finding of the
Commissioner is not yet available. In New
South Wales the Government itself is accused
of disastrous neglect, not by the rela-
tions of patients, but by the Inspector-
General of the Insane. In his report for
the current year he makes the following re-
marks :
The hospitals have been in a chronic state of over-
crowding for some years n0w.... Such a state of
affairs will not conduce to the health of the patients,
and the institutions will be obliged to become
mainly places of confinement, and their value as
curative institutions be diminished. .. .There is an
excess of natients over accommodation to the extent
of 588. The matter of finding room for this excess
number has now become one of extreme gravity, and
it is of the utmost importance that steps should be
taken to effect some relief in this direction in the
immediate future. The necessity of this being done
was recognised by the Public Works Committee as
far back as 1909, when they agreed to the erection
of two new hospitals, one at Morisset and one at
Orange. The institution at Morisset was com-
menced, but works were stopped last year;; and with
regard to Orange, beyond drawing up plans and
passing the authorising Act, nothing has been
done. ...Even if steps are taken imlhediately to
proceed with new buildings, they will take two or
three years to erect.... In the meantime, it may be
fairly anticipated that the number of patients under
care will be increasing at the same rate as in pre-
vious years, or even more rapidly, and the position
will be even more serious than it is at present.
It can be taken as a long-odds bet that when
the head of the department goes that far in
addressing the Minister, the conditions of
the madhouses (they can’t be termed hos-
pitals) are in need of urgent scrutiny.
From a shipmaster who wanders up and
down that coast: —
At last a ship has hit the Proudfoot shoal in broad
daylight! Fortunately the weather was fine, assist-
ance was got promptly by wireless, and eventually
the Houtman floated off, apparently undamaged.
The removal of the Proudfoot shoal lighthouse is
an old sore to the regular navigators in those waters,
and a nightmare to the newchums. How the shoal
is dreaded may be judged by the fact that some
years ago a steamer from England, loaded with rails
for Cairns (N.Q.), faced the long journey round
Australia and the stormy seas of Cape Leeuwin rather
than take the risk of passing through the western
approaches to Torres Strait, where Proudfoot is the
worst of many dangers. The tides in the locality
are most treacherous, a mile an hour north or south
being an every-day experience, and the direction can
only be found by frequent observations for latitude.
In thick weather the only proper course to follow
when approaching this reef-strewn locality is to
anchor until the weather clears and the position is
found by observation.
The lightship was removed in 1904, and since
then numerous appeals have been made to the
Government to have it replaced, but without avail.
Queensland has been guilty of criminal negligence in
its cheeseparing policy of reducing expenditure in
this direction. I urge the Commonwealth not to
follow that bad lead.
If a steamer strikes the shoal in the North-West
season nothing can save the vessel from breaking up,
and great loss of life is then practically certain.
In such circumstances it was a crime to remove the
lightship, and not a day should be lost in restoring it.
From a man who knows Asia:—
I read that Senator Oakes said, at a “complimen-
tary banquet” to Willie Kelly, that “the time
would come when we would have to cry a halt” in
our Defence expenditure. That same day 1 read what
Major Eben Swift, of the General Staff, United
States Army, had to say concerning “The Chinese
Army: Its Development and Present Strength.” It
should be borne in mind that the arrangements are
in the hands of that same Yuan who, between 1903
and 1906, formed, armed, equipped, disciplined and
brought together for manoeuvres six army divisions,
comprising 80,000 men.
“ iVith a three-year term of enlistment,” says
Swift, “one-third of the army will be discharged
each year, and replaced by new men. These will
be taken from the million and a half of youths who
reach 20 years during the year.... When discharged
the men go to their homes, and three years are
spent in the first reserve, three years in the second
reserve, and three years in the national army....
This means 150,000 trained and drilled men added
to the reserve each year.”
By all means, with that sort of neighbor looking-
this way, let us follow Mr. Oakes’s hint and “cry-
a halt,” more especially as Cook’s Government has-,
undone much of the- little that Fisher’s crowd did!
to induce settlers in the North —notably by cutting;
the freezing works at Darwin off the estimates..
While the doddering Liberal party is tinkering withi
National Defence, against which it has always set
its face; while Millen is busy pinning useless pea-
cock feathers on the tails of Australian jackdaws
and while Cook is shouting wildly about importing;
more experts to waste precious time, “China has:
made plans for military schools, and machine-shops
and cartridge factories in each province.” Eighteen
of them.
And Swift’s summing-up is pregnant: “Whatever
shortcomings we may find in this Chinese army, we
cannot forget it is ideal in many respects. The
Chinese soldier has few needs, is obedient, and is a
fatalist by nature. His daily life would be a trial
and a hardship to almost any other soldier. He
subsists on little, travels long distances, and seems
to be immune to those common diseases which have
destroyed many armies.”
In conclusion, let me throw at Australia the
words of an almost-forgotten Chinese classic, Tichc
Liang, quoted to the Chinese Throne in 1906::
“Though all under Heaven is at peace, if the art-,
of war be forgotten there is peril.” The Chinese-
Throne did not take the straight tip offered in that
case, and even Australian politicians probably know
what happened to the Chinese Throne.

sound from Britain, and Cobdens soul goes
marching on. The report of an inquiry by
the Board of Trade into the earnings and
hours of labor of the people who never shall
be slaves shows that, in a typical week of
full employment in the staple industries,
6,334,627 workers earned front 9s. 3d. to
275. 3d. each. For the men alone the pay
ran or limped from 9s. 3d. to 345. 4d. But
345. 3d. is affluence. Taking a- typical week,
the Board of Trade found that 51 per cent,
of the men in the hair. trade were earning
less than 20s. a week; in the jute trade, 49
per cent.; in the linen trade. 44 per cent.; in
fustian and cordcutting, 23. per cent.; in
other textiles, 22 per cent.; in the hemp
trade, 22 per cent.; in the silk trade, 19 per
cent.; in the carpet trade, 19 per cent.; in
the cotton trade, 16 per cent.; in bleaching,
printing, etc., 16 per cent.; in woollens and
worsteds, 15 per cent.; and in the flock and
shoddy trade. 15 per cent. And here is evi-
dence that Brother Cobden was the founder

A recent conversazione tendered to Willy
Kelly, the Little Minister of the Federal
Government, was enlivened more or less by
a professional comedian. Unfortunately for
the doting lady-electors of Wentworth, no-
body. had given the jester the tip to temper
the joke to the feathered voters, so he got
off one or two good things—but things of
the sort that no lady may laugh at when
another lady is present. Though somewhat
shaken, the feathered voters agreed, never-
theless, that Willy was “a dear” which
seems to be a correct diagnosis of Willy’s
trouble.
“Pink Tape”: There are ructions and
ballyhooley in the N. S. Wales Public Ser-
vice. as a result of the P.S. Board’s crusade
against undue overtime and similar fees.
Some heads of departments have been rak-
ing in currency at a rare rate for doing odd
official jobs after 4.30 p.m., and their ex-
planations on the carpet have not tended to
mollify the Board, nor cause it to swallow
its insinuation that all has not been square
and above deck. Of course, nobody has
carpeted the Board and asked it why it
paved the way to the objectionable business
bv its cheap-wages policy. Nobody’s busi-
ness is sadly neglected these days.
A really pleasant “drunk” (English
variety) has been before Adelaide police
court. “Not aggressive,” said the constable
when cross-examined ; “just drunk in a quiet,
gentlemanly way.” The P.M. talked of a
small fine, but defendant protested. He was
a respectable man, he claimed, and was not
drunk. He always took three bottles of
whisky a day, and could stand it. He was
a military captain, and was “going Home”
in March. Till then he could take care of
himself. And the Court was so impressed
that it let him off —“but don’t do it again.”
"I shall do it as often as I please.” said the
Captain haughtily, and left. The imper-
tinence of these blawsted colonials !
Listen to John Madden, orating to the
glory of Bent at the unveiling of the deplor-
able statue: —
Although he began in the very humblest way
he had managed by his acumen, his energy and
knowledge of affairs, Which so often had pulled him
through, to become a wealthy man. Yet he fell on
the Flodden Field of ’92 and ’93, with many others.
It was said that there was the end of him. But he
was not that kind of man; he was undaunted and
wholly undismayed. He had begun by taking off
his coat, and he took it off again. He laboriously
and earnestly beat himself up enough wealth to
enable him to come again to the service of his
country, and to confute those who had said that
there was an end of him.
The allegation that Bent —the blind, roaring
bull of the land boom —lost all his money
and earned another fortune within eight
years by bogging in with his coat off, is
hardly less untrue than other allegations in
defence of that ugly public character. Bent
went into temporary retirement with avail-
able means to “pull round” when land values
should return to normal, and other falsely-
depreciated investments should buck up
again. The fat man is represented as toiling
artlessly in his shirt-sleeves to accumulate
fresh capital. How much capital can a fat
old profligate earn by honest toil in eight
years? Out of politics as in politics, Bent
throve by his impudence, and nobody should
know that better than Madden. The
Brighton statue, anyway, was erected to the
politician—Thomas Bent, Premier of Vic-
toria and ex-Speaker. In both of those capa-
cities Bent was a disgrace. In public he
was a log-roller, jobber and “tactician” of
the deepest dye. In private his conduct was
scandalous. His whole life, in fact, was a
shocking example. Yet when an effigy of
this awful man was mounted on a pedestal,
the clean-living Madden spouted about him
as an object for emulation. No doubt it was
his business as Lieut.-Gov. to do so. A vice-
regal personage occupies a position
where he is required to tell the untruth
about most things—especially such things
as the late Tom Bent.
A month ago Tiie Bulletin received
numerous newspaper cuttings of an adver-
tisement, purporting to be from the S. M.
Herald, for a typiste who was wanted to
“travel India and the East.” Native ser-
vant, complete outfit and other attractions
were offered. Application by letter, with
photo and various personal details (such as
height, age, religion and personal appear-
ance) were to be sent to an address in
India. The Bulletin, which suggested
that inquiries should be made regarding the
advertiser, now learns that the advertise-
ment was not printed in the S. M. Herald,
and sincerely regrets that it connected that
naper with the advertisement. From the
days of the original John Fairfax, the
Herald has had strict rules regarding the
character of its advertisements, and re-
serves and exercises the right to censor them
rigidly. The Bulletin feels that it owes
an apology to a paper which steers clear of
such doubtful announcements. Wherefore it
withdraws that part of the story and offers
this apology accordingly.
