Verdi’s greatest opera, Aida, showed the
Quinlan Co. at its best. Amongst the prin-
cipals the only exception to this high stan-
dard was Jeanne Brola, as the Ethiopian
damsel. She was too shrill in her efforts to
beat the orchestra, and Act 3 supplied her
only pleasing contributions. At this crisis
she walks out with her young man, Rad-
ames, on the shores of the Nile. The or-
chestra and the male chorus (the latter
with early Sphinx bonnets on their heads)
were magnificent. The curtain glides up
on the first scene immediately after an ex-
quisite whisper from the fiddles has deep-
ened into a warning growl from the whole
team. Voghera was in command of this fine
performance. Verdi repeats in a similar
strain the tones of a cathedral organ—a
wonderful piece of orchestration —to bring,
down the curtain in the final scene. Aida*
and Radames are buried alive, while the
Sphinx-bonnets of the male chorus and the
feather fans of the female chorus waggle
overhead. They die hard. Radames
(Maurice d’Oisley), singing to the heavenly
music just described, lives about one second
longer than Aida. This favoritism to a
tenor is part of the inexorable tradition of
grand opera. Radames, though occasionally
overwhelmed by Voghera’s department, was
mostly satisfactory. Even his surfing cos-
tume (Act 1) was in good taste. The King
of Ethiopia (W. J. Samuell) was a pale
buff understudy of kings as we see them at
La Perouse camp. The rest of the La
Perouse contingent was inky black. Aida
was a shade more sunburnt than her father.
Edna Thornton, the Amneris or Miss Phar-
aoh of the cast, gave the finest vocal and
dramatic performance of the evening. The
King of Egypt (Charles Magrath) occasion-
ally lapsed into intelligible English. But
he was pleasingly inarticulate in a massive
voice that seemed to come from the bottom
of an Egyptian sarcophagus. The High
Priest of Isis (Anderson) looked like the
first Plymouth Rock or one of the early
Methodist brethren. His long, lank, black
mop was combed all over his head after a
pudding-basin clip. His voice is so impres-
sive that it sits on his chest like a clap of
thunder, and rarely gives way to anything
so frivolous as mere words. The stage trum-
peters were the most uncertain quantity in
this evening-out with Aida and her young
man.
The first production in Sydney of Puc-
cini’s “Manon Lescaut” was so reminiscent
of Puccini that one met its music half-way
like an old friend. But the story is poor,
and not worth the fine orchestration. Manon
(Jeanne Brola) is the Flying French-
woman. She has the instincts of a mono-
plane. She elopes with lover des Grieux
(tenor d’Oisley). leaves him for rich-man
Geronte, and flies from him back to des
Grieux. She is still flying in Act III —this
time, per convict-ship to America, whither
she incites the distracted des Grieux (who
deserves a better girl) to go too. In Act
IV the flight has tamed down to a limp;
yet Manon is still egging on her tenor to
fly as fast as his stout waist and starvation
will permit. They then die to slow music.
Lack of space forbids any attempt to write
their epitaph beyond the one line that the
stress of Manon’s songs is too great for
Jeanne Brola. “Carmen,” with Edna Thorn-
ton as a doubtful blessing in the name part,
was remarkable for the success of Spencer
Thomas as the love-tossed corporal. Don
Jose. After Thomas, came Graham Marr’s
dashing Escamillo. The dandy bull-fighter
was a strapping specimen of a Seville mata-
dor. He sang the famous Toreador song in
the approved fashion, with one eye on the
conductor and the other on Carmen. Edna
Thornton can stand in a corner (if she has
an hour to spare) for beefing or butchering
this part. Gladys Ancrum (Mercedes) may
take another corner, and Alice Prowse
(Micaela) a third. The chorus, which gave
its worst performance this season in the
first act. may crowd itself into the fourth.
“The Flying Dutchman” may be divided
into the Dutchman (Parker), the Norwegian
skipper Daland (Magrath), Erik a hunts-
man (d’Oisley), the nice girl Senta (who
falls in love with the devil-doomed Dutch-
man —Perceval Allen), the helmsman, and
the spinning chorus (2nd Act). After this
the audience falls back on the orchestra un-
der the remarkable Eckhold. Wagner puts
the hiss of a hurricane and the roar of an
angry sea into two-thirds of the orchestra-
tion : the story of the chivied Hollander’s
life and final dive into the trough of a heav-
ing cardboard breaker—his ship lit up with
red lights to show him the way to the bot-
tom —is told in every bar of the music. Per-
ceval Allen and Parker shared the vocal
honors of a good performance.

# * * *
The accomplished illusionist who sells
shares in a mere hole in the ground and
makes his patient believe he is a shareholder
in a gold mine is looked upon coldly, while
the poor Fiji nephew has to escape by the
back door of the pub. Yet Jansen, at Syd-
ney Royal, is allowed to deal peacefully in
deception and take in hundreds. It seems
unjust; also, it seems to suggest that, after
all, the gold-brick merchant supplies one of
the permanent needs of humanity.

“Within the Law” is still within the Cri.,
and likely to remain so while boom business
continues. The advertisements, which speak
very well of the show, mention that the
present is its seventh week.

“Barry of Ballymoore,” Allen Doone’s
porous plaster at the Palace (Sydney), is
billed for another week. On. the 15th
“Molly Bawn” will be given a brief recall,
and the season will close with the presenta-
tion of a play new to Australia, “The
Rebels.”
Marcus Clarke’s “For the Term of His
Natural Life” is the cause of the latest
riotous conduct at the Adelphi. This bel-
lowdramatic version follows the book for
the most part, but loses sight of it towards
the end end wanders round to find a happy
ending for everyone but Captain Frere, the
Commandant. This, of course, serves two
purposes: the hero and the heroine hadn’t
to be drowned, and consequently there was
no need for a shipwreck scene ; and it was
the means whereby the world was rid of a
villain, which is the aim of every bellow-
drama. Taken all round, there is no reason
why this version shouldn’t further materi-
ally expand the Adelphi treasury. Anyway,
to start with, there, was an overcrowded
house on Saturday night. As for the char-
acters, there is little fault to find with them.
George Cross gives a very even representa-
tion of good Mr. Rufus Dawes, and Miss
Vera Remee is equally successful as, Sylvia.
T. W. Lloyd (Capt. Frere), Frank Crossley
(Rev. Meekin) and Miss Muriel Dale (Sarah
Purfoy) all call for a good word. Of the
others C. Taylor and Miss Fanny Erris, as
Gabbett and “the Crow.” the convicts, stand
out most prominently.

“The Black Diamond” was the Crystal
Palace’s best attempt this week ; also it' was
the best attempt it has made for several
weeks. The chief attraction of the film a
very well-got-up Pathecolor. was the leopard
hunt in Central Africa —ending disastrously
of course, for the leopard. The management
was at the time of writing holding out pro-
mises to have the Melbourne Cup pic-
tures showing by Wednesday night. What
punters are looking for is a show that will
give them these things on Monday night.
The American people who made their first
Melbourne rise with “The Grafters” at the
Bijou, are now grafting some more at the
King’s, in a revel described as “A Pay
at the Races.” The whole performance
is better dressed and generallv less
inexpensive than “The Grafters”' was.
Otherwise it is much the same collection of
vaudeville “turns”—dialogues and ragtime
songs by the principals and fleeting inva-
sions by the chorus —with a thread of plot
as thin, as a spider’s web. Sometimes even
the spider seems to have struck work.
The most notable “turn” last Saturday was
a reckless song by a young man whose
confederates came bustling from the audi-
torium under circumstances likely to pro-
duce a panic. Yankee notions in entertain-
ment are usually harmless, but this might
have resulted in a rush for the doors and a
lot of sudden death. No other incident was
exciting. Nevertheless, an audience that
found entertainment in moss-grown jests,
fun in the flowing patter, and humor in
anything, was delighted with “A Day at the
Races,” which can be described as simple
piffle for crude people, strengthened here and
there by droll absurdities that move even a
mourner to smile aloud.

SARTO.
INJUNCTIONS AGAINST PASSING OFF.
LATEST CASE.—SCHWEPPES v. O’CONNOR.
In the Supreme Court, Queensland, before his Honor Mr. Justice Real and a jury of four.
(See Brisbane Couriers, August £7 to September 13. in which an Injunction was granted.)
During this case, which lasted 12 days, the following evidence was given, showing the
health-giving properties of Sarto and the amount of Sarsaparilla contained therein.
“George Alexander B.vru, member of the firm of Dixon and Byrn, assayers and
analysts, of Sydney, deposed that he had made analyses of a bottle of ‘Sarto’ and a
bottle of ‘Sarsa.’ He found that ’Sarto’ contained 11.47 per cent, of solids, .108 of
similicia, and the balance consisted of water and a small quantity of alcohol. ‘Sarsa’
contained 11.15 of solids and .006 per cent, of similicia, and the balance consisted of
water and a small quantity of alcohol. There was, therefore, eighteen times as
much similicia in ‘Sarto’ as there was in ‘Sarsa.’ Similicia was the active
principle of sarsaparilla; it was a medicinal tonic. There were other properties
of sarsaparilla of medicinal value, but the active principle was similicia.”
Mr. Feez: “How does ‘Sarto’ compare with ‘Sarsa’ according to your analysis?”
—“There is a finer quality of sarsaparilla in ‘Sarto’ than there is in ‘Sarsa.’ ” “Is
it correct to say that some sarsaparilla root may have no similicia at all?”—“Yes.”
“What does the presence of .108 per cent, of similicia indicate to you?”— “That
only the best sarsaparilla root has been use<J in the preparation.” “Is that a
large or a small quantity for a drink of that kind?” —“A very fair amount.”

At the Royal the Comte de Can-
dale continues to win his fair young
bride from the chevalier who had taken her
indiscretion for granted. But the last night
of “A Marriage of Convenience” is to-mor-
row (Friday), and on Saturday the prin-
cipals in that transaction will figure in a
modern divorce-court scene. “A Butterfly on
the Wheel” is to be staged, with Madge
Titheradge as the butterfly and Lewis
Waller as chief spokesman of the Wheel.
The money that was last week turned
from the doors of the Auditorium on John
McCormack nights will be duly received this
week if it gets there before all the seats are
sold. McCormack is on the crest of popu-
larity’s foamy wave, and Caruso would be a
comparatively small potato if he happened
in Melbourne just now. Resisting the temp-
tation to restrict himself to Irish ballads, he
put “Salve Dimora,” “Down in the Forest”
and “The Lord is My Light” into the pro-
gramme last Saturday, but his finest vocal
achievements count for less than his “Molly
Brannigan,” which, to be sure, is a work of
McCormackian art. When he implores
Kathleen Mavourneen to wake up, the whole
house can conscientiously lay its hand on
the place where its heart ought to be, and
vow that it never before heard so much
meaning put into that long-suffering song. To-
night (Thursday) the seventh concert of
the extended series will come to pass. On
Saturday the McCormack warbles again in
the Auditorium, and on Monday he will take
Miss Brannigan and Kathleen to Geelong.
* * * *
At the Bijou the question as to What
Tomkins Did is still under discussion.
Nordica commenced a quartette of fare-
wells at Melbourne Town Hall on Saturday.
She opened placidly enough in a bit of
Puccini, but became restless in Bemberg’s
“Chant de Bacchante,” and created such a
commotion in her few moments with Wag-
ner that her head-dress became disarranged.
Brunnhilde’s battle shriek was almost as
much a gymnastic display as a vocal exer-
cise. Still, Nordica has some good high
notes left, and in her last struggle with
Wagner—from “The Twilight of the Gods”
—she got through like a veteran who knew
every bit of the road. As usual, tenor
Dufault lavished his caressing attention on
some pleasant numbers; Franklin Holding
made his fiddle perform prodigies in two or
three Mendelssohn pieces; and pianist
Romayne Simmons presided over the concert
grand and smiled suitably at intervals.
* * * *
The Fantons, Reynolds and Donegan and
Miss Willette Whitaker are the most favored
for the Popularity Stakes at Melbourne
Opera House this week. Miss Bonnie Leon-
ard, of the Countless Smiles, still continues
the even tenor of her decollete , and Fi'ank
Fox is absorbed in warbling a lot of stuff
that sounds like the philosophy of an aban-
doned curate. Williams and Rankin and
the posy of Primroses are preparing to
trek; and Gene- Greene and Du Calion are
due next Saturday, Meantime Lillie Lang-
try is announcing herself in the old strident
soprano and several changes of raiment.

At Melbourne Olympia last week the
kinetophone got busy on an excerpt from
“Faust,” attributed by the programme to
W. Shakspeare. but probably the same com-
edy that the enterprising young Gounod set
to music. Edison’s clever but uncertain
instrument gave a very fair rendering of
the classic fragment, and was also moder-
ately successful in an alleged comedietta
and a vocal item introducing the Edison
quartette. The sensation of the week was
“King of the Air,” affording some gossip on
an aerial tragedy; while a matter of in-
dustrial interest was a film showing how the
far-famed delftware is potted and painted.

A biographic version of “A Message from
Mars” is now delivering a silent sermon on
Otherdom to the respectable patrons of Mel-
bourne Majestic. Charles IJawtrey appears
in his old-time part of Horace Parker, and
the film includes some alleged snapshots of
Mars which hardly help the story, but ap-
parently are chucked in to correct some
popular rumors on the subject of the planet
and its untidy inhabitants.

There was hardly a quorum present at
the Melbourne Athenaeum on Saturday when
Miss Phyllis Hopwood-Foldi commenced a
short season of light and inoffensive enter-
tainment. The plump lady was responsible
for the usual programme of songs and chest-
nuts, and showed that she is cleverer in
the cultivation of the latter vegetable than
in making a piano her accomplice. A lack
of personality or something is Miss Phyllis’s
shortcoming, especially noticeable in items
wherein she challenges the art of Margaret
Cooper, and brings all the dire penalties of
comparison on her fair young head. George
Cowley assisted with remarks in song; W.
T. Barker’s harp was heard occasionally:
and Miss Madeline Hopwood-Foldi tried her
voice on “Softly Awakes my Heart,” from
“Samson and Delilah,” and made a very
fair success of it.

(the Comte de

The Anderson “Grafters” Co. gave a trial
trip to a “musical revel” at Adelaide Royal
before showing it during Cup week in Mel-
bourne. “A Day at the Races” (parentage
unacknowledged) is another of those
American concoctions that always seem
borrowed from the “variety” stage. It de-
pends largely on elaborate conventions. For
instance, a restaurant makes a rule that
anybody who sits at a certain table must
order champagne every time the bell is rung;
then one waits confidently to see innocent
lemonade-swiggers sit at that precise table,
and —-even after they have learnt the rule —
hit the bell countless times by inadvertence,
or even tread on it. There is a flavor of
Horse in the piece, and a racecourse crowd
“calls” a race all round the track in the
“Arcadians” manner. Dave Nowlin and
Gertrude St. Clair do some good singing, and
that fine low-comedian, Eugenie Le Blanc,
has a Baby Bonus song which succeeds in
being funny without getting vulgar. Among
other localised work is the song showing
(with copious illustration) how the girls of
various cities kiss. The verse devoted to
Brisbane is shockingly libellous. Brisbane
must have annoyed someone seriously.
A new “Dandies” troop is doing good
work at the Exhibition gardens in Ade-
laide. The city had a special regard for the
Dampier-Morgan combination of last season,
but is rapidly making friends with the
strong new lot Branscombe has sent along.
The Tivoli is good on the athletic side ; the
graceful trapeze work of Julia Gonzales has
been added to the easy jumping of Frank
Parish, and the dancing of the Golden Troop
of Russians is mostly a series of athletic
feats. Merian’s dogs continue to walk in
boots and enact a scandal in High Life. The
King’s puts up a Comedy with a London
reputation, “A Sister to Assist ’er,” and
throw's in a Crimson Ramblers crowd with
the Bletsoes at its head. At the Royal,
G.R.Q. Wallingford has happened at last,
with the usual immediate success. Hein-
icke’s orchestra has given its final Town Hall
concert for this year ; and a big Government
House fete comes into this column by reason
of a Delmar Hall concert, and a double per-
formance of “How He Lied to Her Hus-
band,” wherein Bernard Shaw burlesques
his own “Candida,” and makes the admiring
husband assault a poet for not passionately
regarding the wife.
Adelaide City Council, which mostly fixes
up these things in a friendly way, has de-
cided that Alderman Simpson can have a
clear run for the mayoralty on Bonython’s
coming retirement. He will be the second
mayor taken out of the batch of young sons
of well-known families who butted into the
Council about 10 years ago. Of the others,
Lav. Bonython has been mayor, Alfred
Roberts has dropped out, Frank Moulden’s
turn has yet to come, and John Baker re-
tired (hurt) four vears ago. after his ap-
parently clear path to the Chair had been
suddenly and successfully disputed by the
revived veteran Lewis Cohen.
AT POVERTY POINT.
E. J. Carroll, Queensland’s most enterprising
theatrical manager, is due back to Australia next
week after an eight months’ tour of America and
Europe. In that time he has booked some important
engagements, the most notable of which will probably
be an English co. to produce “The Rosary” and a
few other big bites for popular taste. The Queens-
lander is spreading his interests widely over Aus-
tralia, and will immediately get work on his return
upon the big “White City” venture at Rushcutter’s
Bay, Sydney, in which he shares responsibility with
C. Spencer, Ben Fuller and A. B. Davies. The
building of the “city” is approaching completion.
Carroll is also in J. and N. Tait’s undertaking to
introduce Harry Lauder to the Commonwealth.
“J.F.H.” (from London) : If I were asked for a
tip for Australian playwrights, I should say “Keep
a keen eye on the newspapers, jot down the sensa-
tions as they occur—the Sunday morning raid on the
Melbourne cafes and the pair of damsels who put
poison in the tea are a couple of examples—weave
them into a dramatic story, invent some comic relief,
distribute the mixture over three acts, and—then
find your manager.” That is how the Drury Lane
“autumn drama” is put together. It is always a big
success, and there is no reason why a local produc-
tion should not be similarly successful in Australia.
Says the London Daily Telegraph in opening its
two-column notice of “Sealed Orders”: “The func-
tion of the Drurv Lane autumnal drama is to crowd
into a few amazing hours a vastly magnified vision
of all that has been thrilling you in the cold print
of the newspaper.” There you have it. Now go and
do likewise!
“0.K.” : In England the touring “grand”-opera com-
panies are still alternating the old ballad operas with
“Tannhauser” and “Romeo and Juliet” and sich.
And still the wonder grows that no operatic manager
thinks of amending the libretto and rationalising
the song-words of “Maritana” and “The Bohemian
Girl.” They exist on their popular airs, in spite of
the absurdities which might be easily amended. The
same with “II Trovatore.” There seems to be a
deep-rooted belief that the old familiar, words have
become endeared to the public for evermore, whereas
the public, which is always changing, would soon get
used to newer and better words. The tunes by any
other name would sound as sweet. Why should the
Bohemian Gyurl always dream that she dwelt in
marble halls, when she might fancy herself on the
rolling deep? Every ancient melody will adapt
itself to better words than it was born to. AVhy, the
other week, at the Crystal Palace, Tetrazzini was
singing a version of “Home, Sweet Home,” from
which the old thatched cottage had been expunged.
Matheson Lang’s proposed tour of the Common-
wealth with a company of his own is now defi-
nitely postponed. He has, instead of coming this
way again, entered into partnership at the Strand,
London, with Louis Meyer, the lucky Hebrew who
made a big fortune out of “The Glad Eye.” Lang
will appear in leading parts, produce the plays, and
generally control the stage.
“0.K.”: Bill Anderson, who for some years has
alternated his output of awful English bellowdrama
with productions of milder Australian stuff, is now
running a musical absurdity at his Melbourne
theatre, and promises another Amurkan extrava-
ganza, “The Land of Nod,” for Christmas-time.
Seemingly he is arriving at the conclusion that
crude, long-winded bellowdrama, written for the
utterly uneducated classes of Britain, cannot any
longer compete against potted film-dramas, rapid
in action and change of scene, finely acted in many
cases, and devoid of “comic relief.” The antique
Andersonian bellowdrama, which was out : Andersoned
by Marlow, has done its dash. It is has-fceened. The
potted picture-dramas contain all that was most
interesting to the multitude in those old, four-act
blood-love-and-thunder stories, whilst dispensing
with the conventional dialogue and stereotyped funny
business. First-class artists tell the story in dumb
show, and the photographer and film-faker supply
sensational effects which couldn’t possibly be intro-
duced into an articulate drama. Lately I saw a
JOHN McCORMACK
in the throes of
pathos.
picture play at Hoyt’s, in Melbourne —-one of the
non-Combine theatres. Made in Italy, where the best
film-mumming is done nowadays, its Australian
title was “A Leap of Despair.” And what was the
title act? The heroine (or a circus lady who looked
like the heroine) sat on a horse that stood quietly
on a narrow, swinging platform, 50 feet or more
above the arena, whilst fireworks fizzed around it.
Among the audience below a giddy lady was making
love to the husband of the heroine, who expressed
her despairing jealousy by turning the horse’s head
and making it step off the platform. Horse and
woman appeared to fall into the arena, and an in-
stantaneous change of picture showed them lying
on the ground, the horse motionless, the lady greatly
disarranged and damaged, but not dead. How does
the simple gymnasticism of “The Span of Life” or
“The Ladder of Life” compare with such a title act
as thatt The possibilities of actual stage-sensation
scenes have been exhausted; but to the faked pic-
ture drama anything is possible, including acting
which no Bland Holt or Anderson could afford to
engage. The picture drama spares its patrons the
foolishness of front scenes and the dreariness of
intervals between the acts. Best of all, it holds
its silly tongue.

“J.P.”: Lewis Waller made a brief bid for turf
honors a score of years ago, just after he had
played “Monsieur Beaucaire” at Liverpool. On
the advice of a leading Goodwood trainer he bought
a chestnut colt by Royal Kendal, christened him
Beaucaire, and later got some good trials out of
him. But in public the prad ran like a funeral
crock. Waller tells that he last saw the horse in
the shafts of a hansom in which he rode to Water-
loo —and missed his train. It was his AVaterloo
sure enough.
“C. Ockney”: Commenting on the slump in accept-
able plays in England—a slump which appears to be
getting chronic—a London daily of eminence bluntly
asserted a few weeks ago that it was due to the
fact that managers are quite unintelligent and in-
capable of spotting the right stuff. This half-truth
had, naturally, the effect of “drawing” the head of
the profesh—Beerbohm Tree. Speaking at a send-off
luncheon to Cyril Maude on his departure for
America, Tree, in his defence, rather succeeded in
giving away the show. After admitting that he had
refused “Kismet” (an ideal play for him and his
theatre, one would have, thought) and “Bunty Pulls
the Strings,” and had turned “the cold shoulder of
stupidity” to a suggestion by Bancroft that he should
revive “Diplomacy,” which is at present the success
of London, he remarked: “Perhaps I am betraying
a secret when I tell you that ‘Peter Pan’ was brought
to me. But I thought that the leading part was
unsuitable.” Oh, that leading—which should be
called mis-leading—part! Many people, I don’ t
doubt, would just as soon see Tree play “Peter Pan”
as “Mark Antony,” or “Ulysses” ; but the point is
that many a good piece is turned do-wn —Tree tacitly
admits it —because the actor-manager doesn’t like the
leading part. It matters not how excellent the play
may be if the leading role is “unsuitable.” Back
goes the MS. On the other band, if the part is all
right, though the whole may be rotten, up goes the
play, and, mirabile dictu, down go the receipts !
“Nutty”: It is true, as a Poverty Pointer said
lately, that robbery and murder incidtnts are cen-
sored from picture plays in Germany ; but it is also
true that the Gimlet Brigade of the Fatherland gets
full compensation in being permitted to enjoy
naughtier pictures than would be tolerated in any
English-speaking country. Some German Cinematograph
stories are just about the limit in salaciousness, yet
the censor sees nothing “dangerous” in them. Morals
are a matter of geography. What is chaste in Mar-
taban is crime in Pimlico, as Jonah (or it may have
been Barnabas) said.
“Way Verley Penn”: Tokio Town’s tour of the
Commonwealth (more or less) has just concluded at
Rockhampton (Q.), and the ghost walked gamelv
enough throughout the 6J months. The show has
to date appeared in only tw'o capitals, Adelaide and
Melbourne, but is to tackle Sydney under new man-
agement presently. In its 64 months of life no
fewer than five touring managers have been in front
of it, shouting volubly. The first was the late Harry
P. Lyons, who was also ahead of the first Japanese
village show ever seen in Australia.
“Screen” : The Saturday-night picture show attend-
ance in Melbourne is about 65,000. There are 57
regular, covered-in shows every night, besides
numerous occasionals and open-airs. Over 1000
people are employed directly, and an incalculable
number indirectly. In the matter of capital outlay,
£70,000 has been expended in hard cash on three
city theatres alone. The total investment would
probably be over £350,000. And still they come.
Two new theatres are to be built in Bourke-street;
one of them will start in six weeks, and will cost
£20,000. And a third is likely in Swanston-street.
The ordinary theatres and variety shows do not
hold more than 14,000. Where did the 50,000 go
before the pictures started ?
“Y.Z.”: Splendid films were taken of the Johan-
nesburg riots, but, so far, none of any consequence
have been shown anywhere. These films settled the
disputed point as to the firing of the first shot, and
were “very damning to the Government.” The fact
is the films have been “censored”; not in the
usual way, but in a much more amiable manner.
The authorities, having decided that it would be
“injudicious” to show the pictures, have bought
them in. The living picture threatens to be a living
witness who will make it difficult for Property to
keep up many of its favorite fictions. It is a dan-
gerous substitute for the film so sedulously drawn
over the public eye in the past.
Galsworthy’s latest play, “The Fugitive,” promises
to be his best-paying production thusfar. It features
a pretty woman who breaks away from a very decent
sort of husband because he has no physical attrac-
tion for her. “My marriage,” she says, “has become
the reconciliation of two animals, one of them unwill-
ing,” and the reconciliation grows intolerable. She
cannot find her affinity. She would live contentedly
enough with an idealistic journalist, but for the fact
that her husband would ruin him by making him co-
re. in a divorce suit. She decides she must earn her
own living, faces the necessity for “going gay” in
order to do it, and prefers to die. So she poisons
herself in the restaurant where strange men have
been accosting her. Apparently she never brings her
superior mind down to the idea of earning her living
as a lady-help or a missionary. She is the sort of
heroine whom Ibsen would have endeavored to
glorify. But Galsworthy neither defends nor con-
demns her. He presents the interesting lady, and
the men who have to do with her, as “simple facts” .
in social life.
“Norbell” : The Royal Dramatic Co. had exploded,
and Ginger Bowman and Dicky Harden were hope-
lessly stranded in Gastlemaine. They couldn’t even
finance a beer, and an unromantic but athletic porter
had unfeelingly booted them off the railway pre-
mises when they had tried to “jump the rattler.”
After much anxious debate, they decided there was
nothing else for it, so with melodramatic maledic-
tions in their mouths and nothing in their pockets,
they set out to walk to Melbourne, 75 miles away.
When they got to Kyneton they were savage and
desperate, and aching for a drink. Ginger sug-
gested the R.C. nresbytery as the likeliest spot for
unconditional charity, and they drew lots as to who
should tell the tale. Ginger lost. The evening
shadows were falling as Ginger accosted the black
figure that was pacing to and fro in the presbytery
garden. “I crave your pardon, Father, but we are
too indigent actors,” he began, in his best leading-
juvenile manner, and so on to the carefully-prepared
peroration. “Come inside,” said the genial Father
Doyle, “an’ let’s have a look at'"'the pair of ye.”
They entered the well-lit study. “Why, ye’ve both
been drinkin’, that’s what’s wrong With ye,” said
his Reverence. “Here, take a nip each,” and he
passed the decanter. Ginger and Dicky made a
poor pretence of polite indifference, but measured
out a pair of generous snifters. After some questioning,
Father Doyle gave them a shilling each, and directed
them to the Harp of Erin. “I’ll ring Fogarty up,”
said he, “and tell him ye’re to have tea, bed and
breakfast. Be off now, and come and see me in the
morning.” With joyous hearts Ginger and Dicky
departed. They were back again bright and early
next day, and the good-natured priest gave them a
lecture, a shilling each, and two second-class tickets
to Melbourne. They left the presbytery full of
gratitude and good resolutions, and two hours later
had sold the tickets to two swagmen for four shil-
lings, which were shamelessly squandered. During
this process Ginger acquired a black eye. Next
evening they were at AVoodend. Again they drew
lots, and again Ginger lost. They waited till it was
dark enough to hide Ginger’s damaged eye, and then
approached the blaek-eassocked figure "which was
pacing up and down the presbytery verandah. Ginger
began: “I crave your pardon, Father, but we are
two indigent actors” “What!” roared a sten-
torian voice. “We are two indigent actors,” Ginger
repeated mildly. “And I’m an indignant priest!”
roared his Reverence. “To the Devil with the pair
of ye!” Rotten luck! It was Father Doyle again.

SOCIETY
One of Victoria’s new immigrants—a
painter and decorator who admitted that his
earnings averaged £3 10s. per week —was
sued recently by his wife for maintenance
for herself and their two children. He
coolly offered 15s. a week. The Court said
it wasn’t enough, and ordered him to pay
20s. for his wife and ss. each for his chil-
dren, and to find a surety of £2O that he
would obey the order. Whereupon : “And if
I don’t pay, what then?” The J.P.:
“Gaol.” “How long?” “Six months.” “I’ll
do the six months.” Just so! And under
the present fool system of penal administra-
tion the wife and children may starve until
the wife petitions for his release, and then
takes the few shillings he chooses to dole out
to her. Yet a painter and decorator who is
worth 70s. weekly to a private employer
should be worth quite as much to the State.
On that basis he could be made to pay for
his maintenance in gaol, plus about £3 a
week, which might be handed to his wife.
The chances are that this precious immigrant
would speedily weary of that game.

way they have travelled lately, litigation will
soon be a thing of the days gone by, and bar-
risters will be able to beat their wigs into
hassocks. Possibly the millennium is at
hand ; perhaps the people are moving a tacit
vote of no-confidence in Judges and Gentle-
men One; maybe empty purses are the
vogue ; or, most likely,
the ordinary bloke is
just sick and tired
of the whole, long-
winded, cumbersome
and expensive busi-
ness. Anyhow, where-
as the number of
Supreme Court causes
entered for trial in
Victoria in IS9O
reached 535, and the
amounts at issue
£687,000, the corre-
sponding figures for
1912 were respectively
108 and £BO.OOO. It
was just the same
with all other courts,
barring the brilliant
exception supplied by
the Divorce branch;
in that jurisdiction
the average number of
decrees before 1890
was 12 per annum, as
against 120 since. I
don’t know whether
there are any morals
hidden in this last bit
of information. The
chances are that there
were more immorals
hidden when the di-
vorces numbered only
12, and the way
wasn’t often open to
a regular second
wedding.
“Melb ” : There is a strong move, eagerly
helped by the Age, to shift all Caulfield rac-
ing to Flemington. Why not? Fiemington
is one of the finest courses in the world.
Caulfield disputes with the English Epsom the
distinction of being about the worst. The
hump is worshipped in both. In addition
to the big corroborees, there are Caulfield
races about once a month, dislocating the
traffic on two great lines of railway. Schuler
says it> reminds him of the rout and con-
fusion following in the track of a beaten
army. Shunt all this, over to Flemington,
and you interfere scarcely at all with regu-
lar traffic, the race crowd being diverted to
its particular line, not used otherwise. Then
also Caulfield frequenters howl loudly over
delays. The trains crawl along the six
miles. It is no unusual thing for the jour-
ney to take an hour and a quarter. Popula-
tion has grown enormously in Armadale,
Malvern and Caulfield, and the dislocation of
traffic annoys these people and the tens of
thousands who live beyond. It is absolutely
certain that Caulfield Races will become
blankly impossible in a few years. Well,
then, make the reform now, before any
more vested interests are created.
After being held for nearly 90 years in
close grip, Mount Pleasant, near Bathurst
(N.S.W.), is to be cut up and sold in small
farms. The history of the estate, shortly
told, is this. Major-General Stewart arrived
with the 3rd Buffs, carrying with him a
commission as Lieut.-Governor, in 1825.
He also brought with him from the Duke of
York an order for the selection of 3000
acres in any part of the colony he might
choose —a reward for services in the Penin-
sular Wai*. It was not until 1827 that he
made his selection. He took up his 3000
acres in the parish of Mount Pleasant, along
Evans Plains Creek, but not including Mon-
ument Hill, where the General’s tomb and
monument are. Before leaving for India in
1828, he obtained a lease of 12,000 acres,
the remaining portion of the parish! In
1832 he returned to Bathurst to look after
his property, and deciding then to settle in
the colony, he purchased the 9000-acre block
on which Monument Hill and Mount Plea-
sant House stand. He gave ss. an acre for
it. In 1838 he secured the 2400-acres block
at the southern side of the parish, for whiqh
he paid 12s. per acre. The General —or
Colonel, as he was then —was Acting-Gover-
nor for 18 days between the departure of
Sir Thos. Brisbane and the arrival of
General Darling. A mighty land-grabber
he was ; yet one thing remains to his credit.
Brisbane left several prisoners under sen-
tence of death to be dealt with by the in-
coming Governor. Stewart, learning that
not one of them had committed a really vile
offence, exercised his power as Acting-Gover-
nor and cheated the over-worked scaffold of
the lot.

“Cylinder’s” unholy exposure :—-
N.S.W. Navigation Laws provide for every steamer
on Sydney Harbor carrying a duly certificated engi-
neer and a duly certificated master. But the fool of
an Act puts it such a way that there is nothing to
prevent a man who holds both certificates acting in
both capacities. That is, he can, theoretically, put
the engines astern in obedience to his own signal
from the wheel. Just how the oracle is worked was
shown in the Industrial Court the other day. A man
employed on a harbor vessel sued the owner for not
paying him an engineer’s wage. His union argued
that he was fireman as per engagement, but engi-
neer actually, performing the duties at a
fireman’s wages. His case got very short shrift.
“Have you an engineer’s certificate?” blandly asked
the Court. “No.” “Do you act as a bona-fide
engineer?” If the complainant said “Yes” he ex-
posed himself to a £5O fine, so naturally he said
“No,” and lost his case with 575. costs. Thus the
Law lays it down that the master on the upper deck
is the engineer in the bowels of his contraption,
when, as a matter of fact, he is really the engineer
at the helm.
On the authority of “Jeames” :
The following, which appeared among the Sun’s
cables last week, has points of humor: —
“Mr. Henry Savage Landor, the famous explorer,
has returned to England after a period of wandering
in Brazil. He states that for one, period of 16 days
he had nothing to eat, and his life was only saved
by the attacks of ants. Millions of these swarmed
the forests, and whenever, overcome by faintness, he
fell, they swarmed all over him and bit him sav-
agely, compelling him to struggle up and on.”
Landor may be described as the low comedian among
the world’s explorers. He has certainly covered a
good deal of country in his time, but no one dreams
of taking him seriously. His grandfather was that
highly imaginative poet, Walter Savage Landor,
which may have something to do with.his tendencies.
When Landor goes exploring things happen to him
that never befel another man on earth. His account
of his wanderings among the Hairy Ainu —the people
who do not eat the fish on which they chiefly subsist
till it has lain festering uproariously in the sun for
a week at least —staggered the civilised world of
the middle ’9o’s. It did not know its Landor at
the time. His more recent travels in Tibet were a
shriek from start to finish. The Grand Llamas tor-
tured him and tried to kill him. However, they
proved to be sorry bunglers. They would truss him
up, sear his back with red-hot irons, and, after hours
of strenuous toil, discover, to their annoyance, that
he was still alive. This disheartened them, so thev
let him loose and shooed him on to another batch
of Llamas further down the roafl. The same per-
formance was repeated by the new crowd, with simi-
lar results. He arrived back in England with what
purported to be Llama-marks all over him, and these
he was prepared at any moment to show to inter-
viewers. At the time of writing he is doubtless
showing the usual cynical gathering of Fleet-streeters
his alleged ant-bites. In fancy I, who knew him
well, as one of the most imperially humorless little
men on earth, can see him at it. He writes attrac-
tively, and illustrates his works with excellent photo-
graphs and nice little drawings something after the
style of Belloc’s pictures in “The Path to Rome.”
William Macleay, a distinguished N H
Wales colonist and scientist, who q<- his own
cost organised an expedition to New Guinea
in 1874, was one of the founders of the
Linnean Society, to which he presented a
meeting-house at Elizabeth Bay, Sydney He
also set apart a sum to presently provide for
four fellowships of £4OO each yearly a t
Sydney University, so that students might
follow up their studies after attaining the
degree of B.Sc. He died in 1891, and for
the first time the Council has been able this
year to invite applications for the fel-
lowships, the bequest having now reached an
amount which will allow of £l6OO a year
being paid out regularly.
From “Henri Singemoine” :
The intimation (29/10/’l3) that Sir Ernest
Shackleton “hopes to lead another Antarctic expedi-
tion in 1914” draws attention to the fact that the
polar exploration business is being overdone. The
immemorial practice is for the intrepid explorer to
begin by trying to collect sufficient cash to defray
the expenses in his native land. That failing, he
passes on elsewhere—say to Australia or M.L.— arK ]
the hat is circulated with renewed enthusiasm, lie
starts for the world’s chief refrigerating chamber
amid a scene of indescribable enthusiasm on the part
of mayors, subscribers to his fund, and newspaper
reporters and photographers. On the way to his
destination he has moving-pictures taken of the sea
—as seen when lashed by an 80-mile-an-hour breeze
—of penguins, of icebergs and of seals. Arrived
there, or thereabouts, he photographs more blizzard-
effects. more ice, more penguins and more seals. This
task over, he leads the life, as nearly as possible, of
a hibernating bear till the weather permits of his
starting home again. Then —provided that he has
not, during the hibernation period, fallen down a
crevasse and got killed, or died of frost-bite or
scurvy or exposure or hunger—he gets under way,
and presently reaches his ship. Moving pictures are
taken of the ship with the crew waving deliriously
at him over the bulwarks, and of him advancing over
the ice with his fellow-explorers and such dogs as
remain uneaten. The ship starts for civilisation.
During the next six months the explorer lectures
furiously to steadily-diminishing houses, and writes
a book against time, telling how he did it all. His
object is to pay the expedition’s debts. He hardly
ever does it, the public having little more use for a
returned explorer than America has for a beaten
pugilist. The Government is appealed to—any old
Government, whether British or Australian, will do
—and after much sordid recrimination the matter is
adjusted. The nett result is practically nil.
“Science” is supposed to benefit in some vague way,
and Australia’s public astronomers profess to be gra-
tified at the confirmation of their darkest suspicions
in regard to the origin uf certain gales. But it is
hardly pretended that anything of the least tangible
value to the world has ever been unearthed—nothing
that would, in reality, justify the untimely decease
of so much as one mariner. My view is that, if
people like Shackleton feel that they simply must
spend the public’s money in the attempt to get to
the world’s more or less hidden places, they should
proceed to some region which has a reasonable chance
of returning a dividend. The interior of Australia’s
Northern Territory, for example. It would be well
worth this country’s while to subsidise a party of
scientists to spend a year searching for gold, iron,
radium ore and the like in the Territory. There
would be no tragedies, since the climate is perfect
and the indigenous nigger comparatively tame; no
hardships worth mentioning; a splendid field for cine-
matography ; and in the final upshot the possibility
of a new Kalgoorlie or Broken Hill, or what not.
Antarctica can never hope to make a return of that
sort. About id. in the £ is the very best that can
be looked for from it.

STEIN : “ Then you didn't set the place on fire
yourself? ”
LIVINSKY : “ Veil, the chury disagreed. ”
The lighthouse-keepers at Cape Jervis
(S.A.) had a shock during a recent big
Sunday storm, and are lucky to be alive to
tell about it. The big flagstaff was struck
by lightning, the top 15ft. of it being split
into spears and laths. Then the fluid ran
down the pole, took to the chains staying it.
and was safely “earthed” within 6ft. of a
bisr mixed lot of kerosene, carbide, rockets
and oil that some humorist had established
in a galvanised shed right at their feet.
THE SPENDERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I should like to see women have the responsibility
of spending money earlier in life. —-Archbishop Clarke.
The trouble, with our womcnkind
Is that they learn too late the art
Of handling coin, and husbands find
That they are called upon to part
Their good red gold to spenders who
Have no idea what they should do—
Who never regulate the flow
Of current cash,
But ope the purse and let it go
In one big splash.
We need to catch them young and shy,
And give them golden quids galore ;
That when, as wives, they have to buy
They’ll find it something of a bore,
And habit will their souls possess
With an all-saving weariness.
Then men may waste oof at the bar,
Or lose or lend it,
Because their blase matrons are
Too bored to spend it.
Good Dr. Clarke has faith. Perhaps
He’ll offer proofs. Unless he will
He’ll not convince the other chaps,
For use is second nature still,
And sisters whom no gold’s refused to
Will go on doing what they’re used to.
They’ll gaily dissipate like smoke
The treasure golden,
Then break the new man when they’ve broke
The hapless old ’un.

Lawyer Croker, or, to be more correct,
Mrs. W. H. Croker, is disclosed as the land-
lord, or, to be more specific, the landlady,
of the Inter-State Commission. The resi-
dence is somewhere in East Melbourne, and
tariff-reformers will run the risk of forget-
ting their mission in the excitement of the
chase. Many weeks ago the Crokers’ pri-
vate house was mentioned as the probable
home of the Commission, but for some rea-
son no official announcement was made.
Then advertisements were inserted inviting
tenders for a suitable place in which the
Commission might hide its identity and be
forgotten, and, after a decent interval of
time, the admission was made that the
Crokers’ premises were successful in the
tendering. Which, of course, was no end of
a surprise.
“Icon”: The Felton bequest market has
been dull of late ; in fact, almost lifeless. A
fortnight ago, however, £2236 worth of stuff
came to hand from abroad, and the canvas
market closed in better tone. A half-finished
Claude Monet, costing £l2Bl, is called
“Rough Weather,” and should be re-named
“Rough Painting.” Fortunately for Monet
it is placed a long way from Peter Graham’s
gorgeous seascape on the same subject.
Monet was the protagonist of impressionism—
and the harbinger of post-impressionism—
that dreadful occupation followed by per-
sons suffering from paranoia. Alongside this
petit mal hangs a convulsion by Alfred
Sisely, called “Strawricks” —ugly, misshapen
heaps of dirty-blue hay, made out of burnt
sienna meadow grass, thatched with burnt
umber tiles. Of all the many commonplace
and unimaginative pictures in the dreadful
Felton collection, this is surely the very
acme of all that is banal. It cost £787.
The very light is opaque, and the trees and
foliage so intensely deformed as to convey
gloom without breadth. “Havre Harbor,”
by J. Boudin, cost £l6B, and shows how
necessary it is that Havre should spend
money on improvements if it wishes to keep
abreast of modern progress. This latest
consignment of poor paintings calls up the
query whether the Felton Bequest trustees
are not being shamefully used by their selec-
tors. Why do they not occasionally buy a
modern example of genre painting, portray-
ing the incidents of everyday life, such as
are found in such profusion in the Dutch
galleries? The public prefers Longstaff’s
“Bush Fire” to Turner’s “Okehampton
Castle” (£1500), and Streeton’s “Sydney
Harbor” to Corot’s “Bent Tree” (£3500).
And surely a public gallery should mainly
contain pictures the public can understand
and admire.

The Webber stain has been wiped off the
name-platb of Victoria, and a Colonel did it.
Incidentally he happens to be a New South
Welshman, but he did the
glorious act in Victoria. The
Opportunity came at the
George Reid luncheon, at
Federal Parliament House,
on October 22. “The King,”
shouted the well-fed throng,
with its glass in the air.
The wine was trickling down
loyal throats when a burst
of noise came from the
centre table. It was from
Ryrie, M.H.R. He Gor-
saved in a nasal falsetto that
might have made George V.
himself wish he had been
born a republican too. Those
who were not spluttering
over the half-drunk toast
tried to join in. Piggott,
M.H.R., who is proud of his
ancestry, aided and abetted,
while Jocook smiled benign-
antly, forgetting the days
when he also was a repub-
lican. The Governor-Gene-
ral’s health was proposed,
and again the welkin
shivered. This time George
VI. led half the jolly-good-
fellow-chorus two bars from
the rear, which is the right
place for the astute politi-
cian who is in a stiddy job.
He finished well behind the
first three past the post.
Irvine also sang. Then
Joe’s health was jollied.
This time Reid got a flying
start, and sang with his face
turned to Heaven, in a
kookaburra pose. A bar
from the end he seemed to side-slip into
an air pocket, but when the discordant noise
escaped through the window, somebody said,
more in sorrow than in anger, that George
hadn’t lost his voice.
Archdeacon Boyce, of Sydney, broke out
again last week with a long list of horrors
in which assault, suicide and sudden death
were ascribed to drink. Probably excess of
alcohol had something to do with some of
them. But the fact missed by the well-mean-
ing Archdeacon is that the causes that lead
to suicide, murder and other horrors are t)ie
same that lead to drink. Financial worry,
ill-health and unrequited affection drive
people to take stimulants in order that they
may lift themselves abttve their troubles —in
order that they may el as they ought to
feel without the stimula. ts. If they got good
stimulants the chances a. - e that drink might
have the desired effect. But good liquor is
scarce, and the question is one that the State
—and incidentally the Archdeacon—ought to
go into. An exhaustive analysis of every
brand of wine, spirit and malt liquor should
be carried out, and those which do not attain
a high standard of excellence should be pro-
hibited. The abolition of alcohol of every
sort does not seem to lessen crime or make
people more humane. Probably not one Turk
in 100,000 ever drank either beer or whisky,
but he seems to have just as big a category of
crimes as anyone else. Yet, according to the
Boyce theory, the non-alcoholic Turk and
Chinaman should be patterns of virtue and
sweetness.
A Canadian paper has run out breath-
lessly to call the compulsory defence
trainees in Maoriland “hired murderers.”
Following that line of reasoning, a police-
man who is trained by the sergeant to de-
fend life and property is a hired scoundrel,
and the fire brigade that is drilled to pro-
tect the public’s sleeping missus and the
kid and the time-payment piano is a hired
incendiary. Also, the boy who is forced to
go to school so that he can be trained to de-
fend a salary later on is a hired waster in
prospect. It seems a strange way of looking
at things; but, then, there are lots of
strange people about.

“Camp”: People have all sorts of vague
ideas as to what is enough to make a parson
swear. A Launceston (Tas.) congregation
learned, the other Sunday morning, that the
übiquitous “thrummer” will do the trick in
that State. After a stirring sermon a vigor-
ous old preacher made this announcement: —
Don’t forgpt the service of song this evening, and
don’t bring threepenny-bits. It will be worth more
than threepence. Damn your threepenny-bits! 1
wish they were all melted.
And turned into “plate,” presumably.
* * * *
One Barnett, of Victoria, had a hideously
narrow escape from a nasty, sticky end last
week. In defiance of every known law of
commercialism he backed a bill (for £59),
and was duly left to meet the obligation.
Being unable to do so at the moment, the
sheriff came on the scene, entered into posses-
sion of about £lOO worth of his furniture,
and proceeded to advertise the same for sale.
This upset Barnett’s young wife, who was in
a delicate state of health; so, fearing serious
consequences if the furniture were actually
sold, he went to the newspaper office and
stopped the advertisement by means of a
forged order. He was further induced to
this course by the knowledge that he would
be able to pay the £59, and did actually pay
it three days later. The order-forging busi-
ness, however, constituted contempt of court,
so Barnett was brought before Chief Justice
Madden, who, controlling his outraged feel-
ings with a furious effort, spoke as follows: —
This conduct on the part of Barnett was most
audacious and insolent. One can scarcely conceive
the confidence with which these interferences with
justice are resorted to in recent times ; in the olden
days people would hardly have dared to think of
such things....On the face of it, this appeared to
be a most outrageous act. Barnett not only took
the law into his own hands, but he defied the law
and he defied the Court....He will be imprisoned
for one month —sentence to be suspended to a recog-
nisance to be of good behavior for 12 months.
How his Honor must have grinned as he
wound up that deliverance!
A large building was going up on the
premises adjoining the suburban private
hospital of a prominent Melbourne doctor,
and the contractor had noticed a decided
falling-off in the daily outpi. t of his brick-
layers. As a result of anxious investiga-
tion, he presently made his appearance in
the office of the medical man. He was
rather angry. “Dammitall,” he said, ‘‘can’t
you pull down your blinds'? How do you
think I’m going to get my building com-
pleted if you keep the gangs off work half
their time watching your infernal opera-
tions '?”
The strike of P. and 0. officers
which happened last week has been ex-
pected since 1910, when the Merchant Ser-
vice Guild in Australia appeared before the
High Court at Sydney. The then chief
officer of the Moldavia, C. E. Irvine, aston-
ished the social natives when he detailed the
salaries paid by the cheap nigger co.—for
instance, fifth officers, £4 a month, rising to
£5 after two years; chief officers, starting
at £l4, and rising to £l7 after three years’
service. After seven years a chief mate was
overwhelmed with £2O a month. Irvine
broke the news that the senior chief mate of
the fleet staggered under a salary of £22 10s.
or £23 a month. The commodore of the
fleet received £9OO a year. The Court on
that occasion expressed no opinion on the
P. and O.’s pay-sheet, but could be heard
two blocks away thinking strenuously.
“A. Sparagus”: Ministers of the Crown
are often referred to as animated “rubber
stamps.” In many cases the description
fits, as anyone who has had much experience
of departments will know. In the
present N.S.W. State Ministry there is,
however, at least one man who performs
his duty conscientiously. Only last week a
huge pile of papers was placed before him
for noting and signature. Amongst them
was the death certificate of one of the depart-
mental inspectors. After scanning it he
laboriously wrote “Approved” across the foot
of the document, and sent it along for filing.
The Almighty will no doubt be pleased at
receiving this official endorsement of His
actions.
LOST OVERBOARD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two members of the crew were lost overboard
during the passage. One was a German, 21 years of
age, and the other a Norwegian, aged 22 years.
—N civs xtcfi\j»
We cruised about till daylight came. We searched
each wrinkled crest;
We knew that they were young and strong, and would
not give it best.
And fancy plays some cruel tricks when eyes are
strained to see ;
We thought we saw them many times and shouted
joyously.
But ev’ry time it was some mean illusion made of
weed —
Of foam and shadow—phantom-play to make a
mate’s heart bleed.
Hope never faltered. Round and round our longings
bade us sail;
We looked to see a waving arm and hear a faint,
brave hail.
But never came a human sign from that grim waste
of sea ;
We saw the belts and wood we’d thrown receding
steadily.
Then silence grew upon the ship, when lingering
hopes had gone ;
And, like some great and startled bird, the guilty
ship went on.
We knew that they, poor souls, were drowned ere one
dread hour had fled ;
We had to hover round their grave to see them safely
dead—
To see that they were safely dead, else we could
never trail
Across the haunted seas without a faint, pursuing
hail.
And who could keep upon his course or joy in
liquor take
Who heard his shipmates cry to him from every
starlit wake?
We had to watch long hours for them, though hope-
less it might be.
God pity women who bear tons to have them drowned
at sea!
x.s.w.

“DRAT THAT BOY !”
Edward Dyson has a namesake in Perth
(. ;A.) who runs a pawnbrokery, and who-
distinguished himself last week by capturing
a burglar on the premises. The burglar*
after being relieved of 40 gold watches and
10 gold chains, gave his name as Harry
Pickards.
Recently the cable columns of the dailies
have been full of tales of English and
American colliery horrors. His most ardent
admirers are quite right; Australia offers
but a poor field for the display of the nobler
qualities of one of our most eminent poli-
ticians.
Thomas Alwright Dibbs, general manager
of the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney,
reached, his 82nd year a week ago. He has
been 6G years. in the service of the
Bank, and for 46 years has been its general
manager. His associate-manager nowa-
days is H. H. Massie, the one-time famous
cricketer.
“0.K.” pictures Panton’s worse side :
The story of how he came to sentence Bob Sutton
Sievier to a month’s imprisonment, for punching
Lord Deerhurst’s nose in a Melbourne pub, supplies
historic proof of Panton’s unfitness for the position
he longtime held in the Melbourne Court. Deerhurst
was an impudent defaulter to bookmaker Sutton, and
when they met in the pub they made themselves
unpleasant to one another. Sutton may have been
the more active aggressor, nevertheless the lordling
had invited the punch on the nose by making bets
with no intention of meeting his losses. But because
this precious lordling was aide-de-camp to Sir Henry
Loch, the then Governor of Victoria, Panton sen-
tenced Sutton to a term of imprisonment, without
the onti.on of a fine—for a petty assault in a pub.
The decision was upset on appeal, of course. Panton
privately excused himself on the ground that the
sanctity of vice-regalism must be maintained. If he
didn t treat the punching of an aide-de-camp's nose
as a criminal offence, said Panton, somebody might
think of assaulting the holy Governor himself. It
never occurred to the ingrained snob that a Governor
who repudiated his gambling debts would be equally
deserving of his creditor’s violence,

THE LAB.: “Don't worry, old chap. So tong

John Creuze Hiugeston Ozier, the old
litigious Victorian ex-P.M., who died the
other day, was at Rutherglen when
the big mining boom made itself heard there,
and was a borough councillor. In
later years he used to speak lovingly of the
beautiful times they had. Each councillor
took a waddy to the meetings, and one meet-
ing broke up through the mayor being pro-
jected out of the window.
F. S. Delmer, an old Bulletin contribu-
tor, formerly a master at Sydney Grammar
School, issues a “History of English Litera-
ture from Beowulf to Bernard Shaw,” an-
nounced by English booksellers a few weeks
ago. The work first appeared in Germany,
where Delmer is Professor of English Litera-
ture at Berlin University. After all, the
“English” of Beowulf is 9 good deal nearer
German than any English of the present day
—except perhaps that spoken in George
V.’s household.
Mrs. Florence Barclay, who continues to
turn out “best sellers” in the sentimentally
religious style of fiction —“The. Rosary” and
“The Following of the Star” are perhaps
her best-known novels-—has a son in Aus-
tralia, Rev. Cyril Barclay, of Chillagoe
(N.Q.), to whom one of her recent books is
dedicated. Barclay is a “high” churchman
—about 6ft. high, to be accurate —and
burns candles and incense while wearing
various vestments abhorrent to the soul of
Sydney’s Archbish. Wright; but his popu-
larity in what the local papers delight to
style the “Cairns hinterland” is due not so
much to his ritualism as to the fact that he
is a good sport. At Cambridge about four
years ago he held the hurdles and other
championships, and he is an assiduous and
successful stage manager of amateur thea-
trical shows. Moreover, he can and
does shake a lively leg at the local dances
(the enervated residents of the Australian
tropics, where the white man can’t live, are
great on dances, and will even dance for the
church funds if no better excuse is avail-
able), and withal he can use his hands a
shade better than the average next man. A
rearrangement among the Bush Brotherhood,
of which he is a member, has just sent him
from Chillagoe to Hughenden.

Brassey, who used to fall oft' things in
Victoria, has resigned the Lord Wardenship
of the Cinque Ports —a merely nominal office
now, though it still entitles the holder to the
usq of Walmer Castle. The Duke of Wel-
lington was Lord Warden for 24 years, and
died in the rent-free humpy. The actual
work of the office was abolished in 1855, so
that, though Brassey is 77, old age is pro-
bably not the real reason for his retirement.
Lord Ivelburn, the new Com-
mander of H.M.S. Pyramus
(now in M.L.), is distinguished
chiefly by the fact that he is the
son of the Earl of Glasgow,
sometime Governor of the coun-
try whose harbors his son’s
vessel is now pervading. The
old Earl has been remarkable,
all his life, for his extreme in-
difference to appearances, not
to say criminal untidiness. In
the early days of railway travel-
ling he went to buy a ticket
and planked down a five-pound
note in payment. The booking
clerk, rendered suspicious by
the disreputableness of the old-
ster’s appearance, asked him to sign his
name on the back of the note. The seeming
tramp or hoboe duly wrote “Glasgow.”
Said the clerk bitterly, “I asked you to
write your name, you old ass, not the place
you want to go to.”

Paris Nesbit, K.C., of Adelaide, in an in-
terval of his crusade against enclosed-ground
coursing, takes on the militant suffragettes
and cheerfully suggests: “We shall have to
turn the hose on them.” “What these women
really want is husbands; of the mamy kind,”
he says. Then he recalls the Middle Ages,
when “similar phenomena appeared from time
to time, congregations of women leading an
unnatural life of sexual starvation,” and it
was found that for their hysteria “a jolly
good hiding was the most effective cure.”
“Melb” runs after the funeral, gasping as
he goes:—
Magistrate Panton was born at Knoekiemil, Aber-
deenshire, 1831. Father in the Hudson’s Bay Com-
pany’s service. Young Panton was educated at the
Scottish Naval and Military Academy and Edinburgh
University. Came to Sydney in 1851 ; then 20 years
old. Meant to be a jackeroo on a station, and
apparently was for a while, for it would be about a
year before he offered himself to Governor Latrobe
in Melbourne as a trooper. Latrobe was struck with
his commanding height and intelligent look, and
made him a Commissioner offhand. Probably there
was some influence behind him. Soon appointed
Assistant Commissioner at Bendigo, under Colonel
Bull, and became Chief Commissioner there from
1853 to 1858. Thus he held charge at Bendigo
during the Eureka trouble at Ballarat (December,
1854). Bendigo was in keen sympathy, but Panton
held it down well Denovan, the local historian,
writes: “Serious disturbance was prevented through
Panton’s tact and forbearance.” Skilful also in deal-
ing with the Chinese difficulty. Panton’s-roads,
through the Whipstick, were named after him. He
visited England and Europe in 1858. Established the
first Bendigo police court, the magistrate sitting on
a packing-case, and the clerk on a five-gallon keg.
Back from the Old World, he did some exploring.
He was at Kimberley, North-West Australia, and
made inquiries in Westralia generally about the
outlook for pearling and gold mining. Returned to
work in Victoria as magistrate and warden for the
Woods Point, Heidelberg and Yarra districts. He
mapped the whole Upper Yarra Valley, from the
Goulburn watershed to the Latrobe watershed. Pan-
ton Hills were named after him. He discovered and
named Mount Donna Buang, which he first called
Acland, but rightly changed to the aboriginal name.
Transferred to the Geelong and Western District.
Then to Melbourne in 1874, and held on there until
1907. He refused a knighthood. Panton’s auto-
cratic military predilections and general Toryism
were not altogether favorable to him as a magistrate
He was not reckoned quite so good as' Sturt and
Call. Yet he was seldom capsized in a higher court.
His deficiencies were mostly in style.

Parliament.

, W : Dooley, M.L.A., of Westralia, who
died a few days ago, was a young Parlia-
mentarian, but an old hand at Labor’s
plough. Twenty-five years ago he started in
to help it along in Svdne.v, and remained
more or less on the job till Death, the old
timekeeper, called “Spell-oh.” He was elected
for the Geraldton seat two years back, and
his political opportunities were the brighter
for the fact that, with a past crowded with
experience gained in various parts of Aus-
tralia and Maoriland, he was only 45 years
young.
John McNeill, M.L.A. for Pyrmont
(N.S.W.) until the recent redistribution,
was. the midget Laborite whose seat looked
so secure that any insurance co. almost
would have issued a policy on it; yet he
now loses it by merger, fellow-Labor man
Cochrane’s electorate of Darling Harbor
swallowing it. McNeill became known as
“Jack the Giant Killer” through having once
caught the ougnacious Wood asleep, and
bound him like the little men did the big
man in Gulliver’s Travels. Wood was so
astonished at the audacity that for once in
his life he forgot to talk back.
WILLY KELLY ret urns thanks for an
illuminated, address.
Dr. Thornton, Assistant-Bishop of Lon-
and Bishop of Ballarat (Vic.) from
1875 to 1900, is about to marry Mrs. Bice,
widow of the vicar of Battersea. The Bishop
and the lady were boy and girl together,
and the boy was the officiating minister at
the girl’s first wedding, her boy being at the
time the other boy’s curate. The plucky
youngster will be 77 when he takes his gaiters
for their second honeymoon.
I HEARD OF LATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f heard of late, in pious exaltation,
Our Bishop lash the vices of the Age—
Its lavishness, its greed for fine appointments,
Its endless avarice, its thirst for gaiety,
Its lust for sof£ luxurious devices,
Its love of flaccid ease.
“Why, only lately at our confirmation,”
His chiding voice ran on,
“I noted girls approach that holy office
Whose thoughts, ’twas plaip, were far more deeply
centred
Upon the snow-white muslin of their costume
Than on the rites divine.”
And then my thoughts flew back. A great cathedral
In mighty London rose before my eyes;
In the midst there stood, by bishops six attended,
A grave Archbishop tall,
Whose duty ’twas on this august occasion
To consecrate a new Episcopus
Unto a distant land, where priestly peers,
Striding abroad magnificent in gaiters,
Were hitherto unknown.
In sooth, a lordly prelate was his Grace.
A noble calm sat brooding on his brow,
And such the proud perfection of his garbing
That peace of mind a soft, contented halo
About his presence wove.
No single flaw or speck upon his robing
Could captious eye descry ;
And hence his air was full of satisfaction,
His glance discreet and bland.
His soft grey hair was down the middle parted ;
A ring enormous on each hand he bore;
A gown of richest satin, sable black,
With train a full yard long he deftly managed
With true jrtistic grace ;
The lawn sleeves of his rochet, swelling wide,
Were finished off with frills that sat quite smartly
Around his snowy wrists, while at his neck
A narrow band of scarlet, appertaining
To the square hood that hung behind,
Harmonious fell.
Of pleated satin was his Grace’s stole,
And fell before him, gently, softening down
The part where white and black had made a junc-
ture.
Of patent leather were his dainty shoes,
With diamond buckles glist’ning in their splendor.
And he who flaunted thus in regal raiment
Did call himself a fellow of the fishers,
Who drew their nets with Him, the Galilean,
And loved the Nazarene.
And, strange to tell, this all flashed back upon me,
As on that Sunday morn I heard the Bishop
Inveigh against the vanity of maidens
Who came arrayed to take their confirmation
In snow-white muslin fine!

The Bulletin notes with deep sorrow that this
most unfortunate co. is in a sea of trouble again.
As usual, the chairman—his name is Kater, under-
stood to be short for Catastrophe—supplies the infor-
formation in his address to the shareholders. (1)
The sugar excise duty has been abolished in Aus-
tralia. This (previously payable on the sale of the
refined sugar) added, of course, to the value of the
eo.’s stocks. This meant writing them up, which
was a bother. (2) Trade has “suffered so much
from the operation of the Commercial Trusts Act”
in Maoriland. “It has proved to be a singularly
unwise piece of legislation.” On the other hand,
“there has been no falling-off in our Maoriland trade
in consequence of the prosecution”—that is, from the
operation of the Act. The Bulletin doesn’t know
how to reconcile the two statements. But, whatever
has happened, it is bound to have been serious. (3)
The men in the refineries want more money, and
have gone to arbitration about it. Mr. Kater fears
that “the public has not yet realised the enormous
cost of these inquiries, and the harm they do in
industrial affairs.” This co., for instance, was able
to make a profit of only £255,440 last half-year.
It is beggarly. (4) Thank Heaven, a new tramway
in Fiji is at work, and the traffic is being conducted
without trouble. But “all the [extra] profit will be
needed to pay interest and upkeep, as the construc-
tion of the line has cost us much more than we
expected.” (5) The crops are turning out very well,
but we want rain for the cane to be cut in 1914.
To have to rely on Providence in this way is humili-
ating. (6) A Bill has been introduced in the
Queensland Parliament providing for Boards “to fix
yearly the prices to be paid for cane,” the farmer to
get the advantage of any rise in prices and of im-
provements in the work of millowners. Of course
this co. is “opposing the Bill.” Farmers should be
prepared to take what the co. is prepared to give.
Yet the co. may do well to view that Queensland
Bill with some alarm. The Bill itself isn’t a very
serious one, but it means that at last the farmers
have, in dealing with the C.S.R., joined forces with
the Labor people; and that combination is the most
powerful in Australia. There isn’t much in the way
of legislation that it couldn’t do. So long as the
Labor party alone was hostile to the co., nothing
much happened: it is quite a different matter when
the farmers change over.
The co.’s capital is now £3,250,000, of whi'ph no
less than £825,000 has been presented to sharehold-
ers by way of bonuses within seven years. The pres-
ent 12} p.c. div. is thus equal to 16 p.c. on the
capital as it stood seven years ago plus the amounts
paid in money since then. And in addition to the
12} p.c. dividend, enough is now put by to pay
another 4 p.c. on that capital. Even assuming that
all the capital in 1907 was actually paid—which it
wasn’t—the present visible profits are at the rate
of over 20 p.c. p.a. on the actually subscribed capi-
tal, and there is no telling what profits have been
made that are not visible. Unquestionably the co.’s
position, financial and otherwise, is extraordinarily
strong. But when a co. which is practically a
monopoly can afford to give away £825.000 of
capital in less than seven years, and meantime pay
12} p.c. on the watered capital, and on top of that
put by enough to pay another 3} p.c. : and when all
this is done by acting mainly as a middleman between
the producer and the consumer of an article of
every-day use, that co. is bound to attract hostile
attention. It is quite true that for 10 years at
least the co. has seemed to thrive on that -hostile
attention ; but the investor who is prepared to back
his opinion that it will go on thriving in that way
seems to this paper to leave out of his reckoning that
awkward factor known as one man one vote. The
one-man-one-vote yeast may take a long time to
work, but it will work in the end, all right.

Though profits have sagged a little this half-year
they are still good, and after paying the 7 p.c.
dividend all round, the lay-by is highly respectable.
In this matter of strengthening its reserves the
National has been doing very well indeed: close on
£350,000 has been added in seven years. True,
£60.000 has been transferred from the internal con-
tingency fund ; but that isn’t altogether a bad sign.
The National, like the rest of them, was left after
the boom with a big load of depreciated securities,
and apparently it went on accumulating an internal
reserve so that it might write these down. It doesn’t
seem to have been in any hurry to realise, and when
Federation, Labor Ministries and other calamities
arrived, and property values recovered in a manner
that would be wonderful in any other country, this
bank apparently unloaded at prices which didn’t call
for any help from the old contingency reserve. If
it means what it ought to mean, the transfer from
that internal reserve indicates that the old trouble
has been entirely got rid of, and that reasonable pro-
vision has also been made for shrinkages in new
securities. That, of course, implies that there is, or
ought to be, a contingency reserve still in existence,
1 he idea that a bank sliouid show all its reserves
isn’t as good underneath us it looks on the surface,
ft might be all right if a bank knew just when it
had made a bad debt; but it doesn’t. Jones borrows
•'5OOO to-day, and the security looks all right.
N'ext year, however, it doesn’t look quite so good,
but there are hopes of an improvement. In the
third year Jones takes to drink, hot the manager
doesn’t know that, though he should have found it
out. In the fourth year things get worse, and in the
fifth year the security is sold bv the bank and
fetches only £2OOO. It isn’t a fair thing to charge
the loss of £3OOO all to the fifth year—the one
year, perhaps, in which the bank did nothing for
which it could be blamed. The right thing is to
accumulate a contingency or bad debts reserve, and
make good the loss or the greater part of it out of
that.
Returning to the National, the bank has pursued
a bold or (as it has seemed sometimes to The Bul-
letin) cheeky policy. It cracked hardy even when
it must have been feeling pretty sick. The public
took it at its own valuation, and these figures show
the result in 10 years:—

Most figures have moved down ; but that isn’t unex-
pected. General conditions have been responsible for
that. The bank certainly couldn’t afford to go on
lending more in the face of shrinking deposits.
Even now, with nearly three-quarters of a million
less advances out than it had 12 months ago, it has
only 35 p.c. of its liabilities represented by liquid
assets. The latter don’t amount to as much as the
current-account deposits, exclusive of bills and notes.
Theoretically, at any rate, the position isn’t strong
in regard to bullion and liquid resources. From other
points of view things look better. The shareholders’
money in the business (capital and reserves) repre-
sents £1 for every £5 10s. of the public’s; and
that is a safe proportion, other things being normal.
Also, as before remarked, the capital and reserves
are to all appearances intact and sound. At any
rate, they show very fair earnings, which, is gener-
ally an indication of soundness:—

The Royal of Queensland is in some respects like the
City of Sydney. Its losses 20 years ago were con-
siderably greater than were provided for out of
capital or reserved profits. That of course meant
making good the unprovided-for losses out of future
profits; and no outsider could possibly know in
either case how much the unprovided-for losses
amounted to. That uncertainty, in its turn, meant
that the general _publie didn’t, view the two concerns
wrth much favor: it stood off their shares as an in-
vestment, and it didn’t rush the officers with either
deposits or first-class advance business. So the re-
covery of both banks has been slow, and even now
the investing public isn’t very cordial. The Queens-
land Royal, for instance, claims, in addition to its
eanital, £75,755 of reserves. That is to say, osten-
sibly it has its capital intact, and in addition about
30s. for each pref. share (of £lO paid), and 19s. for
each ordinary share (of £6 10s. paid). Yet the
£lO prefs. are under par and the ordinaries are
worth only about £4 15s. to £5 in the market. It
may be that the investor doesn’t care a hang about
his principal and looks only to the interest return ;
and in that case, seeing that the div. is only 5
p.c,, of course he wouldn’t pay par at present. Still,
there are people who are more or less concerned about
their principal as well as their interest; and if this
bank has its capital intact plus (say) 3s. in the £
of reserves,, there should be an attraction about it.
What really is the Royal’s position The Bulletin
doesn’t know. The profit disclosed in excess of the very
moderate 5 p.c. div. is small—scarcely enough to
pay another J p.c. Calculated on capital and alleged
reserves combined, earnings have lately been as
follows: —
Profits Compared with Shareholders'’ Funds.
Mar., 1910 £4 9 11 p.c. Mar., 1912 £5 3 10 p.c.
Sept., 1910 4 9 9,, Sept., 1912 5 2 4,,
Mar., 1911 5 1 0 „ Mar., 1913 4 19 2 „
Sept., 1911 5 2 2 „ Sept., 1913 5 2 5 „
If all the profit is disclosed, or if only enough extra
is put into the stocking to provide for reasonable
contingencies, then it would seem from this low
return as if either the bank’s current business is
poor or many of its assets are lame ducks still.
Certainly, even if there wasn’t a single ■ over-
valued asset in the Royal’s books, the profit calcu-
lated on capital and -corves wouldn’t be likely to
be large. Take these figures:—
Sept., 1912. Sept., 1913.
Bills and notes., £12,993 £11,394 Dec. £-1,599
Deposits, etc. . . 1,535,459 1,537,265 Inc, 1,866
Liquid assets . . 668,547 779,137 Inc. 110,590
