The public’s money in the business is thus
£1,548,659; and as capital and reserves amount to
£623,059, there is only £2 9s. of other people's
money for every £1 of the shareholders'. Most other
banks have from £5 to £7. Further, of Die pub-
lic's £1,548,659, no less thifn £779,137 is liquid—-
mostly coin and bank balances —so that less than
half the public’s money is used in the ordinary busi-
ness of the bank. Between them, these two thing's
must mean comparatively poor earnings. But they
mean something else. Having so little of the pub-
lic’s money on deposit, and having .so little even of
that little out in advances, the position of the de-
positor is remarkably strong, while the shareholder’s
risks are remarkably small. Further, assuming that
the capital is intact, and disregarding the reserves
altogether, the bank has the foundations for a much
larger business; and if the right securities were
available, it has the cash to increase its advances at
once. Probably because it lias had a pretty tough
fight, the management appears to be bent on making
things safe and sound, dividends being quite a sec-
ondary matter for the present. The Bulletin has
no stones to throw at that policy, though on the
other hand it hasn’t much to say for a policy under
which moss is allowed over the premises.

That “written off goodwill, £12,000,” is more dr
less guessed at. The amount certainly has been
written off reserves, but what asset or alleged asset
was written down to correspond isn’t so obvious.
Twelve months ago, however, the directors said
that, as specially desired by shareholders, they had
got “a reliable valuation of the co.’s freehold and
leasehold properties,” and as this valuation was
higher than book values, the directors during the
year would “write off a substantial sum from good-
will account.” Apparently while they were about
it they wrote off some more, out of reserves. How
much of the capital is still represented by goodwill
isn’t ascertainable, because that intangible asset is
lumped with tangible assets like freeholds and lease-
holds and machinery and rolling stock. For all the
light the balance-sheet throws on the matter, there
may be 10s. in tangible assets for every £1 share
or there may be 19s. lid. If, however, there are
tangible assets worth anything like the full amount
of the capital, the co. ought to be paying a good
deal more than 5 p.c. Still, though the position is
so well cloaked, it is plain enough that, in the
management’s opinion, a considerable improvement
has taken place of late years. The foregoing table
covers 12 years’ operations, and during that period
no such profit as £15,803 was ever disclosed before.
The exes, have increased, like most people’s exes.;
but receipts have increased still faster. This next
comparison brings most of the co.’s figures into a
few lines:—
1901. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Liabilities to public £68,307 £69,855 £74,177 £80,910
Properties, goodwill,
plant, etc . . 215,922 263,400 266,027 263,405

account) . . 41)848 48,528 46,673 50,180

The growth of receipts from £46,231 in 1911 to
£75,317 in 1913 is very remarkable. And, better
still, it hasn’t been accompanied by any great
capital outlay involving new liabilities: the co. cer-
tainly owes more money than it did, but the growth
lias not been at all staggering. Altogether, then,
though the eo.’s position is enveloped in mist, there
are various things to suggest that trade and profits
have improved, and that, internally, the co. is much
sounder than it was a few years ago. It operates
in a district which isn’t likely to become less popu-
lous, and among people who are not likely to forsake
beer in a hurry ; and, though this, like every other,
brewery co. will have to spend money in fighting the
Intemperance party, its strongholds probably won’t
be much shaken hy the present local-option law.
For the asininity of that law lies in the fact that,
where pubs are most numerous, there reduction is
most difficult. In other words, where there is most
necessity for reduction, reduction is hardest to win ;
and where it is least necessary, or not necessary at
all, a victory comes ouite easily.

a 10 p.c. div. calls for as much money now as 224

years the capital was £175,000; then £25,000 was
transferred from reserves, and, though the money
wasn’t wanted, £50,000 was got in from sharehold-
ers, making the total £250,000. Last year £150,000
was added to capital, the lump being lifted out of
reserves; so that for every one share held in 1907
the holder now has two—provided he hasn’t realised.
As a matter of fact, a good many of the old hands
have realised. For a few years the co. and its stock
were neglected, and the shares got into a compara-
tively few hands; many of them were mopped up
at a’few shillings. The people who owned them had
a lot of influence, and, having also the goods to
deliver, a very fine trade —largely bickering—was
built up. Profits raced ahead, and the dividend on
the comparatively small capital dazzled the public.
From being neglected, the stock thus became just
about the most pooular in the coal division, and
there were buyers for it when almost all others were
neglected. There is such a thing, though, as over-
loading a co. with capital; and the issue of bonus
shares in this case has been too rapid to be altoge-
ther comfortable. Of course, its position is remark-
ably good It isn’t, like the Adelaide Steamship Co.,
for instance, which, while also making rapid bonus
issues, got largely into debt. The Bellambi has no
debts to speak of; —

Its mines are very large (over 200,000 acres) and
well-equipped, and its colliers are among the best.
But £400,000 is a big capital; and, though the pro-
fits have for many been more .than enough to
pay a 10 p.c. div. oil that lot, the margin isn’t so
large as to hold out much hope for anything con-
siderable in the wajr of further bonus issues in the
near future. Last year’s prqfits certainly are not
much of a criterion, because labor troubles havi.
lately been numerous; but costs are higher, and an.
likely to go on being higher, ami it isn’t so ease to
“pass it on” in the export and bunkering coal trade
as it is in some others. For one thing, oil comneti
tion has a steadying effect on prices.

This co., which used to mine under the sand around
Newcastle Harbor, worked its patch out a while ago,
ami, by good luck or good management, or some of
both, got hold of a cheaply -developed and appar-
ently first-rate property at Cessnock. From this
Neath colliery it has now begun to make another
good living. Its reserves don’t mount very rapidly;
but it is doing now what it wasn’t wise enough to do
before—as it takes out the coal, it writes down the
book value of the mine, and is accumulating the
amount thus written off. In other words, as the
coal which represents the co.’s capital is eaten up
in producing revenue, other assets are piled up to
represent that capital—the only honest way of doing
things. Assuming that the amount added to the
pile is adequate, this means that the dividends are
dividends, and not partly return of capital. During
the past 12 months, while a fair amount has been
added to the plant, and a large amount of back
dividend has been paid up, the surplus of liquid assets
over liabilities has been increased from £22,429 to
£ 24,803. A trifle of that is, however, due to in-
creased capital, which now stands at £109,749.
There are 90,000 ordinary shares and 20,000 124
p.c. pref. shares; £251 is outstanding in calls on
ordinaries, otherwise the shares are now all fully-
paid. The past half-year’s trading was hardly so
profitable as that of the corresponding half of 1912,
but the outlook is satisfactory; and, when industrial
conditions settle down a bit, and the public isn’t
quite so scared of putting money into labor-employ-
ing industries as some of it is just now, investment
in the coal section will no doubt get more attention.
It seems to deserve it.

“A beer-pump in the pub is worth two dozen in the
scrap-heap” is evidently the golden rule the directors
are working under. Hence that £7OO mentioned as
written-off, as well as the £lBOO for the previous
half-year, goes to a Building and Plant Fund. Prior
to that the amounts in that column were merely
written off in a general way. But whichever way
it is put, the profit written off is no doubt more or
less laid aside for the drier day that may come when
Mr. Stiggins has closed another pub or two belonging
to the co. The co.’s holding of pubs is pretty ex-
tensive : rents from that source for the past six
months were £BO2, or more than sufficient of them-
selves to pay the 10 p.c. dividend. But the rent
item is only safe while the licenses are; and licenses
are always in danger these days. Yet even if all
rents were stopped—and they are not likely to be, for
as one pub is closed up go the rents of the others —
the brewery receipts would still stand by the share-
holders. These figures are moving* fast. For the
Feb.-August half of 1911 the net revenue from that
source was £782, and for the past six months
£2OBO. Apart altogether from rents, the net profit
for the six months would have been £1270 —enough
to pay the usual 10 p.c. and leave almost £7OO
to put aside for contingencies. The co.’s capital
is £11.488, and of that and the reserves, no less
than £19.756 is in the pub property before men-
tioned. That is the value of the pubs with licenses;
what it would be without licenses is another matter.
But the population of the- State is-growing, and
that means more thirst. And more thirst means
more beer, whether pubs are closed or not.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow i6in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscrav, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
inquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required, to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu
factured.*

when he was here; but when he left Block 10 and
was the uruler-dog, he didn’t get half a fair deal.
Among other things, his last published estimate of
ore was 606,240 tons. When he left, the directors
regretted to report “a serious diminution of the ore
reserves, the figures now reported (March, 1912)
being 283,500 tons.” 'this paper has reason to
believe that a subsequent estimate of Low’s wasn t
published ; and, anyhow, Time seems to be showing
that the old estimate was a good deal nearer the
mark than the new one.
That things have improved both underground and
at surface under O. B. Ward's management The
Bulletin doesn't doubt. The new mill is certainly
doing better work. That plant, however, dates from
Low’s day. Not that Low had much to do with it ;
in fact, some of the trouble between Low and the
Board was, no doubt, due to the latter calling in
George Weir, manager of the B. H. North, to advise
as a mill expert. Weir designed it. In its first
full half-year (to September, 1912) it recovered
42 17 p.c. of the silver and 68.42 p.c. of the lead ;
in the past half-year 45.27 p.c. of the silver and
71.20 p.c. of the lead. But dort’t put all that im-
provement down to better mill practice. The ore has
had a good deal to do with it —-a fact which the
directors admit: “The ore coming from the develop-
ment work on the new lode is more amenable to
treatment than the ore hitherto available.” The
development of this new lode on the western side of
the lease has been the important feature of the half-
vear. Nobody can say yet what effect it will have
on the life of the mine; but it certainly won’t
shorten that life. Ward says of it: “This lode is
opening Up in a most promising fashion, having been
proved for a length of over 100 ft. at both the 515 ft.
and 615 ft. levels, these two levels being connected by
a rise and winze in the ore body. The lode averages
about 20ft. in width, and is somewhat patchy in
values, but the ore so far opened up will average
silver, 13 p.c. lead and 9 p.c. zinc.” The ore treated
in the October-March half-year, before this lode was
tapped, averaged 10.200 z. silver, 11.9 p.c. lead and
13.1 p.c. zinc. The new lode at the present levels
is, therefore, much poorer in silver but rather richer
in lead. As for zinc, the contents may be expected
to increase lower down. The co. certainly needs this
ore. Reserves are not estimated now, but, as before-
mentioned, Ward thought there were 283,500 tons 18
months ago, and 143,247 tons have been milled since
then.
Block 10’s financial position is still excellent. The
surplus of liquid assets on September 30 was
£108,434, as against £82,181 six months earlier;
and over £BO,OOO of this is net cash. There are
100,000 shares in the co.
From a man in the business, with no axe
to grind, after inspecting the Broken Hill mines;
The North is a perfect pattern, and looks as though
it would beat the South in the long run, having the
most regular ore bodies, the best grade and the
lowest working costs on the field, besides the best
organisation generally. Weir, the manager, is accu-
mulating a reserve of ore (now about 10,000 tons) on
the surface, in case of any interruption below,
whereas the South has no storage capacity, the ore
going straight into the crushers, and if the latter
are not working the truckers are thrown idle. The
new selective flotation processes are all in the ex-
perimental stage, but they promise well. The South
people will probably adopt the Owen process, and
hope, when their slimes plant is complete -some time
next year, to treat 1000 tons of slimes weekly from
current production, and a similar quantity from
stacked slimes. These are estimated to yield 20s. a
ton net profit with lead at £l4, or another 10s. a
year in dividends. I went through the British, and was
favorably impressed. They have plenty of ore available
at every level from the sth to the 10th, but it is
patchy. The 10th level looks best of all, and the
indications in the crosscut at the No. 11 level from
Thompson’s shaft are similar to those at the corres-
ponding 10th level. Within a week or two they
should, at the 11th level, run into the big bulge of
ore which is now being opened up at the 10th. The
mill is in course of rebuilding, and will not be com-
pleted before February or March next. The Mineral
Separation process is doing good work elsewhere, and
I think the British will also be able to show good
results from it. In any case, British is likely to be
the gamble of the coming year.
A syndicate is on the stocks to relieve the
Eleanora (N.S.W.) of its troubles. _ The idea isn’t
to buy the mine, hut to acquire the interests of Tar-
butt and Janson, who have been propping up the
co. in London, where the office is. These two men
own 27,945 out of the co.’s 45,650 issued" shares ;
also they hold £5OOO 'of first-mortgage debentures.
The syndicate can hasre the lot for £lO,OOO cash on
condition that it puts up another £7500, the latter
to go to the co. and be secured by second-mortgage
8 p.c. debentures. The man who holds the option
which the syndicate is invited to buy takes nothing ;
this is the smallest amount The Bulletin has ever
known an option-holder to rake off. However, is
mentioned that if the syndicate scores, he won t be
insulted if it offers him a trifle; and it is observed
that there are £2500 worth of shares in reserve.
The present Eleanora co. has had a very brief life:
it was formed only about 15 months ago. As usual,
things cost more than had been figured on, and now,
with the plant unready, it is out of funds. The
£7500 will, it is estimated, complete the equip-
ment —anyway, make it complete enough to allow
the co. to start milling and keep things going for a
month afterwards. The ore reserves are reckoned at
23,000 tons, besides lower-grade stuff which should
pay on a big-milling basis. In any case, the syndi-
cate comes in on reasonable terms. Of its £1i,500
a total of £12,500 will be secured by mortgage, and
the plant should be worth that much for breaking-up.
The flotation of the syndicate will shift the control of
the co. from London to Australia. #
“Main Shaft” : Golden Springs looks bet-
ter in J. H. Fawcett’s report than The Bulletin
said it was, and the cleaning out of other crosscuts
at the 200 ft. level further improves the show. The
200 ft., at which level the French co’s plans give the
ore as 190 ft. long and 90ft. wide, is now cleaned up
for 140 ft. of length and 35ft. of width, and for those
measurements bulks lldwt. Above the 200 ft. there
are at least 20,000 tons of lldwt. ore ready for easy
winning, and below the 200 ft. the mine is virgin.
At the 250 ft. the ore must have been showing in two
places when the French co. closed down. It was a.
very careful closing down, with all timber secured
for a re-opening, so that the mine is excellently pre-
served after 17 years of submersion ; and the sus-
pension of work, like most other deeds of the French
co., looks sinister wherever it isn’t stupid. The
250 ft. level has been driven 180 ft., and midway a
rise went up and struck the ore, carrying it to within
a foot or so of the 200 ft. After submersion, the
water found a way out of the lode at the 250 ft., and
is now pouring" into the level, thus exposing the ore
in places. About 100 tons of ore which had run
into the levels and crosscuts has been cleaned up. It
bulks lldwt.
Golden Springs (or Brown’s Creek) seems to the
writer to be a smaller and poorer Mount Morgan,
with a similar genesis. Five-ore bodies have been
located in the vents of ancient thermal springs l —ore
bodies of flint, iron and limestone splinters, and their
binding sands, all carrying gold, which has impreg-
nated also the decomposing granite, showing in places
as a footwall. No. 2 ore body, which .shows in the
largest open-cut, and Was sampled by the French co.
for more than Bdwt., cannot be touched yet; and
No. 3 need not be worked for many years. Mclntyre,
who was an underground manager before the days
of the French co , is back in his old job, and re-
lates how he offered to break and deliver ore from
the 200 ft. at Is. 6d. a ton. Whear Roberts, Who
has stood by the mine for 10 years in the face of
heart-breaking delays, crushed 6319 tons in 1904 at
a total cost for mining, milling and removing over-
burden of 7s. 4d. per ton. In 1905 he crushed
10,933 tons for a total cost of 6s. sd. per ton.
He recovered only Bs. sd. per ton ; but the tails were
worth 15s. a ton. Of these tails 7720 tons were
cyanided in 1909 for £3422 worth of gold, or
Bs. lid. a ton. But all this was in mining
from cuts too deep for economy. All the big money
made in Brown’s Creek belonged to a time when
cyanide was unknown, and tails \vorth 3dwt. or 4dwt.
went down the creek. Fifteen head of the stampers
are to be commissioned as soon as shaft and tram
repairs are made, but the reduction scheme sure to
be followed will be coarse crushing in solution,
sliming everything, tube milling and cyanidation in
agitation. Bulk tests show a 96 p.c. extraction by
this method. The mine has a curiosity in a flint lode
in No. 2 open-cut; it averages 6ft. wide, and bulks
lldwt. of gold, discoverable, however, only by fire
or cyanide. Mining at the Golden Springs will be
cheap ; the coarse crushing and pulping will increase
the capacity of the present 60-head mill to a very
big tonnage, and as the tonnage is available, and the
samplings bulk a good half-ounce, the old mine
should be in for the best years of its life.
General-manager Bellinger, of Great
Cobar, drons a line to say that G. A. Laird, late of
Mexico, doesn’t take charge of underground opera-
tions at the big mine vice Treloar, but “has been
appointed manager of Chesney, in full charge of un-
derground and surface operations.”
“Bulli” : As an instance of the astounding
rot published at times by the daily papers, the tale
l of a special reporter on H.M.A.S. Australia re the
cussedness of South Coast (N.S.W.) coal would be
hard to beat. According to this credulous writer,
the boiler fires, during speed trials, became banked
up with ash to a level higher than the fire-doors ; the
fires could not be “drawn,” and there was unlimited
cursing by all concerned. Information was also
thrown in to the effect that, as the South Coast coal
contains 30 p.c. ash as against the 14 p.c. of Welsh
coal, the name of the former is mud for steaming
purposes. Now, all this is drivel, and must have
been pumped in by someone with an object to serve.
Boiler fires are not “drawn,” except in case of
emergency, and there is no earthly reason why they
should not be cleaned (provided the firemen keep
awake), no matter what percentage of ash, within
reason, is in the coal used. As a matter of fact,
Australian coals are all high in ash contents, the
average being about 14 p.c. ; good Welsh coal con-
tains about 5 p.c. Still, South Coast coal has been
used for firing ships under severe speed-trial condi-
tions, and there is no record of their not having been
able to avoid getting 18in. of ash on the fire-bars.
It is generally in the Foster Fraser type of journal-
ism that such absurd inaccuracies occur. The man
with the notebook lands, say, in the stokehole of a
battleship, hot-foot after a story. Information is
fired at him from several directions at oqce, which
he more or less gets the hang of. He seizes on un-
important details, magnifies them, and, in general,
gets a wrong idea of the relative importance of
things. The net result is a collection of “shop” terms
and misapplied technicalities, which are worked up
into a dish of journalistic tripe, liberally seasoned
with inverted commas and exclamation marks. The
man in the street mostly swallows this with gusto,
’this description of newspaper article is nearly always
harmless and amusing, but perverted stuff like that
concerning South Coast coal, damaging as it is to
Australian interests, is merely a disgrace to the
journal which prints it.
The British-Anstralian Oil Co., which
made a mess of things at Murrurundi (N.S.W.),
has got out of the hands of the receiver, and Mr.
Gray has arrived to take charge again. So far as
can be judged at present, the re-constructed co. will
have another £75,000 to play with, and this will be
spent in completing the second bench of retorts and
carrying through other mainly-surfaee works. It is
apparently accepted as a fact that the shale is in the
mine—shale, that is, worth 50 gallons or thereabouts
to the ton. But there are at least doubts on that
point, and if the co. were wise it wouldn’t spend,
another penny on the surface until it had made dead-
sure of its possessions underground. So far the co.
never has been able to get out 50 gallons to the
ton ; the most it could manage was about 45 gallons,
and that was for only a very brief period. Also,
unfortunately, it hasn’t been able to get much of a
market for lubricating oils and the like. Naturally
enough, engineers are loth to try new lubricators,
and the introduction of brands is a slow and
tedious process. Further, there is the keen compe-
tition of the Standard Co. and its associates in every
line, and thus far the Australian Government hasn’t
given much help to the local producer. No doubt
like the Commonwealth Oil Corporation, the 8.A.0.
has wasted a lot of money; but matters with the
latter concern seem to have been put on a much
better footing, and if this paper’s good wishes can
do it any good it has them.
Mountain Queen (W.A.) is busy sam-
pling, and in all probability will acquire the Great
Victoria. This show is some miles from the co.’s
present property, and, as hinted in a recent par., the
idea is to use it as an additional source of siioplv
for the mill. There is an immense tonnage in sight,
but most of it is low grade. The average values are,
however, considered good enough for the Mountain
Queen to make a profit on.

that the 10,000 oz. mark will be reached early in the
new year. * 4
Lake View and Star is officially credited
with a development at 2100 ft., but already its value
has been more than discounted by the market. Some
time ago good values were struck in a winze from
the No. 19 level, and continued down to about the
2100 ft. workings The same values have now been
met with in a drive off the main crosscut at the
latter point, and for the first 20ft. or so assays have
been fairlv good.

Fortune has again taken Associated Nor-
thern under its wing. The last 45ft. of driving at
No. 5 level in its Ora Banda show has assayed from
85s. to 130 s. ppr ton, and the presence of telluride
in the ore is reckoned to pulverise the theory tba +
the rich oxidised stuff exnosed in the- No. 4 lev n l
was only a secondary enrichment. The shaft is now
down to No. 6 level. Should good values be met at
this point, shareholders may be much more hopeful
about the future.

Edna May (W.A.) proposes to “materi-
ally increase its output at an early date,” and to put
it up higher still when it starts on the treatment of
accumulated sands. This information is all very well
from a market point of view, but the co< must be
careful not to over-reach itself. Several Westralian
concerns that The Bulletin knows of did this, and
when the cry of “stand from under” came without
official warning, there was much agony and grief.
The holding of the co. has just been added to by the
acquisition of the Myrtle Consols, The value of this
lease to the Edna May is based on the possibility of
the main ore shoot dipping into it at a considerable
depth The financial strength of the co. is gradually
being built up, and it is now estimated to have about
A 8000 in reserve, and dumped sands and slimes to
the value of £5OOO. The reef is expected to be cut
in the crosscut from the new shaft at 150 ft. some-
where about the middle of November.
By treating other people’s ore and resi-
dues as well as its own, Greenfinch (W.A.) obtained
a revenue of £3506 for the half-year ended Septem-
ber, and made a profit of £1259. Liabilities at the
date of the balance-sheet were practically nil.
Machinery and other property are valued at £3223
and stores at £l3l, while cash assets are estimated
at £2243. The manager reckons that there is nine
months’ profitable work ahead of the co., and a new
reef sunk on at a point some distance south-east of
the original workings is well spoken of. But a deathly
silence is preserved regarding the future of the mine
in depth.
Mount Morgan’s explanation about the
sudden and heavy drop in the grade of ore treated
during the four weeks to October 19 isn’t convinc-
ing. The drop was from 306 p.c. to 2.44 p.c. copper,
and from 7.7Bdwt, to 6.l3dwt. gold. The explanation
—the official one, of course—is that in making prepar-
ation for the new plant it is necessary to open up
the mine in order to provide ore suitable for the
concentrator. “This ore is low grade and highly
siliceous, but, being mined, it must be smelted, other-
wise it rapidly deteriorates. The necessity of smelt-
ing this low-grade siliceous material has. caused the
reduced output.” It may have done so; but the
necessity of smelting this ore wasn’t due wholly to
the reason given. For many months the smelters
have been treating ore much higher than the average
of the reserves, and an end must come to this policy
some timp. But to deal with the explanation in
detail. Firstly, the Mount Morgan siliceous ore
does not rapidly deteriorate on exposure. Even its
most pvritic ore takes some time to do this, under
hot and rainy conditions. Secondly, there wasn’t
enough of the siliceous low-grade ore mined from
development to make a difference worth mentioning,
even if the alleged deterioration did take place. Take
the official figures supplied to the Queensland Gov-
ernment (published in the Mining Journal) from
July to October inclusive. The figures testify to
feet of driving and 160 J feet of sinking. These,
after making liberal allowance for sectional area of
the openings, give about 50,000 cubic feet, or less
than 5000 tons. That is, a monthly average of
1000 tons—a mere capful. And it may not have all
been low-grade siliceous ore. There is something
hollow about the “explanation” somewhere.
The Inauirv Board, after taking evidence
at Charters Towers, doesn’t favor the deep-sinking
scheme proposed by Tom Mills. It thinks a shaft to
3300 ft. and of smaller diameter than 18ft. will ven-
tilate the levels to 3000 ft. If the shaft is started
it will take about four years to complete, so in order
to continue development the Brilliant Deeps and
Block are pushing on with a ventilating scheme of
their own, which means that the Block shaft will
become the upcast from No. 4 level (2460 ft.). A
connecting shaft from the Block’s 2000 ft. level to
the Deeps’ No. 4 should be completed early next
year. The reefs are too patchy to make future mine
developments anything but a gamble.
Tin-mining in the Herberton (Q.) dis-
trict is much more prosperous than it was a while
ago. From the Rainbow, owned by Forrester and
Thompson, a recent crushing of 507 tons returned 61
tons concentrates, valued at £7230 The Vulcan,
at Irvinebank, for a run of four weeks, obtained 40
tons of concentrates ( £3488) from 725 tons of ore.
Average gross value, £4 16s. 2d. per ton. At the
550 ft. level there is a face of high-grade ore Bft.
wide, assaying up to 30 p.c. black tin, and down at
the 840 ft.” level there is a stoning area 33ft. x 23ft.,
bulking over 2J p.c. black tin. There is a revival
also at the Gilmore, and some good stuff is being
won by tributors and on behalf of the co. ; 147 tons
from the dump returned 4 tons-7cwt. black tin. But
the co. still badly wants another rich shoot like the
one that was worked from the Dolly Gray shaft.
Annan River (N.Q.) is to be fortified by
an increase of capital. Present shareholders are to
be offered 24,000 new shares at £l. As the shares
are nominally 10s. each, this means a stiff premium.
The co. is building a dam at a neighboring creek,
and will install a pumping plant. A bucket dredge
is also to be purchased to deal with some ground
lower down the Annan. It may be noted that the
September output was 111 tons of black tin, value
£l4OO. The new shares will have a prior claim on
profits up to 12s. 6d. per share ; they will then rank
equally with the' old shares.
Great Fitzroy (Q.) has called up its final
6d. per share to provide funds for the Laloki purchase.
The 450,000 ss. shares are now fully paid up, and the
other 450,000 are optioned. The Laloki workings
are down to 160 ft., and the outlook at that depth is
good. The ore assays from 3 to 15 p.c. copper and
3dwt. cold, and there are 170,000 tons of it proved
—and what is proved shouldn’t be more than a trifle
compared with what remains to be proved. Laloki
may yet pull Fitzroy out of the slough.
Chairman Harry Dodd says that at the
Cathcart, Ararat, out of two tons of gold, worth
£190,000, profits have been £55,000. To date
24.228 fathoms from the gutter have yielded
48,5860 z., or an average of 2oz. per fathom. Burnt
Creek Co. battled along on a 2dwt. yield with a
jangling plant at No. 1 shaft, and with the pumps
going full strokes Cathcart had a shallow .gutter,
the ground wasn’t conspicuously heavy, and the
water was comparatively light. The profit should
therefore have been much more. Dodd puts the blame
on the miner, whose efficiency, he says, has dimin-
ished bv one-half. As the contract system prevails, the
explanation isn’t conclusive. It is far more likely
that the able-bodied miners have flitted, because the
inducements to labor in the wet alluvial ground are
not comparable with the earnings and conditions on
Broken Hill and other mining fields.
Mount Lvell has joined or is joining forces
with the Wallaroo Phosphate Co. in a little scheme
to make more money out of the manure business in
S. Aus. A new co.' is to take over the interests of
the two concerns in that State, and is to. have the
extremely modest capitalisation of £400,000. Both
the original cos. are to be represented on the
Board, but it isn’t hinted that any part, of the
£2500 extra voted to the Mount Lyell directors for
supervising the superphosphate side-line is to be
abandoned. Bowes Kelly and Joe Synje hated the
work entailed in running an industrial concern, so
the junior men on the fat-feed Lyell Board are pretty
well certain to get the new job. More-
over, no love is lost between some of the
“hummers” on the Wallaroo Phosphate Board
and the Lyell seniors. With this fusion, the S. Aus.
manure business will be pretty well in one lot of
hands, which may mean toasting the farmer.presently
per medium of an “honorable understanding.”
The Primrose, at Rosebery (Tas.), has
had a good development in a footwall crosscut below
No. 2 tunnel. This crosscut has proved a new lens
that has given payable assays over a width ot loti.
There are 137 ft. of backs. For some time past the
Primrose has been shipping silver-lead-zinc ore to
the smelter at Zeehan, which, however, has had to
suspend work because ot a shortage of coke. lne
Primrose and Hercules lately amalgamated with the
smelting co. at Zeehan with a view to obtaining a
better extraction of metals from complex sulphides,
and the good development at the Primrose will put
heart into the new concern.

me a heavy, depressed feeling. After using various
remedies without success, I tried Chamberlain’s Tab-
lets, the first dose of which did me so much good
and gave such relief that I persevered with them
and now enjoy good health.”*

Sydney, November 3, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
Cup Day in Melbourne brings its minor
joys to ardent bridge players, who stay in
Sydney while their friends are losing money
at Flemington. Bridge tables —seven make
a busy afternoon —are arranged, and a Cup
Sweep is put together in somebody’s old hat.
Husbands, who justify their existence on
these occasions, ’phone the winner’s name
from town. Savory sandwiches and other
delicacies support ladies who draw the wrong
horse. Drawing the wrong horse and dream-
ing that Jockey Lobster on Nightmare won
the Cup are topical events just now. Mrs.
A. E. Hughes, of Carthona, Darling Point,
and Mrs. VValter German give the two big-
gest Cup-bridge parties here this week.
A Natal paper has recently given, as in-
formation supplied by its London correspon-
dent, precisely the “news” that a ’Frisco
paper had a while ago. Here it is, boiled
down. “Lady Denman, wife of the Gover-
nor of Australia, is on her way back to the
commonwealth” (they give us a small c,
by the way) “to join her husband. If the
truth must be told” (apparently this un-
usual departure almost breaks the heart of
the Natal publication) “she was very loth
to leave England.... She is counting the
months, in fact, hours” (why not minutes?)
“until Lord Denman’s term of office shall
have expired. As the daughter of Lord and
Lady Cowdray she is a great heiress” (“she”
is the cat’s aunt in nursery chatter, if you
overwork that personal pronoun too long at
a stretch) ... .“It is funny that two such
brainy people as Lord and Lady Cowdray
should have had the frantic” (frantic is fine
descriptive stuff) “ambition that their only
daughter should possess the finest string of
pearls in Europe and one that would take
the shine out of the Duchess of Marlborough’s
1 am told” (presumably by the cat’s
aunt) “that Lady Denman is in the greatest
anxiety lest any of her family should pick
up the terrible Australian cockney accent,
and is taking every precaution to obviate
such a possibility.”
After this we can consider ourselves
snubbed. It is quite true that the terrihle
accent was imported about 100 odd years
ago from London. It is quite untrue that
every Australian talks like a coster address-
ing his donah. The really well-flavored
Amurkan accent (genuine vintage) is much
worse than our local article. As we hear it
(say in the U.S. play now running at the
Criterion), two-thirds of the conversation
from, the male portion of the cast is as in-
telligible as the swear-words of a Balkan
brigand. Therefore, we don’t take saucv
remarks about Australian accent from a
Murkan critic in a Christian spirit or lying
down. Parish-pump politics drove the Den-
mans out of N. S. Wales, but they’ve had a
fairly joyous time elsewhere; hence the yarn
about “counting the hours” bears a strong
likeness to a plain old lie. “Urgent family
reasons” is a much safer scapegoat. When
Governors are tired of their billets they can
always trot it out. If her Ex. is exhausted
from the alleged hour-counting process (an
old-fashioned sandglass would be helpful)
Lord Denman could, therefore, produce the
before-mentioned animal and ride off behind
it to-morrow.
Mrs. Baronet Ward and daughter Eileen
just looked in on this town on their way
back to the Dominion, via the Cup, after one
of their many flights to London.
The most heavily diamond-spangled wed-
ding of the season packed St. James’s Church
to the front steps the other evening. Miss
Mabel Preston, the only daughter of the
wealthy H. ,T. Preston, who made his pile in
the West., was the bride. The groom, Boy
Moffett King, is a grandson of the late Mrs.
Thorne, of Prestonville, Potts Point. The
bridal finery eclipsed anything the dazed
spectator has seen for some time. The dainty,
fair-haired Mabel was more or less hung with
diamonds —not diamante trimming at ss. 6d.
a yard. Her adoring papa gave her a chain
of diamonds, so long that the girl wore it
round her head as a glittering rope, and tied
it in a knot behind. A shimmer of pale
pink, like the first peep of dawn that meets
the morning papers, blushed under a foam of
lace and tulle. The tail of the bridal robe
was estimated to have been nearly as profuse
as an electioneering politician’s promises.
Bridesmaidens Dorothv King (the lucky
groom’s young sister). W. Gibbs. Neil Holden.
I. Smith and W. Holden were pale pink
and white.echoes of the pale blush-rose bride.
This sensible little person chose her lady-
helps frocks herself, so there was no last-
moment nonsense as to what these damsels
would or would not wear. Laurence Godfrey
Smith —a friend of the Preston familv and
the bride’s musical referee —presided over
the organ’s majestic roll. Mrs. Spencer
Brunton (who is the groom’s aunt) and
his new mother-in-law bustled out of the
church to stage-manage half a mile of
motors, waiting to petrol the wedding party
off to Warrinella, Kirribilli. The honk of
their horns and the mixed smell of bad eggs
and gunpowder which perfumed the air in
their vicinity attracted a crowd of sight-
seers. A groomsman in charge of four or
five pale-pink bridesmaids was walled in
by the most excited end of the street crowd,
and only escaped with his life and his top-
hat and his pink ladies after the white cot-
ton gloves of the Law had described a few
circles in the evening air.
Pretty Georgie Neild. only daughter of the
late Senator J. C. Neild. and of Mrs. Neild
(who still abides at Greycairn, Edgecliff-
road), is engaged. The happy sailor is
Lieutenant Vaughan-Lewis, R.N., of H.M.A.
S. Australia. He’s a tall, blue-eyed mariner
and a senior lootenant. Our Flagship onlv
poked its sharp grey nose into this harbor on
October 4, so Miss Georgie Neild’s black vel-
vet eyes bowled her bluejacket over in record
time.
Artist Mildred Lovett, the brilliant Tas-
manian pupil of Julian Ashton, and latterly
his colleague in the Society of Artists’
studios, is. giving up teaching to wrestle with
housekeeping. The wrestle won’t amount to
much, for the Hobart girl can toss an omel-
ette as easily as she can model a plaster
cast or paint a canvas. The lucky man is
F. L. Paterson, son of late bank-manager
James Paterson, of Sydney, and the wedding
—which will be of the quiet order—occurs
this month. A studio, in which the bride-
elect will devote her spare hours to Art, is
an important feature in the new nest.
The Midsummer Week’s Dream, which
was to have occurred on the Cricket Ground
during the first few days in December, is
postponed till April. Some genius discovered
that this excuse for raising funds to help
pay for Shakspeare’s tercentenary party
would bump into the election uproar. Hence
a resolve to let the air clear before the Bard’s
efhsry has a week out in the open.
Seaside camps for women and children
from the Outback will be pitched near
Deewhy, in the Manly-Narrabeen district.
This scheme, which ought to pay the but-
cher, baker and other urgent morning callers
by next summer, is at present yearning to
take your last shilling. Hon. treasurer
Russell Crane (16 Spring-street) will be
glad to collect all the cash you hope to win
at the Cup, or even the eighteen pence you
meant to put by for your old age.
The Girls’ Exhibition in September brought
about £350 to the Girls’ Realm Guild. This
result was served up with a minor note of
thanksgiving at the recent annual meeting of
the energetic body. As the average Exhibi-
tion in any State, for any cause, is expected
to lose the cash and temper of everyone
concerned in it, the result of the Girls’ dis-
play would be good—but for the exes. The
Town Hall rent for 10 days was a terrifying
±352. A day- and a night-watchman and a
ticket-seller at the entrance had also to be
paid for by the Guild. The kitchen in the
basement is charged for as another extra,
ihis is as logical as letting a house and ask-
ing more for the use of the scullery. The
Town Hall platform, which the
Girls Committee paid to extend, was 10s.
od. a day. Our municipal stepfathers have
the landlord instinct so highly developed
that some day they’ll make a special charge
for the use of the front door.
H. N. Southwell’s friends in Sydney are
organising a recognition of his great services
to every form of charity entertainment for
the last 25 years. As a concert manager he
was reckless of his own time and health
m en i lll7 deservin g cause knocked at his
office door. How many concerts in every
year he managed, just for love, no one is
likely to know. A meeting will take place
on November 13, at the Australia, to decide
what form the testimonial will take.
Judging by an account in the Manchester
Guardian, an Eisteddfod in its native
Wales must be a battle of voices second only
to the fight put up by Wagnerian principals
against the full blast of the orchestra. One
day’s competitions at Abergavenny (which
till August, 1913, hadn’t sheltered an Eis-
teddfod for 50 years) included the part-song
dealing with the Spartan heroes who fell at
the Pass of Thermopylae. The male com-
petitors apparently bawled themselves
hoarse,_ for the critic politely remarked that
to exhibit sheer lung-power seemed to be
their sole aim. Ella Caspers, the Goulburn
girl, sang at these concerts. The Guardian
says: “Miss Ella Caspers, a young contralto
singer of imposing power, and equally fine
presence, did better than any other artist
during the week. Even in such an intimate
son S she carried her audience away.”
As a number of English vocal stars were
engaged, the Australian girl snatched this
praise in the teeth of worthy competition.
Ella, bv the way, is going to tour Australia
next year. She leaves London with her sis-
ter in the Scharnhorst on March 16.
The Fred McQuades are still faithful to
the front stalls at the opera. The Chief
Justice Cullens were camped in the front
row of the d.c. at “Carmen.” Henry Bracy
strolls in from the Criterion to Her Majes-
ty’s o’ nights, to hear the magic music of
the overture under conductor Eckhold. That
Teuton, whose baton is such a force in the
Quinlan Opera Co., has been trimmed up
and partially shayed by some Australian
barber who has no music in his soul. Some
of the original mane is left, but it is only a
sample. Edna Thornton was a Carmen
whose appearance suggested a mixture of
strong working housekeeper (“washing not
objected to, but no shirts”) and hooligan
on the spree. It is the worst histrionic per-
formance the vocally-good artist has given
us.
Eleanora Cisneros’s industrious press
agents keep this office gorged with
information about their singing lady—the
superb Delilah and possibly unrivalled Car-
men, who, on the concert platform, during
her Sydney season, tore her magnificent
voice to tatters by frequently singing flat
or sharp (mostly flat) with all the crescendo
force she could hurl at the Town Hall ceil-
ing. This _ week’s mail has brought only a
postcard, instead of the usual bundle of
papers. . It says, in very spiky waiting, that
Mrs. Cisneros was one of the warblers at
the great Verdi Festival in Parma last
month. She took one of the leading roles in
the opera “Don Carlos.” That she took it
on the fight notes goes without saying.
The cables would have told us of
an uproar in the Italian theatre if there
had been anv falling-off the pitch as we heard
it here in 1912.
Goring Thomas, who used to wear a black
velvet coat and other picturesque garments
in this cito, has a new book, “A Strong
Heart,” on the linotype. An Australian in
England tells me: “Thomas and Valentine
Williams, the clever younv London journa-
list who is engaged to Alice C'awford, went

to actor Tree (who dropped the Beerbohm

Aw,’ said the great man, ‘which Duchess?’
When you ask Londoners if they have seen

we have seen too much of Isaac and his rela-
tives. ”
Artist Griiner claims to have been one of
Julian Ashton’s first pupils when the Julian
academy was in King-street. In those
days George Lambert, Thea Proctor and Sid
Long were absorbing art at the same fount.
Griiner (then a small urchin in knicker-
bockers) used to play the wag from school
and pay stealthy visits to the Julian
academy unbeknown to his mother. While
his maternal parent was arranging a musical
career for her treasure, he was showing his
juvenile ideas of how breakfast crockery
should be sketched to the sympathetic Ash-
ton. Finally a compromise was arranged.
The boy was allowed to take lessons from
the head of the academy, on condition he
pursued the five-finger exercises with all his
might and main in his disengaged moments.
His love for art is incurable. In February
he _ becomes assistant-instructor in the
Society of. Artists’ eyrie, the post formerly
held by Sid Long, and more recently by
Mildred Lovett. Griiner. the well-known
designer in Paris, is a relative of this de-
votee of the brush.
Eda Bennie, a Melbourne girl, sang the
warble, which Offenbach gives the doll
Olympia (“Tales of Hoffmann”) at last
week’s matinee. She is a pupil of Marchesi,
and has a most attractive notion of Miss
Olympia’s waxen charms. By sometimes
forcing a light, flexible soprano, however,
the Victorian singer took an occasional top
note with the sharp squeak of a pencil
scratching a slate.
The house (together with £5OO a year)
which the late J.C.W. left “to the partner
of his early joys and struggles” is one of
the deceased chief’s earlier homes. The gift
was either provided for during Mr. Wil-
liamson’s lifetime or the bequest wasn’t
mentioned in the “official” text of the will,
as supplied to the papers, for pretty obvious
reasons ; yet I don’t suppose the public was
half so gratified over any of his other disposi-
tions. The amazing thing is that any alleged
friend of the late chief’s should have gone
to so much trouble to deny what was so
greatly to his credit.
The R. H. Todds, of this town, are coming
slowly home via Cyprus. There they have a
snack with the bustling little ladv’s brother,
who is military commandant of that isle.
Later they take a glance at the Milky Way
through the huge Kodai Kanal telescope on
the roof of India.
Off to California, to grow fruit, goes Mr.
J. C. Hill, an ex-teaplanter. of Taprobane,
which is now Ceylon. He has been looking
round this continent for the last year or
less. Now he packs up for the orchard state
that you reach by the Golden Gate. Mr
Hill, who is a nephew of that high and
mighty potentate, Gen. Lord Arthur Hill
was a gravel-crusher in the York and Lan-
caster regiment in his green and salad days.
He gave up the glory of serving his country
per boot, as an officer in the above-mentioned
corps, and assiduously planted in n 1
that which cheers but doesn’t
the wander-thirst sent him this way -fi
wander-thirst is a craving that passes S,
resistance. It is even worse thin drink
When it comes upon you the best thing v!,'

carpet bag, cork up your valise, and go reel

Kettlewell. of Sydney, godmother of the
battle League, has been working hard to found a
branch among the more-or-less resident Australians
ln ? r .Y ain .' Miss Schlank and Miss George, of the
Adelaide branch, have conspired with her. and on
October _ they enticed the Australian family to the
High Commissioner’s office. The invitees responded
with such gusto that there wasn’t more than breath-
ing space, even in the corridors, and shillings poured
r„im!c the ,. lnfa " t . league most cheerfully. Captain
Collins, acting-High Commish., took the chair, but
he was in such haste to attend another meeting that
he hustled through the service with frightful celerity
and dismissed the congregation before it had
reached the first lesson. However, the infant was
bom, and will probably cut its teeth on January 26
when we here have a kind of unofficial Wattle Day. ’
A lady who gave her name as Mrs. Maw-
son had a slight tram accident in Melbourne
the other week. A Salvation Army officer
helped her into a cab and passed the inci-
dent on to the local War Cry. The London
edition of the Battle Yell copied the par
which stated (on the authority of the lady
or, rather, on the authority of the gentleman
who helped the lady) that she had come
from England to meet her husband, the
leader of the Antarctic Expedition. Prof.
David, who is regarded as an authority on
everything down South, from boiled blubber
to the domestic affairs of Polar wanderers,
has been wired to from Adelaide re the truth
'or otherwise) of this statement. As Dr
Mawsnn is engaged to a girl at Broken Hill.
Adelaide and Sydney profs, can’t make head
or tail of the incident. I can’t either; but
I really don’t think the Broken Hill girl—
whom Mawson met at Adelaide Uni., of
which she is a graduate—will leave off get-
ting her trousseau ready. The wedding-date
isn t a fixture yet, of course; but it will
probably be within three or four months.

Skipper Doulton, of the inter-State craft
Canberra, which raced the new Katoomba
to Melbourne and won by a wave’s length,
is an old Bedford (Eng.) Grammar School
boy- This is possibly one of the reasons
why the jovial Doulton likes to be ahead of
competition. Bedford Grammar School is,
or used to be, a noted caning ground for
young gentlemen who hoped to get
in front of everything their own size.
Col. „ Fred Burnaby, the “dashing sab-
reur, as a fond biographer calls him, who
made the famous ride to Khiva, is always
spoken of as one of the star pupils. The
grateful manufacturers of certain pills pro-
bably remember him as their best adver-
tiser. Burnaby didn’t exactly say he never
would have .ridden to Khiva if he hadn’t
swallowed, a consignment of those pellets;
but he said nearly everything else in their
favor.

JUST PUBLISHED—
“Bully Hayes: Buccaneer”
and Other Stories
By LOUIS BECKE.
(Author of “ My Native Wife,” “ By Reef and Palm,” etc., etc.)
With Portrait, and Illustrations by Norman Lindsay.
Price, l/« ; Post Free, 1/1.
The name of Louis Becke is sufficient guarantee that the writing is strong, dramatic and
interesting, and no man knew “the Islands” better, or had more thrilling experiences there, than
this well-known author. Having served under the famous “Bully Hayes,” his personal anecdotes
anent that worthy are doubly valuable, whilst his other stories are amongst the best, as they are
the last, that Becke ever penned. The Author’s photograph, specially prepared for this work, is
characteristic and lifelike, and is a memento well worth possessing.
A racy sketch of the author’s chequered career forms a fitting introduction to his final effort.
Sodger Sandy’s Bairn.
Bv A. BATHGATE.
With six Illustrations by Harry Julius, and a cover design by Percy Spence.
Price, 1/-; Post Free, 1/1.
Mr. Bathgate is new to Australian readers, which is unfortunate for both author and readers,
for “Sodger Sandy’s Bairn” displays such an amount of ability that it seems a pity he did not ven-
ture sooner. He gives us some splendid pgn-pictures of Scotch and colonial types, describes the
early gold rushes and their attendant excitement with wonderful vividity, charms us with his insight
into human nature, possesses a command of the Scotch vernacular that reminds one of J. M. Barrie
or lan Maclaren, whilst his fund of pawky humor is rare and refreshing. Scotch people especially
will welcome this book and want to know more of its author.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 3, 1913.
.My Dear Yarrie, —
Melbourne was quite happy on Derby
morning, and it wasn’t entirely due to the
line weather. Chronic racegoers always
start the chief horse carnival like the op-
timists they are. The tips are all dead
certainties, and there’s money enough to
go on with; also, the bill hasn’t yet come
in for the wife’s sumptuous gear. The
motors whirred down Oollins-street to the
cafes, and the chauffeurs abandoned the
steering wheels and hurried in to get ham-
pers carefully ordered a week in advance.
Early women-racegoers and a few men fore-
gathered at morning tea, while business hus-
bands scrambled through urgent affairs with
the race glasses ready on the desk. Bright
sunshine and a harmless baby-breeze brought
out the dresses, till the grey streets glowed
like a garden. Heaven was hurriedly
thanked for its perfect weather arrange-
ments between sips of tea.
G.-G. Denman was too keen on the racing
to wait while his lady finished her toilette.
He arrived on the course early, and
promptly started the business of the day.
in the afternoon, when the rest of the house-
hold arrived in state, his Ex. was on hand,
and gravely took part in his programmed
arrival-turn. Up to that Anthemed mo-
ment he had not been present. The open
carriages rolled majestically up the course,
and the only variation from the pomp of
Cup Day was that the postilions were not
powdered. They weren’t even shot. The
Denman dame wore a startling modern edi-
tion of Joseph's coat. It was pink-purple,
white and turquoise-blue, shot with green.
The lady inside the garment drooped or
flopped in the fashionable manner. Rear-
Admiral Patey’s nose was surrounded with
smiles, and he wore a buttonhole of corn-
flowers. The Barttelot dame added to her
height by a large green windmill of tulle in
her hat. Mrs. Guise was merely the ad-
vance agent of her own ostrich feathers.
One of the visitors to the vice-regal box was
wrapped in a purple chiffon cloak. Miss
Knatchbull-Hugessen wore, her ridiculous
cretonne chair-cover coat and a satisfied
smile. The John Maddens helped to fill the
official pen. Help Burnham, of the Den-
man’s staff, brought fiancee Maggie Niall
along to have a word with the house party.
Maggie, who is just back from her Japan
tour, wore purple and smiles.
Leoni Peterson-Mackworth looked like a
nice little lump of cocoanut ice, as she tried
to keep step with her tall, military hus-
band. Mrs. Dorothy Vane Menzies was
doomed to walk the lawn all day. The
dressmaker had put a small posy of flowers
on her dress, and if she sat down that posy
would be flattened. As the dress came from
Paris, to crush the posy or alter its location
would have been plain sacrilege. Mrs. Den-
ison, from Sydney, sailed the lawn in the
expensive simplicity of a white cr§pe gown.
Miss Milne stepped with her in black. Mrs.
Claude Reading looked adorable in a mole-
colored chiffon coat and a grey frock that
gave no sign of how she had got into it. It
looked like a case of burglary. Mrs. Wills-
Alien had a batch of pretty Sydney girls
with her; they resembled a bundle of fluffy
kittens, and Harry Chisholm’s young
daughter Sheila heightened the effect by
wearing a large pink bow. Mrs. Stephen
Brunton steered a very stylish team of
daughters and nieces. The Sydney women’s
duds are really wonderful, and rouse a chorus
of envy and helpless rage wherever they are
seen.
Mrs. Bowes Kelly paraded in black
and white, and Mrs. Miller ruffled her neck
feathers over blue. Mrs. Geoff. Syme posed
near the terrace geraniums in a black chif-
fon race coat, and sister Mrs. Thwaites
looked a day dream in pink. Secretary
Byron Moore supported his white high hat
and practised the art of conversation in the
Governor’s crate. There was such a rush
of exhibits in that section that the kitchen
chairs had to be requisitioned. Mrs. Willie
Kerr, from Sydney, arrived this year with a
license to drive her own car. Last year a
hired machine broke down and stranded her
in the waste. She eventually arrived at
Flemington wuth her party on a motor-lorry.
Hay, of Manara station, has come back as
a member of the V.R.C. after 20 years’
absence. He was always a keen racing
man, but for years he deserted Flemington
and only raced his horses at country meet-
ings. Aggie Thorne (Mrs. Deery) hailed
Melbourne friends in a blue dress which
matched her eyes. Mrs. Harry Kirkby
looked like an animated banana in yellow
from feather-ends to show-tips. Many Dago
brigands in charge of fruit-stalls looked at
her admiringly. Dr. and Mrs. O’Gorman
Hughes and the Thomas Hughes pair were
present. (They were there for the races, so I
don’t mention their clothes.) Mrs. Glenloth
Peter looked cool, bound stylishly in white
cloth.
As usual, the Rupert Clarke lady provided
the dress convulsion of the day. Her black,
clingsome skirt was cut to represent trouser-
ettes without actually being them, and round
her waist w T as an absurd little plush crino-
line which did not reach to her knees. It
was wired out in a low-comedy way, and
bounced gently at every step. Rupert Clarke
lagged behind during the promenade.
A few lunch parties found plenty of room
to eat under the vines. Derby Day is not
the best for lunch parties. The pea-
cocks do not settle down to eat until the
tine feathers have been shown on the lawn
after two o’clock, in the week the
clatter in the feeding-paddock is deafening,
and the tangle of tables, top hats and guests
bewildering. The Derby gazers made up
parties on the course for the various theatres,
and the Savoy hummed at supper time.
The Naval and Military Ball happened on
Derby eve, as usual. Red roses on blue
strings marked off the dancing area, and
flags and electrics the promenade. Nine
o’clock was the appointed dancing hour. The
vice-regals burst on the scene an hour later.
The advance toot of their motors simply
paralysed the preliminary waltz. The alleged
revellers ignored the pleading dance-music
and swarmed round the entrance, or scuttled
upstairs to the balcony. All the field-mar-
shals present formed a square close to the
entrance, and the band blew a few bars of
the Anthem. A dancing-master backed like
a well-trained horse, and cleared a path to
the dais, where tall palms stood to attention
in blue draperies. The G.-G., heavily deco-
rated with orders and things like that,
walked with Colonel Burston’s wife. The
Denman dame was dressed in a little pink
brocade and a peck of diamonds. John
Madden’s wife put seven diamond stars in
her hair, and wore black. Daughter Lesley
was conspicuously absent. Mrs. Hennessy
wore a livid shade of mauve under a lace
overdress. Mrs. Burston’s black clingsome
lace w T as what Grandma would call “ele-
gant.” Rear-Admiral Patey effaced himself
as much as possible in the procession, but
kept station in the lancer evolutions. Gen-
eral Gordon had a perfectly lovely time
dancing Two Steps. Admiring girls say
there' would be no wallflowers at all if
every man did his dancing duty like the
General. Talking of wallflowers, the posi-
tion is really desperate when the wealthiest
wool-maiden in Victoria has to sit lonely in
a corner while half a dozen young naval men
yawn in a bored way under cover of the
palms. Hasn’t the Navy got a social intel-
ligence department? Didn’t they know she
was rich?
Mrs. Isaacs wore a gold lace panel on
her gown and brought along clever daughter
Nancy, who cheerfully admitted that she had
only come for the supper. Mrs. Miller, mag-
nificently trimmed with opals, brought niece
Belle White, who wore her own magnificent
red hair. Mrs. Leslie Stuart was a quiet pic-
ture in pastel tints. Mrs. Moss wore purple,
with a blue sash and red roses. Mrs. Shel-
ton, an imposing matron, brought her boys
and saw that they danced. Dave Lilley,
who has a classic profile and is rated
as an eligible, danced as if he were being
paid an insufficient wage. Warriors of all
ranks and shapes cavorted for the honor of
the army. Tettie Appleton, daughter of an
old officer and one of the cheeriest partners
in the room, wore black ; while the Fairbairn
daughter, in several colored tulles, looked dis-
contented. One of Frank Madden’s daughters
wore pantaloons of blue and gold and tried
to disguise them with a lemon-tinted train.
Mrs. Jack Winter Irving dazzled in bide and
silver, and a bridesmaid from the latest
Sydney wedding was noticeable in a scant
black dress.
The gold-laced hosts of the cruiser En-
counter have sent out cards notifying guests
that their marine revel, alongside the Town
Pier, at Port Melbourne, has been postponed
to Mondav, November 10. The evening first
selected (November 7) turned out to be the
dancing property of the august Australian
Club, so the Encounter men had to shift
moorings.
Last week G.-G. Denman and his dame
found a spare hour in the engagement ledger
and filled it with a McCormack concert.
They arrived very late; but the watchful
pianist had kept some of the Anthem up his
sleeve and the audience was duly warned of
the vice-regal approach. Her Ex. wore a
weird grey cloak, which was excitedly
conned, whereas Miss Knatchbull-Hugessen
paraded in garments that attracted no notice
whatever. Nevill and the
round-eyed Captain Pollock hovered de-
votedly about the party. Miss Soprano
Huxley was in (and out) of a black-and-
white gown, decorated behind with an im-
mense green obi bow. By the way, Mrs.
Ted Tait, a fluffy circumstance in creamy
fixings, partied with some friends who had
farewelled her and husband Ted with ex-
pensive and indigestible food, by way of
preparing them for the trip to Sydney.
G.-G. Denman has been re-elected presi-
dent of the Melbourne Polo Club. The polo-
ists are having a preliminary dust-up this
week at Kooyong.
Last Friday the University Students
packed the new Melba Hall to listen to and
look at George Reid. The Vice-Chancellor
took the chair and still had it when the
entertainment ended. George isn’t likely to
play a return season. The boys say that
he is satisfactory as far as he goes, but
why doesn’t he go farther and dance?
Mrs. Geoffrey Syme gave an afternoon
tea-party to about 200 people on Thursday
last to introduce sister Margery, otherwise
Mrs. Thwaites, a pretty young matron from
England. The weather broke down badly,
but the Syme house is big and cosy, and the
guests took refuge in its many corners. Mrs.
Geoff, wore a Fortuni frock —a stencilled
blue-and-gold thing wrapped in a grey chif-
fon sheath. Mrs. Thwaites had lace tuckers
in the neck and sleeves of her sky-blue crepe
dress. Mrs. David Syme looked brisk and
youthful in black. All the pretty girls and
some others belonging to the river suburb
came along in muslin frocks; and shed fur
and rain coats in the hall.
The Grand Hotel held whet was officially
described as an “Arrival Tea ” the day be-
fore the Derby. About a hundred hostesses
booked tables, and, in addition, the big din-
ing-room had all the round tables out. A
new niece has come across from Westralia
to share the fun with the adopted Bigjohn
Forrest daughter, and with their help the
dame entertained a big party, which included
tenor McCormack’s Italian-looking wife.
Mrs. Sideton entertained Mrs. George Reid
and daughter Thelma. Well-dressed unem-
ployed were present in force, and some of
them listened to the musical programme.
Mrs. Isaacs, assisted by daughter Nancy,
hostessed at Cliveden Mansions last Thurs-
day afternoon. Five hundred guests came
along and showed by their presence what a
lot of spare time there is in the country.
Miss Borrough married the immensely tall
lawyer Kiddle on Thursday last. It was a
sedate wedding without any petrol fumes,
old-fashioned carriage-and-horses being uti-
lised for transportation purposes. Mr.
Kiddle has trained his girl well as a help-
mate. Since the engagement she has been
doing the extra-special typewriting in his
office, and means to persevere with the key-
tapping.
From London “Rosna” drops me a line
to say that the Hon. John Boyle, who is
booked for marriage with Ethel, daughter
of Justice Hodges, of Melbourne, is a mem-
ber of the Royal Flying Corps, aviation be-
ing the one trade in which the British peer-
age’s young man may indulge without risk-
ing anything worse than a broken neck.
The same mercurial colleague tells me of
the marriage in Britain of Phyllis Jameson,
granddaughter of the late Justice Hensman,
of Westralia, and daughter of a former Com-
missioner for Lands in the same sandy
State. The bridegroom was Lionel Haver-
croft Green, and the ceremony was a very
frilly one, with a silver and brocade bride
and six quaint maidens in pink embroidered
with caps of Irish lace.
Doris Robb is to marry Dr. Rupert
Downes, on Thursday, the 20th. The bride-
groom has the rank of Major, so the wed-
ding should be brightened with much gold
lace.
Arthur, son of the Robert Harpers, is to
be married next month. Miss Barton, a
sister of Mrs. Gordon Knox, is the girl who
is collecting the trousseau, and all Beacons-
field and Berwick are agog. However,
Prahran refuses to be convulsed, and the
glacial calm of Collingwood is refreshing to
witness.
The new Melba Hall, which is to shelter
the University Conservatorium people, was
opened the other Wednesday by G.-G. Den-
man. The Denman dame, in blue, with
cream punctuations and a black tulle head-
piece brought along most of her house party.
At the end of the programme Chamberlain
Nevill dived into the audience and emerged
leading the Peterson daughter Leoni and
her bridegroom, Captain Mackworth. After
which Leoni sat with the Denman lady, who
made a flattering fuss of her.
George Armstrong, son of the songful
Melba, arrived with his second wife the
other day. After the Cup excitements, the
pair will retire to Coombe Cottage, Melba’s
comfortable Lilydale house.
There is serious news of the health of
Dr. Clendinnen, the X-ray specialist who
went to London for the big Medical Con-
gress. He underwent an operation, and the
latest bulletins report a bad collapse.
Mrs. Davey, Melbourne’s ex-Mayoress, is
reported to be on the road to recovery again.
The lady has undergone two operations.
The grateful inter-State manufacturer
delegates, who have had Mrs. Ivy-Deakin-
Brookes as chief hostess, presented that lady
with a picture by Australian artist Pen
Boyd. This looks like an inspiration ; most
people for their kindnesses have to suffer
marble clocks.
Henry Lingham threatens to give a
“unique lecture” at the Austral Salon on
Wednesday, the 19th. Lingham, who bursts
into occasional verse on the subjects near-
est his heart (naughty man!), proposes to
preach “The Religion of Beauty,” with all
the fervor of an ancient Greek. He has the
Elevation of Humanity in view, and he
wants the Glory that was Greece to be ours.
Also at the Salon the Shakspearians will
assemble on Friday this week. Phoebe Bar-
nett and Claude Hall are billed to recite a

Like It. Frank Russell, the hon *

The Melbourne friends of Kathleen Wat-
son Deardon —now domiciled in Queensland
where the timber comes from—are waiting
impatiently for her “Later Litanies of Life"
Lothian will publish it in the New Year \
recent fourth edition of Kathleen Watson’s
original English Litanies, the sweetly senti-
mental imaginings of her young spinster
days,_ has whetted one’s appetite for the
promised Litanies up-to-date. These dreams
of her Australian married life are pretti-
sure to satisfy the eternal feminine craving
for “a good cry,” the authoress being, in
fact, a buoyantly humorous matron whose
fancy turns naturally to thoughts of grief
and woe.
The returned tripper who unpacks a trunk
of imported clothes won at the European
bargain counters and refers slightingly to
local shops as out of style and unreasonably
dear may note that the Grey-Egerton ladv
actually sends to her native land for certain
chiffons, which, she says, are not procurable
in London at the same price. I love to hear
of these little economies amongst the rich!
Widow otaughton’s youngest daughter and
the youngest daughter’s husband (his name
is Eric Byron Moore ) are preparing for
another trip to San Francisco.
A returned squatter-woman thought she
was making rather a hit when she bailed
up Mrs. Pearson with the remark, “I sup-
pose this seems nothing to you after the
Thames Henley?” She was fearfully aston-
ished, though, when the visitor murmured.
“By no means; this is the first Henley I
have seen.”
. The latest importation for the dancing
girl is the Subway Glide. Several Society
cliques are practising it in strict seclusion.
It is said to be more audacious than any-
thing yet seen here. If that is the case, it
will have to be extensively sub-edited before
it gets on to many dance programmes-
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
On Monday' there was a meeting at Government
House of those interested in the big fair which is
being organised with the object of raising monev to
enlarge the Royal Institution for the Blind. A'full
house eventuated—but, as I said, the meeting was
at Government House. People manage to work in
a vice-regal atmosphere who never toiled before in
their lives.
The relatives of a returning widower of means are
contradicting the rumors of his engagement with
frenetic energy. Suffragism hath no fury like the
relatives of an affluent widower in the presence of
one who speaks of his (the widower’s) approaching
nuptials as though the tragedy were a mere trifle.
Dr. Kenneth Fry, the one-time Rhodes scholar,
intends to erect his professional sign-post in this
township. He held a Government billet in the Ter-
ritory for some time, but had a species of argument
or row with the haughty folks who run that pretty
little place, and. now all is over between him and
them and it.
Miss Dora Beresford is here on a visit from Maori-
land. She is staying with the Alec McCullochs in
North Adelaide. They, by the way, have just heard
of the death in England of Lance McCulloch, as the
result of a shooting accident. He was the only son
of the Duncan McCullochs, who owned Eden Park,
near Mount Barker, some years ago. His death
means that there is no male representative of the
younger generation of the family.
The Pittendrighs gave a “Dandy” party on the
opening night of that combination; also a supper
at Jackman’s Caf<s. And yet there are miserable
ones who hint that we don’t see life here, as Paris
and Berlin understand the term.
As a matter of fact, the place is fairly seething
with excitement. Thus on Sunday morning there
was a record thunderstorm—such a manifestation as
no city on earth could better. The streets ran with
water —which is so much pleasanter than the blood
which appears to be a feature of the older civilisa-
tions from time to time. Homing church-goers
waded to their midday meal ankle deep—occasion-
ally knee deep—in the fluid. Others, less resolute,
remained penned in graveyards and the like till the
waters subsided—the tram service looking dolefully
over the fence the while. One city church leaked so
that the congregation had to hoist its collective
gamp and listen to the sermon through the rents.
Miss Emily Reynell is off to England in December.
I expect she reckons that even London can’t show
anything worse in the climatic line than we went
through on that awful Sabbath.
A gross and a half of bowling clubs were opened
on Saturday. At Geelong, Victoria Drive, in the
South Park lands and elsewhere tea, bunting and
whisky were flying like monoplanes.
The Misses Vincent were given a farewell tea at
Manoah, the J. H. Symons’ picturesque house at
Upper Sturt, the other day. They are off to England.
Sincerely yours.
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
SUSANNAH AND THE ELDERS.—
LATEST VERSION.
On June 30, 1912, there were 2054 in-
dented discolored seamen engaged in the pearling
industry at Broome; for the year ended June 30,
1913, there had been added an excess of 69 arrivals
j . T . ~ ,
over departures. Japanese were in the vast major-
ity, Malays next, then Javanese, Filipinos, Chinese
and a few remnants. Of the crowd, 34 died in the
12 months to the end of last June, 11 from diver's
paralysis (eight of them Japs), three from cardiac
failure due to spinal hemorrhage, and the rest from
a variety of causes, including murder. In the list
of offences of this motley crew were 10 charges of
being prohibited immigrants. In eight cases the
accused were Japanese, the other two being Malays,
Four were remanded, and in the case of each of the
other six the charge was withdrawn. Which means
that for 12 months the responsible department made
six failures and four possible failures out of' 10
tries. Anybody who can do worse than that is
entitled to unfavorable consideration.
“Marloo” : Bentley joined the force before
the education test was strict. He was, for all that,
a great stickler for formality, and believed in per-
forming his duties on time and in order. One day
when Mrs. Bentley was absent in town, Bentley
arrived at headquarters with a stranger, who had
been hurling round sulphurous language and chal-
lenges to fight, and had refused to close down. When
Bentley had reduced the prisoner to a reasonable
mood, the missus, who performed the clerical duties,
was still absent; so he requested the gentleman to
write his name and certain particulars in the charge
book. The gentleman told him to have one himself.
Bentley swore and growled a while, then grabbed the
pen. “What’s yer name?” he asked. “Bertram
Scarborough,” lied the gentleman, whose name was
Harris. “Spell it over slow,” commanded Bentley.
“Get work,” said the false Bertram. Bentley con-
sidered, put the pen to the paper, and wrote t-o-m
j-o-n-e-s, and entered it in the book. “Where do ye
come from, Jones?” “Collarenebri,” stated Jones.
Bentley’s request to “spell it” was met with a fur-
ther insulting remark. “M-o-r-e-e,” he droned, while
he wrote. “What have ye with ye, Jones?” “A
portmanteau,” answered Jones. “B-a-g,” wrote
Bentley. Both Harris and the charge-book failed as
guides to the orthography of “riotous behavior,” so
it had to be “D-r-u-n-k.” Then, seething, the Law
grabbed the false Bertram by two hind portions, and,
with a push and a kick, sent him hurtling into the
cell. “Be Heavens, Jones,” said Bentley, “it’s a
lucky man ye are that the missus is away.” And
Harris wonders to this day what Bentley’s wife had
to do with the case.
* * * *
Gordon G.” : I’m into this Multi Synony-
mous Word Competition, started by “The Immature
Male’ (B. 25/9/’l 3). He went on “drunk.” Igo
on “dead.’ Let the cadaver start on his mad career:
“Died, crossed the bar, breathed his last, beered his
last, passed in the checks, thrown a seven, kicked
the bucket, gone on the last journey, passed on to
the undiscovered country, hopped the twig, laid
down his knife and fork, blued it, skied it, pegged
out, crossed Jordan, gone to Heaven, gone to the
Jasper Sea, snuffed, passed on to a better land,
returned to dust, at the cold-meat picnic grounds,
thrown in his alley, made his final exit, passed into the
valley of the shadow, turned up his toes, passed into
the Great Beyond, last-gasped, departed from us dear
brethren, shot his bolt, now in Paradise, with the
angels, gone to sleep, closed his eyes, up the spout,
crossed the Styx, popped off, on the Stygian shore,
gave up the ghost, paid his debt to Nature, gone the
way of all flesh, snuffed his candle, came to an un-
timely end, numbered with the slain, gone to his
last home, with the great majority, dead as a door-
nail, dead as mutton, dead as a herring, dead as
nets, launched into Eternity, gathered to his fathers,
in Davy Jones’s locker, in the grave, blown out,
joined the innumerable caravan, perished, become
one of the immortals, fallen by the wayside, taken
wing,” etc. And, finally, there is the dullest and
most sordid of all: “Sacred to the memory of
Thomas Smith.”
