“TiakiThe get-rich-hurriedly Yankee is
great on appointing “representatives.” A friend of
mine, in reply to a communication from Maoriland,
received two sheets of type-written matter appoint-
ing him a certain firm’s representative for the whole
of Maoriland. They must be people of simple faith,
these wooden nutmeggers, as per
their note. “Our opinion of your
ability and standing is favorable,
and we believe that with our new
and comprehensive system of con-
structing our ‘road force’ by mail
we can give you all the necessary
information, so you will make a
success of the work from the
start,” etc., etc. All this faith in
a man that they didn’t know
from a bag of sand. He had
authority to go to every town,
hamlet and Maori pa in the
Dominion and appoint sub-agents
all over the face of the cow-juice
country. He was not to sell any
goods, but to appoint others to
do so, for which he was to re-
ceive the sum of 20 dollars
per week, with a com. of 15 per-
cent. on all goods sold (payable
every four months), and hotel,
train, car, baggage and other ex-
penses. It seems all right and
rosy up to here, but there is one
disappointing condition. The ap
plicant must send 10 dollars foi
a sample outfit. It seems a pity
that so much faith should fail
short of the Complete Ham by a trumpery 10
dollars.
* * * •
“Trotter”: The Melbourne Zoo is just
reinforced with kangaroo. Falkiner’s Moonbria,
Deniliquin, is sanctuary for birds and animals; and
varieties elsewhere becoming extinct are there found
in abundance. A kangaroo drive, however, became
necessary. Director Le Souef, of Melb. Zoo, joined
in. A wire-netting yard had been constructed on
the Yanko boundary fence, with a wing about half a
mile long for yarding. Horsemen co-operated with
four parties in motor-cars. About 500 kangaroo were
sighted, but most of them broke away. Fourteen
were taken alive, and of these nine reached Melb.
Zoo safely. They were of the red variety. One old
man, of the Macropus Rufus sort, is the largest ever
seen by Overseer Wilkie. He is 7ft. high, with
enormous tail and wonderfully muscular shoulders.
In the next enclosure is one of the Macropus Qigan-
teus. who will never equal tin's rufus. The Gigan
tens is a truer descendant of the fossil kangaroo,
modelled by Le Souef as about 15ft. high.
“Flewis” : I’ve been in the Federal Capi-
tal Territory (or, at least, the territory where the
alleged capital is to perhaps be) for three months,
and it has been blowing a 20-knot gale during that
time. I used to reckon that Wellington (M.L.) could
beat anything at blowing bar King O’Malley, but
Canberra beats them both. As for that lake the
U.S. expert figures on making, on which the youth
and beauty of the capital will go a-boating of a sum-
mer’s night, just listen to this little extract from an
official description of part of the Molonglo. “The
river bed here is fairly wide, of a sandy and gravelly
nature, but the stream is narrow and shallow, and
stock can cross almost anywhere.”
COMBA T UNDER PARLIAMENTARY RULES.
SMITHO (shaping): "Put ’em up, you scoundrel, an’ I’ll smash yer Pad.”
JONSEY (a confirmed Parliamentary galleryite, who thinks he sees an honorable way out
of a difficult situation): "Say, Smitho, couldn't—couldn't we pair in this division?”
“Boswell” records the prattle of the
policeman’s widow: —
“Ah, poor Brian was a policeman—he went 12
months ago, God rest his soul! I could tell you
queer things about some people in this district. It’s
the policeman that knows everything, and what the
policeman knows his wife knows —and a bit more,
maybe. Only for their wives half of them would
never get a stripe at all. You know Sergeant
O’Hehir. You don’t know him? Why, I saw him
this morning in the street with a tweed suit on and
a straw hat. And Mick Donohue? He had his half-
day off yesterday. Geo. Briggs is a senior now.
Well! Well! If you didn’t know Mick I thought
everybody' knew George. He gets his leave next
month. I was up at the barracks to-day, and Jim
Barlow’s going to take me to the circus to-night.
You don’t know Jim? Why, I thought everybody
knew him. Him, and Paddy Flannagan—what!
Never heard of Flannagan! Why, everybody talks
about him. Big Tom, as they call Maguire, was in
plain clothes this morning. He’s got a case on —-a
policeman’s wife knows these things by instinct.
Most women know nothing at all. Of course
Maguire hasn’t been in uniform as long as Phil
Smith and Tim Heggartv. They’re both due for
their two stripes. And it was Tim’s wife that put
him on to the case that got him the second one, as
I happen to know. What, you never heard of Heg-
gartv ! Why. Tim Heggarty’’
And, after half an hour of this, you realise that
half the world doesn’t know how big the men are
who arrest the other half of it for cow-stealing or
“no visible beans of support.”

“Ask Tredennick,” said the confident man
with the Northern mine to sell—“ask Treden-
nick: he worked it once. He’ll give you the
strength of it.” So the city man asked Tredennick
for the strength of it, and this is the report he
got:—
You ask about the Buckley’s Chance mine. It was
a cow. I worked it on tribute before the Synagogue
took possession, and made a bare existence, without
trousers. One year we ran the little furnace a whole
fortnight. Very hard countrv. and very little ore,
but sometimes a small bunch of beautiful zinc or
iron. You can drive 10ft. or 100 ft. without seeing
anything. It’s a beautiful place for driving—you
don’t meet with any interruptions. They have had
a man keeping the bait sweet for a bite; and because
I don’t want to spoil sport, I have much pleasure in
recommending it to you, if you don’t mind being
the fish.

“Celia Lloyd”: When “9-3-11” (B.
4/9/’l3) gets fresh about paspalum being a pest
and a curse on the North Coast (N.S.W.) dairy
farms, it’s reasonable to suspect he’s a Woolloomooloo
farmer. There is no other known feed in Australia
that will carry cattle and return butter-fat like this
weed. Give it a temperate climate with a good rain-
fall, such as they have in the vicinity mentioned, and
it will put £ 6 per year on the right side of the pass-
book for every acre. I have just concluded some
extensive wandering through the North Coast cows
and cockies, and, of the only two good things I
found on the trip, paspalum was one. Like any
other heavy grass, such as buffalo and couch, if con-
tinually eaten off it will mat at the roots, but where
you find the practical dairy farmer (not mere milkers
and turners of separators) who ploughs his paddocks
every three or even four years, you will find pas-
palum in the shape of the very best cow-feed, twelve
inches high, and good from the point nearest Heaven
to the extremity nearest the other place.
* * * *
“The Immature Male”: I have always
cherished a vague idea that when a Poet had closed
up his verse-factory for the day, and had wended hit
way homeward, and had groomed and littered down
his Pegasus, and partaken of a swig of Nectar —
that he invariably spent his leisure hours wandering
around loose amongst fields of Asphodel, or Amar-
anth, gazing sadly at the sunset. But after reading
an account of a recent coursing match, this seems
entirely erroneous. Poets appear to have given up
sadly mooning; for it looks as if they have taken to
working overtime, and have all gone in for inventing
sonorous-sounding dog-names. Herewith a list taken
from one recent coursing event: Vanrov;
Larille; Nanette; Happy Bride; La Valerie; Be-
loved ; Queen of the Forest; Fairy Poem; Lady
Haste; Lawn Bee; Flying Arrow; Le Roy; Rose
Queen; Tisiphone; Kilsheelan; Pomona; White
Pigeon; and Hidden Tears. Hidden Tears as a
hound-label is almost epic! Possibly these names
are all the work of one Sonneteer —some tame Poet-
aster, who is kept chained up on the premises of the
Coursing Club.

THE GOOD BROTHER WHO IS ALSO AMUSING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bishop of Bathurst (Dr. Long) urged the
claims of Church work in the West. People were in
the habit of saying that Bathurst was a civilised
place, where people lived in harmony. But though
he had his letters sent to Bathurst, he was not often
there, for his diocese embraced 83,000 square miles.
Coming along a bush track the other day he had met
a team of shearers, and one of them had said, “Them
bush parsons are the best parsons I have ever struck,
and we would be glad, Bishop, if you would'send us
more of them.”
Rev. E. C. Kempe (the principal) said one of the
papers had remarked that the Bush Brotherhood was
monastic, but if some people were sometimes to pass
the home at Dubbo and hear them singing, “Have a
Banana,” and other comic ditties, they would not
think there was much monasticism about them.
—Sydney D.T.
A pair of gaiters soon are lost
Upon the Narrow Track ;
They travel at tremendous cost,
And seldom go Outback.
They twinkle down the well-kept street;
They speed across the plain—-
Not on two large and aching feet,
Biit in a railway train.
The Bishop by the city stands
Where soft-lapped Comfort smiles;
He has beneath his white, ringed hands
Some eighty thousand miles.
Within his great soul-paddock crawl
The teams of godless folk:
Lone swagmen tramp, and bushmen bawl,
And Life’s a wide-spaced joke.
His gay parishioners, of course,
Forget the Narrow Way,
Until a Brother on a horse
Drops in to sing and pray.
He is the traveller for the stuff
The Bishop vends in town,
And travel-wise he knows enough
To make his goods go down.
He shows his mind is broad with lays
By some good men decried ;
He does not harp on Herod’s ways,
But does a Gaby Glide.
He Turkey Trots Theology,
And Tangoes for the poor
Bush folk, who very little see
And much have to endure.
What wonder then that teamsters beg
The Bishop by the way
To send him round to shake a leg
And sing another day?
N.S.W.

“(xil Gunnia” : The finest sport in the
North is a 'gator hunt as organised by Binghi. I
was fortunate to witness one the other day while
travelling the Gulf rivers, where this “game" is
very plentiful. In fact, some of the small and little-
known tidal creeks and rivers are alive with them
at the point where the fresh water commingles with
the salt. It was on the Staaten, north from Nor-
manton, we had our bit of excitement. Six of the
Maitakendi tribe’s old bucks directed affairs, and in
their wake were some 20 followers—young bucks,
gins and kids. The gins and kids were armed with
waddies and stones, the others with spears. The
plan of campaign is such cases is as follows. First
of all, the water is poisoned with jinbul wood. This
quickly turns up the small aquatic fry, and the
alligators find it so little to their liking that they
migrate. They have to travel overland, as they are
headed if they try to escape up or down stream. This
is Binghi’s chance. When a number have been turned
inland the blacks surround them. The bucks with
their spears prod and tickle the vulnerable undersides
of the great lizards, while the camp followers blind
them with sand when they try to escape. Verv few
of the ’gators do escape. After half an hour of this
warfare the lizards are exhausted ; but while it lasts
it is very thrilling, for with Binghi's yells are min-
gled the yapping and snarling of the ’gators. Then
belts are unloosened and lips smack in anticipation.
Very little of the meat is eaten —only the sirloin
and one or two of the choicest parts. The great
delicacy is the “fry or the embryo egg pouches of
the female ’gator. There is keen rivalry for this
titbit. At the hunt on the Staaten River 10 ’gators
were bagged; and after the titbits were taken the
carcases were skinned and the skins got ready for
drying. Later on, Binghi will swap them at the
stations for supplies of ’bacca and sugar.

"Leo Lear” : Reckoned by self and mate
that there are more kinds of bells in the Australian
bush and plains than in any other country on earth.
There are counties* cow-bells, horse-bells” bull-bells,
camel-bells, sheep-bells, goat-bells, mule-bells, and
bird-bells. In newly-settled country, before fences
are run up, bells are a blessing to the stock hunter.
But they can be overdone. With horses, one bell on
a small mob is all that is necessary; the others
usually stick to the bell-horse. There” is an art in
knowing what beast to bell. Some can walk miles
and graze half the week without giving their alarm
more than an occasional gentle ring. Again, there
is a peculiarity about the sound of some bells in the
bush ; there are those that are almost unbearable
when close at hand, but get a mile or so off, and
the clear ringing clangs out as plainly as the Last
Gong. There is a heap in the selection of bells. An
old cross-cut saw, put together-by a good-tradesman—
but it needs a good one—and fitted-with • a suitable
knocker, makes one of the best stock bells I know
of. And for a camp bell, to rouse out
sleepers in the morning, there is nothing
more resounding or melodious than three dif-
ferent lengths of railroad iron hung on wire, and
hit, banged or clouted with a cold chisel. Stock
bells have a great attraction for emu. The long-
nccked, inquisitive birds that happen to be within
sound of a bell, especially if they are not accustomed
A BILLYGOAT
FLAT MORSEL.
FORMER IDNTITY
(after years of ab-
sence): "Well,
that's the first flock
of goats I’ve seen since I was a kid.”
to hearing it, will rally round the ringer like a
scattered congregation on a dry Sunday. And if
the horses on which the bells may be, are themselves
unaccustomed to emu, there is a fine stampede, mak-
ing the birds more inquisitive than ever. Result,
another horse bolt, followed by another emu stalk,
ad infinitum Hunting over miles of country for
wandering stock with bells on is wearv work. * They
may be camped close at hand, and if the flies happen
to be off duty, no ring comes from the bell till the
searcher is out of earshot. Besides, a man who is
out all day, stopping frequently to listen for the
sound of his own bell (for he knows it apart from
dozens of others), at last gets bells on the brain,
and hears them from every creek and spur. Then
he bolts for home, and finds the missing animals
feeding alongside of the yard, minus the bell which
has been lost.

Billy W inkle : Re '“Norbell’s” statement
(B. 9/10/ 13) that “the people in the country don’t
wash themselves whether they have baths or not.”
I ha\e visited most of the towns in Western Queens-
land, as far out as Longreach, and the
people of these towns seemed to be par-
ticularly fond of bathing, not in a tub
or bathroom such as “Norbell” describes,
but in natural river baths or conserved
bore water. At Blackall, Barcaldine and
Aramac people with towels may be seen
making for the baths at any time of day
or night. Both Barcaldine and Aramac
sport a caretaker for their artesian water
baths, and he doesn’t look as if he needed
exercise either. Between these towns a
friendly rivalry exists as to which has
the better bath. The only man in Bar-
caldine who doesn’t believe in artesian
water bathing is the local chemist; pro-
bably the population is too healthy to
please him. There are other towns,
of course, that have not these natural
advantages, but it is a very poor town
indeed that does not boast a “swimming
hole.’’ I venture to say that more chil-
dren learn to swim in the country towns
than in the city. Wagga, Albury, Hay,
Narandera and all river towns have their
•swimming clubs, and in other towns men-
tioned by “Norbell,” such as Wellington,
Young- and Parkes,. thank the Lord for good rains
there is ample water and plenty of facilities for
bathing. _ Appears from “Norbell’s” par. that he puts
up at 15-bob-a-week hasheries and expects 10s. a
day comforts.
“D.L.H.”: “R.V.” (B. 16/30/’l3) is no
sheep-man. He says of the station-hand: “Then over
a slush lamp or spluttering candle he argues,
wrangles and sometimes fights over sheep.” I’ve
never known it so. The only time I’ve heard sheep
mentioned by a station-hand has been when the talk
has turned to the marvellous way in which his dog
has circumvented an obstreperous mob. The most
common subject of conversation in any bush camp,
whether it’s sheep, cattle, scrub cutters’, fencing
or any other (I except a dairy farm—l never lived
on one), is horse: race horse, stock horse, the horse
I once had, the horse of the man I used to know,
the exceptionally intelligent pack horse (this sub-
ject is inexhaustible), the take-down horse, the wool-
team horse and, of course, the bucking horse. The
next most popular subject is woman, after which
come dogs and snakes in that order. It is onlf in
the “big house” that sheep monopolise the conver-
sation. The owner talks sheep all the time, the
manager most of the time, and the overseer when
its necessary to offer polite agreement with what
his overtords say. I know; I’ve been an overseer.
, . n ’ ust have S ot his experience as a guest of
the big house.”
Gagani-Namo : The Ivikenies (maidens)
are three stone columns which stand by themselves
in the Pacific some 20 miles west of Port Moresby.
Local tradition has it that away back in the haze of
time a coastal chief thereabouts, in order to placate
a & re at warrior inland, decided on presenting him
with his youngest daughter. When he acquainted
her wnh his purpose, she, aghast at being separ-
ated from her young lover, and terrified with the
prospect of going among a tribe who had been raid-
ing and eating her people for as long as could be
remembered, fled and hid in the scrub. Then the
two remaining daughters were told that they were to
go and square up with the mountain chief; but they
too, took fright, and bolted. Angered out of all’
a flection, the father summoned his pourri-pourri men
(magicians) to discover and punish the disobedient
girls. The sorcerers went away to make their secret
magic while all the villagers assembled to await
developments. Some hours went by, then suddenlv
the earth quaked, tremendous thunders deafened the
multitude; and in a blinding flash of lightning the
, Kl . kenle ® were vomited out of a chasm and
hurtled far out into the sea, where they’ll stand till
the end of time for a concrete warning to the
daughters of that part of Papua.
“Sandfly”: The English fox is quickly
spreading over Australia. Two of these pests were
captured quite recently at Normanby, 60 miles from
owen (N.Q.). A blackboy engaged by a scalper
camp home to his boss’s camp brimful of the infor-
mation of the animal he had shot. Said he: “No
nahve dog, no pussy cat, no bear; what name?”
the hide is now on exhibition at Bowen.

Or the other small child, buried in an up-country
Tasmanian cemetery, who was “the blasted hope of
his father.’ One of the monuments now falling to
pieces in the old St. David’s enclosure marks the
grave 0 f Captain and Mrs. Kelly and their numerous
children. A sad little procession of lives these last
that flickered by like the shadow kings in “Macbeth’*-
i»r et ’ , dled 1817 - a ? ed two days; Eliza, died
18-3, aged one month; George, died 1828, aged five
months: Elizabeth Jane, died 1828. aged 14 years
John, died 1831, aged one day.’’ After these came
James Brum, aged 21 years, killed by a blow from
a whale, 1841; and Thomas, aged 16, drowned bv
the upsetting 1 of a boat in the Derwent, 1842. Cap
tain James Kelly holds a memorable place in early
Tasmanian history. A big, autocratic, genial man
he once won a bet that his breeks would hold five
bushels of wheat. His doings and inches filled the
public eye, and the little shipping town buzzed with
interest when he came back from one of his voyages
of discovery round the Tasmanian coast or amoiw
the islands of Bass Strait. His most famous voyage
was that of 1815, when he went round the stormy
West Coast in a whaleboat, discovering Port Davey
and endeavoring, without success, to enter the Pie-
man River. The chartings and soundings which he
then made are still in use. Kelly was a N.S.W.
man by birth, and must have been one of the earliest
native-born Australians. His epitaph is inscribed-
Memory of James Kelly, who departed
this life on the 20th of April, 1859, in the 66 year
of his age. He was born in the town of Parra-
matta, New South Wales, on the 21st day of De-
cember, 1791, and has resided the principal part of
his life Jn Van Diemen’s Land. Now called Tasmania.
Australians will have kind remembrances
of Griffin, the imported American town-planner, for
one thing, anyway—he didn’t hand out the usual
foreigner’s silly gibe at one of the Oldest Inhabitants.
“The gum tree,” enthused Griffin, “instead of being
one big continual monotony, has strongly appealed to
me. It is a poet’s tree. It is a most wonderful
tree a decorator’s tree. Its foliage is beautiful, and
its bark and twigs have a beauty of their own.”
Hooray!

“Ajax”: The Sydney, Telegraph prints
the information, with big headlines, that “COWS
EAT DEAD RABBITS,” which, after all, doesn't
startle me so much as, had the case been reversed
ancl we were told that “DEAD RABBITS EAT
(OW S. But the cowardly intent of the paragraph
lies in the mean effort to rob some of our poets of
at least a portion of their stock-in-trade— i.e., in the
insinuation that “the sweet breath of kine” is not
all that it has been cracked up to be.
CONTINUOUS RAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Softly incessant, on the iron roof
It drums with silver fingers,-while the sound
Of running water murmurs all around—
Grey is the warp of life and grey the woof 1
Grey is the little room wherein we dwell
Shut from the sun, where through the flickering'
pall ' b
Of keen, cold lances comes the river’s call,
A hoarse, sad voice that ever seems to swell.
Not blackest midnight harbors such a host
Of eerie fancies as these sombre dgvs
When all the world lies chilled with dread amaze,
caught m the clammy vesture of a ghost.
The minor melody of gentle rain
Echoes beneath the dark and dripping trees—
Oh for a quick, a cloud dispelling breeze,
And the clear sunlight on the hills again ! ’
victoria.

Sanatogen Brings New
Health and Nerve Force.
** I have used Sanatogen with extra-
ordinary benefit. It is to my mind a true
food tonic, feeding the nerves, increasing
the energy, and giving fresh vigour to the
overworked body and mind.”
These wort’s by Sir Gilbert Parker, M.P.,
the well-known author, have a helpful
meaning for you —if you need real help in
one of those crises of life when your health
is giving you anxiety.
They echo a sentiment of gratitude which
is expressed in many different ways in
thousands upon thousands of letters from
men and women who owe to Sanatogen new
strength and vitality.
The Rt. Hon. Sir John Gorst, member of His
Majesty’s Privy Council, writes: “ Sir John
Gorst wishes to say he has long been acquainted
with the great merits of Sanatogen —has taken
it with excellent results, and when necessary
will certainly take it again. It was also used
by a daughter of his with great benefit.”
To you who are run down, nervous or
weak —to you who are less than yourself —
Sanatogen will bring new health. To you
whose nerves have gone wrong, and whose
appetite or sleep or capacity for work are
impaired, Sanatogen comes as a rescuing
sustainer, a revitalising force, a feeder of
nerves and tissues.

The Vanished Infant.
A Victorian detective told recently of a
nurse of his acquaintance in whose house
seven babies had been born. They had all,
so she said, been “adopted,” but not one of
them could be traced. She asserted that she
knew where they were, but firmly refused
to supply details on the subject. And that
was only one nurse among many. Mean-
while frequency of the deceased baby in
the Yarra and elsewhere shows that the
adoption isn’t always as genuine as it might
be. To cope with this obvious infamy Chief
Secretary Murray proposes a Bill providing
(1) fine or imprisonment for any person
concerned in the adoption of a baby except
through the Neglected Children’s Depart-
ment, and (2) the licensing and' inspection
of all lying-in homes and hospitals. There
are points of merit about this proposal, but
it doesn’t nearly begin to meet such cases as
the above. For example, there is the un-
happy girl who comes to the city from the
country, under an assumed name, gives birth
to a child, and in desperation makes away
with it before returning home. How is she
to be saved from crime and her offspring
from destruction? Society being constituted
as it is, there is only one possible remedy
for her case—a State Foundling Home,
where all infants shall be received incognito.
With such an institution in existence
“fallen” women could get a chance to arise
once more unhampered by what the chronic
chapel-goer terms a “child of shame,” and
the Commonwealth would score, inasmuch as
the infant would be alive, and a prospective
citizen and defender.
■ ♦
A matter on which the visiting British
Parliamentarians were very strong was Aus-
tralia’s urgent need for more population.
The requirement, from Australia’s point of
view, shrieks in the public eye and looms
up like a gibbering ghost beside the public
ear, for among all the countries which are
worth taking this one has the fewest de-
fenders to the square mile. But when a
British person takes up that parable one is
tempted to wonder what unselfish individual
left the door open so that Saul blew in
among the prophets, or to ask whether Saul
came down the chimney, or was previously
concealed on the premises. Last year
(1912) John Bull imported 109.000,000
cwt. of wheat to feed himself, and his wife
Jane Cow, and his young son ’Enery Calf.
Also he imported over 10,000,000 cwt. of
flour. He wants more imported wheat every
year. In 1899 he struggled through with
66,500,000 cwt., so the increase is large and
sudden. And he depends on the empty
countries for his supplies. Many countries
refuse to remain empty, consequently places
that used to supply him are now competing
with him for the wheat the empty countries
have to spare. Inside 13 years, Germany,
Chile, Roumania and Turkey have dropped
out of the list of States which have a lot of
superfluous food to sell cheaply to Pa Bull.
The United States has dropped its supply
one-half in 13 years, and it doesn’t expect
to be a country with a surplus much longer.
The Russian supply is vanishing. And as
Australia and Canada and Argentina fill up
they will also drift out of the exporting list,
and will eat their own corn and beef, and
tell Bull to take to a fish diet. The advice
which SAUL-among-the-prophets gave to Aus-
tralia, about filling up its empty spaces, was
mighty good advice for Australia. But in
one sense it was very bad advice for Bull-
* mong-the-prophets.
“Aiiaswer”- —whose pen-name shrouds the
identity of an old and trusted Bulletin
contributor —puts some queries in connection
with elective Ministries :
As I understand, the idea is that Parliament should
elect its own Ministers. But does it not elect its
Speaker now? Is the man elected to the Speakership
the best man available? Or isn’t the position one
of the spoils to the victors? And would an immacu-
late Minister of Anything be appointed to office from
the Opposition? And if not, what’s the use?
The Speaker hasn’t always been the best
man available, but then he is to a certain
extent a product of the party system. The
Party gets together. The blessed Party
puts up its nominee for the Speakership.
And the grand old Party often votes pretty
solidly for its nominee. Hence such
Speakers as Young in N.S.W., Cowley in
Queensland, Reibey in Tasmania (he meant
well, but he wasn’t a shining light or a city
set upon a hill), Bent, Gillies and Frank
Madden (three lamentables) in Victoria,
and Johnson in Australian politics. But
the Speaker’s billet comes nearer to being
elective than any other first-class job in
politics, and perhaps for that reason this
country has had many very high-class men
in the chair—in fact, the high-class men
probably predominated. Bent, Gillies,
Frank Madden and Cowley went in on a
purely Government vote. So long as there
are parties, and the party in power is strong
enough to provide a Speaker and Chairman
of Committees, and there are faithful hacks
who must be provided for, an occasional in-
ferior Speaker is, of course, inevitable.
According to a recent cable: —
Experts maintain, that it is extremely doubtful
whether the army biplanes ordered by the Federal
Government will fly in Australia, as their planes are
not sufficiently large to lift them in the rarefied air
of this country. The biplanes referred to are of the
BE2 type, and similar to those in the British army.
Barometric pressure indicates that the air,
in most parts of Australia, is denser than it
is in Europe, at the same height. Here are
two days’ readings at Sydney Observatory—
J2/10/’I3: 9 a.m., 30.0S0; 9 p.m., 30.107;
13/10/’l3, 9 a.m., 30.151;- 9 p.m., 30.120.
That shows a pretty constant air-pressure on
the coast of Australia of over 30-inches of
mercury, as compared with an ordinary pres-
sure on the coasts of Europe of something
under 30 inches. W. E. Hart, the New South
Wales aviator, flew persistently in the neigh-
borhood of Richmond and Windsor with
never the vestige of a mishap. The accident
which put him out of the flying game for
good had nothing to do with the state of the
atmosphere: it was a matter of using a
monoplane when he was accustomed only to
a biplane. Incidentally, he flew .in the more
rarefied air of the Western plains and came
to no harm. When it is remembered that
aeroplane flights have been made in Europe
over the Alps, at such heights as 16,000
and 17,000 feet—altitudes where, such was
the thinness of the air, the flying men could
hardly breathe —the allegation against the
air of Australia begins to look pretty thin.
As a matter of fact, the odds are that the
air-pressure is greater everywhere in the
Southern than the Northern Hemisphere ; a
result, so some of the French physiographers
think, of the south-polar ice-cap being much
thicker and more extensive than the north-
polar cap.
“Harbor Watch” from an English port: —
The newspapers here have been hip-hooraying
over the performance of Captain Charles Charleton,
of the Liverpool ship Harold. The skipper, it seems,
navigated his ship of 1299 tons, wheat laden, 15,000
miles, from Australia to Queenstown, without the
aid of a single officer. The trip took 108 days and
nights, and for most of the 108 nights the Old Man
slept in a cabin chair on the poop, and for weeks
at a time didn’t have his clothes off. According to
the published yarn, no officers could be picked up at
Adelaide, Melbourne or Sydney, so Captain Charles
Charleton put to sea on his lonesome —except, of
course, for a crew of the usual mixed foreigners.
Now, there’s something I want to know. Do the
Australian regulations allow a 1299-ton ship to put
to sea with only one certificated officer in charge?
If so, why sof And if not, why did they allow this
skipper to skip out? He couldn’t have waited for
a dark night, because we’re told the ship wandered
round howling for an officer to come and sign on ;
and when it couldn’t get one it was allowed to de-
part. If the authorities can’t see the rottenness of
permitting such a thing they ought to consult an
oculist. And if the regulations and the authorities
don’t permit such goings on, and the captain did it
in spite of them, they ought still to be taken to a
public place and severely spoken to with a rope’s
end. If Captain Charleton had accidentally fallen
over the coalscuttle and broken his neck, or been
run over by a taxi-cab, or lost the number of his
mess by any other common sea accident, the ship
would have gone on drifting round until the grub
ran out and the crew ate one another, or they would
have sailed round in a circle till they got giddy.
There are few men more helpless to fetch a port
than a seaman who knows nothing of navigation,
and even if the captain had not died right out, but
had gone off his head or his feet, any old thing
might have happened to the ship and crew, and any
other ship and crew that came in the way. So,
when the cheers for the captain’s feat have died
down, I beg to propose that those responsible for
the regulation of ocean shipping in Australia he in-
vited to explain things. But then perhaps the yarn
may turn out to be a lie.
“Briner” : Mention of Grice, Sumner and
Co. (B. 23/10/’l3) recalls that that firm
owned the first steamer to regularly do the
round Sydney-Maoriland-Melbourne trip.
The vessel was the little Hero, which in its
earlier and sprucer days had been stipen-
diary yacht to the King of Sweden. After-
wards came the Rangitoto, Prince Alfred,
Tararua and Claude Hamilton, owned by
the Panama and Maoriland Co. Next the
Otago and Albion, the latter afterwards
named the Centennial, in revenge for which
Britannia, then ruling the waves, sunk her
to the bottom of Taylor’s Bay, in Sydney
Harbor. In 1876 came the Arawatta and
Ringarooma. The Union Co. was formed in
1875, and in 1879 put on the Rotoma-
hana, a large, swift greyhound or Skye ter-
rier of 1777 tons, and after the Rotomahana
the Wakatipu, Hauroto, Tarawera, Mana-
pouri. Wairarapa, Waihora, Te Anau, Mara-
roa and Waikare. In 1891 the Union ab-
sorbed the Tasmanian Co., adding the
Talune, Oonah, Pateena and Corinna. And
so it has gone on and on, till, nowadays
have the triple-screw oil-burner Niagara '
13,500 tons. 6 ’ oi
“Masarcus”: “Norseman,” as an advocatft
of liquor-regulation, is disgusted ( R
16/10/’l3) with the no-pub scheme operating
in Mildura (Vic.). The quantity of beer
poured down the neck of the fruit-growini?
community at the clubs is, he savs
ing. Still, little though the club ’ system
may lessen the drink habit, it is immeasur
ably superior to the Private Enterprise wav
For one thing, the irrigator gets his li qiu ,;
good. The clubs are not cursed with a fran-
tic desire to get rich quick on the profits
from doctored tack. The arrangement at
Mildura’s sister-settlement of Remnark
hundred miles down the river, is cer-
tainly better, because more public-
spirited. Renmark’s solitary pub i s
run by a committee elected by the
whole settlement, and all its spare
profits go in road-making and other public
improvements. Even the Wowseristic sec-
tion of the little town, though it is agitating
fiercely for the reduction of the hours
grog-selling, never nowadays demands the
shutting of the doors altogether.
“Dogs of War”: There are various sorts
of dogs; but I wouldn’t care to “fancy” a
dog that was just a blind cataract of hair.
I saw the portrait of one of these outrages
in a Sydney paper recently. It was a prize-
winner in the outrage class, and it looked
like a front view of Niagara in hair-flood.
I don’t see what use a dog of that variety
is. The only way it could be self-supporting
would be to hire itself out as a dishcloth or
a doormat. I have seen these things on the
show-bench. They are generally attended by
a pathetic, unhealthy-looking woman whose
life-job it is to linger round with a comb and
brush and valet the accursed thing. The
animal inside the torrent of hair always
looks miserable, and, I am sure, would be
better dead. Yet an offer to kill it free of
charge would probably be received with
screams by its wretched slave. I suppose
it is the function of the unnecessary person
to look after the superfluous animal, and the
removal of the animal is liable to make the
person feel lonely. That’s the only way I
can account for it.
“Rule. Nisi” writes :
Another tangled case rising out of the consolida-
tion of the Statutes occupied the time of a N.S.IV.
Court last week. The Children’s Protection Acts
were consolidated in 1902, and the clause in the
1892 Act providing penalties for any person neglect-
ing a child was dumped into the division headed
“adopted” children. A father, who had been found
guilty of neglecting to support his child, seized upon
this point to appeal. He argued that “person” didn't
include “parent,” and, as he hadn’t “adopted” the
little girl admittedly neglected, he wasn’t properly
convicted. The Judge confessed that looking at the
consolidated Act, there was considerable force in this
argument, but held that in cases of doubt Judges
were entitled to ignore the consolidated Act, and
refer back to the original and repealed Statute. He
thereupon upheld the conviction. This decision is
fortunate as regards the merits of a case, but whether
it would stand the brunt of a further appeal is
dubious. An Act repealed is presumably an Act
repealed ; and if it isn’t what is the good of repealing
it? Further, what is the good of consolidating a
drove of old Acts in order to save the trouble of
referring to them, if the drove is going as strong as
ever in spite of the consolidation ?

POLICAL
ITEMS
The eminent horse-doctor appears to be
running the Northern Territory irrespec-
tive of the mere Australian Government.
Even Prime Minister J. Cook doesn’t know
what Gilruth is doing. In the House of
Reps, the other night, Joseph remarked: —
We have Professor Gilruth, who is supposed to be
governing the Territory—
Mr. Frazer: Supposed to be?
Mr. Cook: That is all we know about it!
Mr. Frazer: Is he not governing it ?
Mr. Cook: I suppose he is. It is a “suppose”
again. I do not know anything about it.
But there are so many things that Jokook
knows nothing about that a detail like the
Northern Territory is hardly worth men-
tioning.
Higgs, member for Capricornia (Q.) in
Australia’s House of Reps, annoyed Jokook
the other night, merely because he said :
Let me say that the expenditure in this Bill is a
shameless abandonment of the public expressions of
honorable members on the Ministerial side. They
are fools and blind, or they are ignoble knaves. They
are miserable sycophants, crawling to people in high
places, or they are rogues and tricksters who have
got into office by false pretences. Worse than that, I
say they are hollow, insincere, lying hypocrites.
If Higgs hadn’t been pulled up by the
Speaker and suspended from service by 43
votes to eight, he would probably have said
something really nasty.
THE BROKEN PLEDGE.
THE INTRUDER: “Bill Holman, I'm sick and
tired o' you. Three years ago you took
this pledge, and since then you've done
nothing else but break it."
Things turned out most unfortunately at
the annual meeting of the Women’s Liberal
League at Ballan (Vic.) the other day.
The orator of the occasion was H. V.
McKay, the “Sunshine” man, who put up
the tough fight for Deakin’s old Ballarat
seat. McKay poured out his Australian
soul, and urged the tabbies (in quite a
delicate wAy) to keep the continent white.
Then, the White Australian orator having
subsided, the next item was called on —Mr.
Ling Ah Mouey would entertain the com-
pany with a song. When McKay saw his
successor on the White Australian plat-
form, he fled by a side door.
This is how Willie Kelly fell in. The
other day Poynton (S.A.) wanted to know
the circumstances of an alleged saving of
£125,000 made in connection with the Oodna-
datta-Port Augusta railway. Willie replied
that shortly after he was placed in charge
of the Home Affairs Department, tenders
(called by the late Government) for the
supply of £125,000 worth of rolling stock to
enable the railway to be run by the Com-
monwealth came before him. By arrange-
ment with the South Australian Govern-
ment, he said, purchase of that rolling stock
was obviated. The Opposition left Kelly
alone, and presently applied to Glynn, who
answered that the contract referred to was
entered into by the External Affairs L>epart-
ment, of which he is the political head.
Furthermore, Glynn informed the House
that “there is nothing for blame, praise,
credit, or discredit to any side about the
matter.” Kelly took his gentle castigation
in silence. The public rebuke by a senior
colleague was so obvious that even the Op-
position thought it too cruel to rub in the
salt.
A RARE SPECIMEN.
I have lived much in other countries, but in none
of them have I seen so much money spent in sport
and amusements generally by the workers as in this
land of the golden fleece.... They are extrava-
gant and wanton in their use of money. For one
thing, they are too expensively housed, and for
another, too lavishly fed and clad.... Again,
the incentive to save is destroyed by the prospective
payment of the old-age pension ; and that fine, rugged
manhood and spirit of independence—once our boast
—have largely disappeared.
—Daily ray correspondent.

thought your kind was extinct! I want
yer for my political museum ! ”
For the first nine months of 1913 N.S.W.
imnorted from abroad goods to the value of
£24.064,161 : and exported, by way of pay-
ment. merchandise and gold valued at
£20,290,431. So on its trade alone it went
into debt to the extent of nearly
£4,000,000. And as it had to pay about
£3,000,000 in interest on foreign loans and
investments, it really went to the Devil to
the extent of about £7,000,000. Which is
a lot of devil to go to in nine months.
The history of the State-
owned St. Kilda and
Brighton (Vic.) tramway
shows once more the stark
folly of giving somebody
else the trunk traffic while
you look after the branches
yourself. The little State-
owned line seems to do all
right; yet the revenue in
excess of working exes, in
the past year wasn’t enough
to pay a third of the inter-
est bill. The N. S. Wales
experience is the same. As
a whole the Sydney tram-
ways have been showing
over per cent. ; yet the
feeders have been, by them-
selves, a losing proposition
all the time. Even the
North Sydney system, so
important that half a
million has been spent on it,
has been a pretty consistent
loser of anything over £5OOO a year.
Sir Edward Carson, P.C., K,C., etc., has
been sentenced to seven months’ imprison-
ment for using seditious language. (P.S. —
On re-reading the cable The Bulletin finds
that it was Larkin, a Dublin laborer, who
got the seven months.)
The bad-tempered Cook spent a good part
of last week withdrawing injudicious re-
marks at the direction of the Speaker. The
yapping type of politician is best placed as
one of the chorus, because no one has much
time to spare for impertinences from the
wings. Cook being what he is (tempo-
rarily), the Speaker has to give him special
attention —which is disastrous for the ex-
plosive Joe. Still it must be rather a relief
to Cook to even back out. If it isn’t pro-
gression, it is at least movement, and an
evidence that he isn’t
completely paralysed.

Don’t know why; but this par. seems to
insist on getting into this page:—
The “tired feeling” business can be overdone.
F’rinstance, an attendant at Eroni s Circus leant
against the hyena’s cage the other day, and presently
had one of his fingers bitten off. It is not safe to be
tired in proximity to a hyena unless one can be
quite sure that the hyena is also tired.
Sydney Daily Telegraph finds in the
deadlock in the National Parliament an un-
answerable argument against unification.
Supposing all legislative power were vested
in the one Parliament, it says, the whole
business would now be hung up, whereas,
things being as they are, each State can go
ahead developing itself. It was reported the
other day that the Daily Telegraph was
building a new home for itself. This must
be a mistake. If it built one office, and put
the whole staff into it, and an earthquake or
a fire happened along, the entire business
would be hung up. So the Telegraph is
putting its editor into a crib in King-street,
and its news editor into another in Market-
street, and its financial editor is to have his
desk in Mosman, and the sub-editors and re-
porters are to be divided up among the other
suburbs. At least, that is what the D. T.
would do if it ran itself on the principle
which it thinks should guide the running of
Australia.
From “Locust,” who has just fluttered
in:—
The Bulletin’s analysis (9/10/T3) of Willie
Kelly makes plain to me (having just been putting
in some interesting weeks watching the British
Parliament at work) that Willie has caught the
House of Commons manner in the course of his peri-
odical English trips. Among the leaders of the
alleged Mother of Parliaments there is a fine choice
open for the adaptable student, from the rough-and-
ready brilliance of Burns at question-time, through
the truculent, don’t-care-a-damness of Lloyd George,
the fluent cleverness of the very rapidly coming
Masterman (a future Prime Minister), the flippant
God-a’-mightiness of Churchill, to the easy dignity
of Asquith. Apparently Willie Kelly has copied a
little from each, and mixed the various samples into
a thick paste. Some years ago a politician who
measured himself for the Speaker s chair of the
House of Reps., but did not convince his colleagues
that he fitted it, went to London, and, it is said,
spent nine months “stewing” procedure with one of
the clerks of the House of Commons. The local
Parliament, which lately had his valuable services in
the chair, was fortunate in seeing a colorable imita-
tion of the splendid and dignified traditions of the
great alleged Mother of Parliaments.
The Cook Government, which was defeated
last week, as a result of its leader’s rash
acceptance of the battle-gage tendered him
by the other side, will obviously never be
safe except when it is in recess. Take the
problem that is presented by the imperish-
able Bruce Smith, for example. Cook has
been trying his level best, for some time, to
placate the Victorian members of his party.
In the middle of his efforts Bruce uprose
and
complained of the “modern” utterances by the
Prime Minister on the tariff. It seemed as if Mr.
Cook was considering the likelihood of the Liberal
party doing something in the way of imposing higher
duties. He had no sympathy with such a proposal,
and he desired that that should be understood.
About the same time he mentioned darkly
that if any action were to be taken against
the U.S. Beef Trust by the Government, he
would battle, as of yore, for Private Enter-
prise. And in addition to Smith, the
harassed leader has to cope with Conroy, and
he only has a majority of one to do it with.
It is too much to ask of any man.
The redistribution of seats and compiling
of the N. 8. Wales Parliamentary rolls for
the imminent elections have been'completed
ltandwick (city), with tops the list
for bulk of voters; while Macquarie (coun-
try), with 9018, is a mere shadow. Fifteen
of the old electorates, 12 in the country and
three in the city, have disappeared, and have
been replaced by eight in the city and seven
in the country. Thus the grand old conges-
tion policy continues to drain the State’s
body and bulge to bursting point its fat head
N.S.W. Works Minister
Griffith has made the inter-
esting discovery that a man’s
debts are his capital. Hence
if you accumulate enough
pawn-tickets you become a
millionaire; and when you
say good-bye to the bailiff
in the parlor and lie down
on the time-payment sofa
and die, your widow, as she
borrows another shilling to
pay for an advt. asking for
charing, has the great satis-
faction of knowing that her
husband died very rich,
leaving her well-provided-for
-—with debts.
Prime Minister Cook got his revenge early,
and caught the Labor party on Thursday
night with only 14 members in the Chamber,
Taking full advantage of the opportunity, he
bullocked his Loan Bill through by the mer-
ciless use of the closure. Metaphorically
speaking, he danced with both feet on the
Opposition, and taught it a very useful
lesson. There are 75 members of the House
of Reps.—3B Ministerialists and 37 Labor-
ites. Cook managed to gather up 25, or
10 more than Fisher had behind him; which
suggests that either the Labor discipline is
very lax or that the party doesn’t think the
mere pawning of Australia is a matter of any
consequence whatever.
While in Groperdom, Lough, a member of
the British Parliamentary Party, poured the
following remarks into the listening ear of a
Perth daily :
Anyway, Japan is really not near to Australia. It
is many days’ sail away, and if Australia were threat-
ened, and there were any need for intervention on
the part of the fleet at Home, it would not take
long for our war-dogs to arrive on the spot.
Also, there is a fair quantity of water
between Germany and England ; so what is
wrong with keeping the whole British navy
in the Mediterranean? Certainly, if any-
thing went wrong, and the German got in
first, a few English cities might be reduced
to ashes, and a few thousand British turned
into corpses ; but it is really no worse to be
a British corpse than an Australian cadaver.

The Trainer : “No, no, Greggy, we mustn’t
interjuce these inter the ring! It might
embarrass our opponent a lot; but then,
again, the fight might be given against
us on a foul!"
One of the points set forth by Higgs,
M.H.R., during that debate in which Ryrie,
called him “a miserable body
snatcher.” was that the cost of defence in
Australia is excessive. In Great Britain,
which is the worst case of all, the bill for
the bloated armaments is said to be 31s. 9d.
per inhabitant per annum ; France, 275. 5d.:
Australia, 235. 6d.; Germany, 18s. 9d.; and
so on down to Japan (4s. Gd.). But, after
all, area and density of population count for
a great deal. Thus Japan (the four-and-six-
penny country), on the Higgs estimate, puts
up roughly £75 12s. per annum for the de-
fence of each souare mile of its territory,
while Australia puts up something like £2.
The bill may be numerous, but the people
are few.
BALLADE OF GOD-BLESS-YOU!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dubbo, Tuesday.—Mr. Griffith, Minister for Works,
turned the first sod of the Dubbo-Werris Creek rail-
way this afternoon. He was accorded a civic recep-
tion. He assured them that this was no empty cere-
mony. He had already two surveyors’ camps on the
ground, and as soon as a couple of miles had been
staked out and levels taken the work would be
started. — Neu's item.
Censure for many and praise for a few ;
Harsh objurgations for those who offend ;
Mild exhortations from Pulpit to Pew ;
Sulphur for sinners from Hell-without-end !
Lofty advice for each dissolute friend ;
Christian excuses for parties in quod ;
Howls for the Lost who drink Old Highland Blend —-
God bless a Minister laying a sod !
Cheers for the noble and generous Jew,
Ready, aye f ready his shekels to lend,
Just in exchange for our mere I O U,
Or a P.N. —rather hurriedly penned.
Curses far deeper than voice can descend
Light on the folk who regard it as odd
That Labor displays such a pawnable trend —-
God bless a Minister laying a sod !
Hoots for all croakers who say loans fall due!
Jeers for the fools who think prices ascend:
This Ship of State has a jolly good crew—
Who cares a rap what they borrow and spend ?
What does a five per cent, bank rate portend?
Just go ahead with the chain and the rod!
Look ! there’s his motor-car rounding the bend—
God bless a Minister laying a sod !
Envoy.
Prince, for their railways our townships contend,
Pleading they're packed thick as peas in a pod ;
What will they do when the lines turn and rend?
God bless a Minister laying a sod !

SPORITNG
NOTIONS
That Pot of Excitement, the Melbourne
Cup, which has been bubbling with increasing ardor
for the last three months, boiled over at 3.10
on Tuesday afternoon, when out of the crush that
thundered down the straight Posinatus, Belove and
Ulva’s Isle came to the front, and finished in that
order. The winner is the five-year-old gelding son
of Fortunatus from Whakatiko, and is owned and
trained by J. Chambers.
* * * * ♦
Beragoon’s capture of the V.R.C. Derby
was a popular revival of the ancient order of things.
Four years had elapsed since an A.J.C. Derby winner
confirmed his excellence at I* lemington, and in this
case the confirmation was complete. Starting first
favorite for choice, although the odds against him
rose from 6 to 4 at the outset of the betting to
2 to 1 at the start, Beragoon won the race at both
ends, and did it in record time. He took the lead
for the first few furlongs, was never further back
than third, and after rounding the turn for home
took the lead again, and increased it all the way
to the judge’s box. But for the vague suspicion that
he might fail to go the full distance, Beragoon
would have jumped immediately into first favoritism
for the Cup. Had he been a son of Positano instead
of Multiform, punters would have rushed him as a
moral. On paper there was nothing for a first-class
three-year-old to beat among the older horses at the
weights—bar, perhaps, the always questionable Sea
Prince.
No improvement in Beragoon had been remarked
since he came to Flemington, yet he must have im-
proved a lot. That sturdy little Radnor did not
trouble him this time. Andelosia finished nearer
to Radnor than at Randwick, and Ulva’s Isle (said
to have received a nasty bump in the race) was no
further away. Of the beaten ones, Ulya’s Isle looked
the most promising Cup candidate (with the proviso
that a 6st. Vlb. boy could manage him), for he was
palpably short of work on Saturday. Taking them
all on their appearance in the birdcage, anyone
would have picked the Isle as a Cup horse of the
future, notwithstanding his somewhat wicked eye.
As a rule, the books, who are the best judges of
the game, give more heed to the Melbourne Stakes
running than to ordinary w.f.a. form. Twelve actual
winners of the Cup within 25 years have been placed
in the Stakes and several horses that didn’t win the
Cup were placed in both events. For instance, Car-
bine, who won both Stakes and Cup in 1890, was
the runner-up in both events the previous year. The
field for the Stakes last Saturday included eight of
the Cup acceptors, Uncle Sam, Aurifer and Moun-
tain Princess being seriously backed to beat Anna
Carlovna (scratched for the Cup), whilst Harpist
and Wassail were treated as mere out-for-an-airing
candidates with no visible chance of winning. The
somewhat unaccountable feature of the betting was
Anna Carlovna’s price (6 to 4), as compared with
Aurifer’s 5 to 1. Their running in the Caulfield
Cup didn’t warrant this preference for the mare.
Aurifer gave her 61b. at Caulfield, and was going as
fast as she at 10 furlongs, and faster at 12 furlongs.
In the 10 furlongs Stakes race there was only 51b.
difference between them, yet Anna Carlovna was
backed as a certainty, and she ran up to market
form. It seems that Connolly was unlucky to miss
his Caulfield Cup coup with the honest mare. Anna
Carlovna is a good one, and had she been left in the
Melbourne Cup, punters would have taken short
odds that she could stay the course. There was no
doubt as to her being the pick of the handicap on
her Caulfield Cup and subsequent running. Hence
the reasonable assumption that Connolly thought he
had a real good thing in Sea Prince, who ran a
respectable race in the Stakes.
Traquette’s easy victory in the Maribyrnong Plate
is no promise of a career for the filly after she has
done her two-year-old dash. She is by Traquair from
Simmereth. One of the slick and early sort.
The fact that Hallowmas, carrying Bst. 131 b., was
beaten by only half a neck for the Hotham Handicap
suggests that owner Merton should have left the sour
English horse in the Cup to try his luck. Hallowmas
and Matouru (who started favorite and ran nowhere
in the H.H.) strike one as a likely pair for the
V.R.C. Handicap on Saturday.
* '*■**
Cagou, Harpist and Ulva’s Isle were the
most promising trio of stable companions that any
trainer ever prepared for a Melbourne Cup. The pair
of 4-vear-olds had proved themselves capable of stay-
ing the distance at a fair nace, and one of them —
Cagou—had performed brilliantly over 12 furlongs.
The youngster —by Positano from Lord Ullin’s Daugh-
ter—was bred to stay, and had been running on at
the finish of the A.J.C. Derby. In a year when the
Cup field looked weak, these three stable companions
had each a good chance of winning. Scobie didn’t
hold a stronger hand when he sent four horses to the
post, and two of them —Clean Sweep and Malster —
finished first and second.
A Tale of the Turf as the Turf is in
Sydney. The (say) Rooster Bros, had decided that
the time was ripe for another win by their occa-
sionally flying neddy, Walnut. They were confident
that after the way he had driven whole fields home
before him, the price was safe to be 10 to 1 against.
Immediately that price was called a commission was
to be worked in the Rooster Bros’, well-known
polished style. Gus Dasher, a jockey of credit and
renown, was chosen to carry off the coup. Every-
thing was all nicely arranged a week before the
race. But during that week the younger Rooster
told McFlake, the tobacconist, of the sure thing.
Mac immediately handed out £25, and asked Rooster
to invest it for him on the course. He opined that
£250 would come in useful. But, being a generous
soul, Mac whispered the good tidings to a relative in
the softgoods line; who also handed out £25 with
a request to get it on. Rooster duly undertook the
commission, and they went forth to make a libation,
which cost three hours and £2 10s. Betting on the
Rabbitskin Handicap, 7| furlongs, opened with four
to one against Walnut. The brothers grunted hard,
swore a little and waited. All sorts of odds flew
round, but Walnut stuck religiously at fours. Time
was running out and the elder Rooster took the
jockey aside and nointed out that it was not worth
winning at less than 10 to 1 against. The jockey
agreed. “Well, see here,” said Rooster. “You’ll be
going out for the preliminary in a few minutes. I'll
be standing just here. If me hat’s off, it’s no win.
If it’s on, let him go ; and put it to him hot and
strong. Unnerstan’?” The jockey repeated it care-
fully, and swore it was indelibly written on his
memory. On the track Walnut moved as though fit
to run for a continent —but in the ring he never
moved. He stuck hopelessly at 4 to 1 against.
Rooster played himself in position with his hat under
his arm, and as he did so his brother came through
the ring and whispered : “Whatever blanky flash pun-
ter has forestalled us to-day will fall right in!"
There was delay at the post, but Walnut scuck fast
at fours. The younger Rooster slipped away and
wired to McFlake that the win was Off and there was
no bet —all was off, including the horses at the
barrier. The disgusted barber rushed to the draper
and they both took to drink. The elder Rooster stood
doggedly at his post, his hat under his arm, and com-
menced to take cold in the head. He sneezed four
times violently. The field swung into the straight
and a great roar went up: “Walnut walks in—walks
in!” The elder Rooster jammed his hat on and toge-
ther they hustled till they got a splendid view of
Walnut romping home a winner by six lengths. Sen-
sation. Alas, at the precise hour the younger Rooster
was giving McFlake the “office,” Dasher was calling
on Ephraim O'Grady, who maketh a book ; and as
the result of that interview Dasher and O Grady were
considerably enriched by the victory of Walnut in
the Rabbitskin Handicap, 7f furlongs.
“The Emu”: Duke Kahanamoku, the
Kanaka boy the Americans took to Stockholm in their
Olympic team, does not swim the crawl stroke, as is
usually thought, but something peculiarly his own.
It is the laziest-looking stroke I have ever seen. The
Americans say he swims the original crawl stroke,
but they sav anything. At top sneed he is very
deceptive. He appears to be loafing through the
water, yet the clocks have a hard time to beat him.!
I saw him the first time he went out to break a
record, and took photos, of the swim. It was done
in the open harbor, between two wharves, Diving
from his board he covered the distance in a fraction
over the 55sec. There was some doubt as to the
actual time, as the keepers were all amateurs, but it
was plain that the boy was extraordinary, and 1 would
stand watching for the world’s big event. _ He never
hurried once, I, being used to the quick action
of our Australian boys, thought he would not come
within yards of the time. Great long arms Whirl
over his' head at full length and the sweep he makes
with them sends him through the water at terrific
pace. So far as 1 have seen, he always uses the
ordinary scissors kick, and has nothing at all of
the paddle-wheel foot-action. Kahanetcetera is
strictly an amateur, and does not want to lose this
status for the next Olympic games ; but he has the
American love of the almighty dollar. From what
I know of him, I think he will come to Australia if
there is enough money offering, but not unless. He
has some good offers from the swimming clubs in
California, and the last I heard he, or rather his
manager, was debating whether to accent the Aus-
tralian or the American offer. One last word concerning
the yarn that he is accustomed to turning in short
baths. It was not until he went to swim in the
trials for the Olympic team that he had even seen a
short bath. A plank was rigged up out at Waikiki
beach, and there he practised.
One of the most awful cricket lickings on
record has just been given in Adelaide by East
Torrens (local premiers) to Glenelg, which is pretty
weak in the absence of Crawford. Glenelg scored
124 and 126; East Torrens, two wickets for 393
(declared). Len Chamberlain was 201 not out, Mid-
dleton 161. The match was won by an innings and
eight wickets and 143 runs ! Whitty mowed down 15
wickets for 127, and the irrepressible Chamberlain
lent a hand with most of the other victims.
ACROBATIC PUGILISIS.
A frequent position of the muscular boxers,
Young Nipper (the top dog) and Holberg (the
loser) in their mild disagreement at the Syd-
ney Stadium.
The fourth round of Sydney First-Grade
Cricket commenced on Saturday, under ideal condi-
tions, the weatlier being charming and the wickets
excellent. But take it all round, it was a bowler’s
day, the batsmen failing very miserably in the cases
of Redfern, Sydney, University, Central Cumberland
and North Sydney. 'the top score was Bardsley’s
106 for Glebe against Gordon, and the second best
the 82' of his clubmate Kelleway. After that the
figures dropped to the 71 of Barnes, of Redfern. One
of the best performances was that of the reliable
Folkard, of Balmain, who took six Redfern wickets
for 64, and then hit up 41 with the bat. Massie, of
University, took five Sydney wickets for 32 ; Long,
of North Sydney, felled five of Central Cumberland's
for 35, and Dr. Hordern four for 12; to which
Coogan, of Central Cumberland, replied by smashing
up four of' North Sydney’s for 32. Coogan’s club-
mate, Pye, took two wickets for one run; and
Docker, of Western Suburbs, three of Middle Har-
bor’s for 32. Just there the things worthy of notice
end. Balmain dismissed Redfern for 170, and
managed to hit up 72' for the loss of two wickets
before the light failed. Glebe made the score of the
day, bashing 335 off Gordon for the loss of eight
wickets. Most astonishing, however, was the sight
of Sydney, after being put through for a miserable
68, smashing up University for 142. Central Cum-
berland and North Sydney gave a shocking exhibi-
tion, the former making only 80 and North Sydney
doing worse with 61. There was still time for
Central Cumberland to start on its second innings
and hit 46 without losing a stick, so it can be
imagined how the wickets tumbled around. Middle
Harbor rattled up 226, and then Western Suburbs
bashed its bowling for 89 without losing a wicket.
Petersham ran Glebe hard for pride of place, knock-
ing up 352 off Paddington, which started to reply
with eight for no broken sticks. A side issue was a
match between Waverley and Dr. O’Hara’s XI. The
Doctor’s team was slaughtered for 94 ; then, with
the help of Flynn, who smacked 113 before he
vanished from the scene, Waverley accumulated 247
for five wickets,
* * * *
South Australia is on the warpath early
for the Sheffield Shield, and although the first match
—that with N.S.W. at Sydney—does not take place
till December 19, the selectors have chosen 19 men
of might and told them to get mightier still for that
occasion. It's good business —a willing courtesy to
one s opponents that leads to success. #
Of Clarence Weber’s resultless wrastling
displav against Wasem, at Melbourne Guildhall, the
most to be said for the Australian champion is that
he probably would have been given the decision on
points. He was top dog, as a rule, but his superior
height and activity availed nothing against the nug-
getty man from America. In weight they were fairly
matched —134 stone each of them. In methods they
were not strikingly contrasted. Wasem didn’t
know enough to get Weber into serious difficulties;
and Weber labored in vain to fix Wasem with both
shoulders on the mat. Previous to the belated
struggle of the two Ws, a lightweight or middle-
weight champion from Britain took on one of Weber s
pupils for 10 minutes. The visitor had about 4st.
the worse of the deal, but he appeared to be able
to do as he liked with his huge young opponent.
Australian wrastlers have a lot to learn.
The Timaru-Christchurch (M.L.) cycling
record, which looked as firm as Mount Cook, was
biffed aside by Phil O'Shea last week. Philip started
from scratch, and did quite a lot of things. He
gathered the cash that tails to the man who finishes
first and makes the fastest time. Also, he hacked
7min. 2sec. off Henderson’s shr. 22min. record time
for the 112 miles. He is easily Maoriland s fastest
road-plugger, and is identical with the O Shea who
won the Australian championship in the Warrnambool
of 1911.
Waverley (Sydney) had a woolly time in
the summer baseball struggle on Saturday. In the
first grade Leichhardt jumped on the Waverley tied.
Sox —19 runs to six. But the Blue Sox smashed
up Petersham by 27 runs to 14, and so saved the
funereal suburb from being altogether shrouded in
gloom during a pleasant Sunday. The new venture
is somewhat hard hit by the announcement of the
N.S.W. Baseball Assoc, that, it has power to, and
will, disqualify all players under the summer banner.
A lot of the best have therefore stayed out, and the
army of barrackers seems insufficient to make a
financial success of the venture.
Apparently some degree of safety is to be
imported into flying by monoplane. Pegoud, the
daring Frenchman who recently looped the loop
1000 ft. from the earth, has been demonstrating the
value of a new kind of parachute to fliers whose
aeroplanes go suddenly wrong. The contrivance was
designed and made by another Frenchman named
Bonnet. It is contained in a folding sheath fastened
to the frame of the machine. A cord is looped
round the flier’s body just below his shoulders. In
the event of accident all the ’planer has to do is to
grab the cord and pull. This detaches the aeroplane
from the machine and causes it to fill out, and all
the birdman has to see to is that he gets clear of
the falling machine. In Pegoud's case he let his
’plane suddenly go on its own, and grabbed the cord ;
then the parachute, filling out, lifted him bodily
from his now dangerously rocking vehicle, which
dashed from under him. Pegoud circled round and
round, and finally alighted in a clump of trees and
got caught in the top branches, from which he was
soon released by the crowd. The aeroplane flew on,
turned over, and came down with a bang which
smashed it to pieces. Of course, the new invention
does not absolutely do away with danger, but it
should reduce it by about 75 per cent.
* * * *
The oldest living pugilist, so far as this
paper knows, is George Brown, who will be 90 on
November 23. He was one of the ring-keepers at
the Sayers and Heenan fight, at which Vincent Dow-
ling, editor of Bell’s Life, was the referee. George
Brown lives nowadays at Brighton (Eng.), and is in
good health and comfortable circumstances.
When Wells demanded that either Reg
Baker or Arthur Scott should referee his fight with
Mehegan at Melbourne, he, no doubt, did it with a
view to making good advertising copy. But press and
promoter were alike tired, and the spectacle of Wells
or his management asking for' Scott, who had been
absolutely barred in the former fight at Sydney, was
conducive to disgust and ribaldry. The desire for
advertisement was altogether too plain. Baker put
both feet down firmly, refused to either adjudicate
himself or bring Scott all the way from Sydney to do
it, and finally appointed the stipendiary Melbourne
referee, E. Fullalove, to the job. Which was emi-
nently right and proper.
The evil luck that has dogged Danish
boxer Waldemar Holberg ever since he arrived in
Australia hit specially hard on Saturday night when,
at Sydney Stadium, after a heady fight which, in
the opinion of the pressmen and most of the audi-
ence, he absolutely won, referee Scott awarded the
verdict to Young Nipper. To this writer, it seemed,
beyond all questic.m Holberg’s fight for 19 of the
20 rounds. Nippers astonishment when Scott placed
a hand on his head was comical. Then his expression
turned quickly to one of intense elation. For a
moment the audience was dumb with amazement;
then it found its breath, and there was a terrific
demonstration of disapproval. Men of most perfect
odor and high commercial rank stood up and hooted
like- hooligans. It is an unfair and foolish thing,
though, to hoot a referee no matter how wrong his
decision may seem, unless there is reason to doubt
his impartiality ; and not the wildest hooter of them
all questions Mr. Scott’s probity. To hoot a referee
for giving an unpopular decision in all honesty is
merely a crude way of demanding that he shall in
future give the verdict to the more popular contestant
regardless of his own judgment. And the admirable
thing about Scott is that he gives his opinion every
time regardless of what anybody else thinks. All
the same, this appears to have been Arthur -Scott’s
dizzy limit in blunders ; it is almost impossible that
he can ever cap it. Writer has thought hard, but
can’t recall any set of circumstances on which the
referee could base his decision —which only shows
how different the same thing may appear to several
sets of eyes. Young Nipper was in a bad way more
than once. Then, also, the great majority of his
blows were either effectively blocked or evaded alto-
gether, and many simply missed through bad tim-
ing. Over and over again, when he was going to hit,
Holberg anticioated him with a straight left to the
face, just as Dave Smith did Bradley, The Dane,
being unusually short in the reach, has to take a
certain amount of punishment in order to get in
. close enough to hit ; but on Saturday night he took
the most of it on his gloves and forearms. On the
other hand, hardly a blow of his but made imnact.
There was nothing spectacular about it, but it was a
good, hard-working boxing contest. The crowd has
of late, however, been so accustomed to see Jerry
Jerome unfold cyclones of blows and hurricanes of
wallops that it voted this performance dull, and
ironically counted the men out. It looks as though
the poniilar taste is drifting to that of New York
and Pennsylvania, where six-round and 10-rourid bouts
are put on, on the understanding that they shall
roar from the jump If that plan were
adopted here it would he bad for the boxers;
so those gentlemen will, if they are wise,
go in for hurricane fighting, regardless of risks and
the ethics of the sport.
In spite of rain before and during the mill
about il),U00 people roiled into Baker’s Melbourne
Stadium to watch the return Mehegan-Wells scrap
r ihe wild riot over the decision in the last one, and
the loud wrangle over the question whether Wells
displayed “ring generalship” or merely skedaddled
had aroused considerable interest in the disturbance'
hither lured on by his extra Boz, of weight or stung
by the criticism of his style of fighting, Wells took
his fist. lb. into the fray with the fixed determina-
tion, apparently, of putting things beyond all q ues .
tion by knocking the Australian out. But when lie
got into the midst of the battle he found Mehegan out
of reach behind the famous smother, through which
he tried in vain to break. Mehegan was like the
famous Roman tortoise, which, though it could be
hit only on the shield, could wallop back to the heart
or head. After landing a few ineffectual blows, Wells
began to stop something that made him think hard
He is, however, so superbly light on his feet and
quick with his fists that he repeatedly hit Ilughie
very hard when that slower but harder-hitting war-
rior was coining out of his smother. 1-or several
rounds he managed to land quite a lot of lefts at
that juncture; but the obdurate Mehegan, regarding
them no more than thistledown, walloped back hard
and often, and Wells had to break away before the
fury of his blows. As the fight progressed in-fighting
became more frequent. In this Wells was clearly
out-pointed, and Mehegan, like the avalanche, was
gathering spied as he rolled round the ring, follow-
ing up better and hitting more viciously. In the
18th round Mehegan cut loose and gave Wells a very
torrid time, catching him in corners, and bashing him
unmercifully. Still, Wells’s marvellous footwork
stood to him and enabled him to minimise the inju-
ries. It was the same in the 19th, when Mehegan
pressed the Hebrew very hard. Wells tried to gallop,
but Mehegan had been training hard for sprinting.
In the final Mehegan walked straight in for a knock-
out. Wells showed some superb defensive fighting;
yet Mehegan had just got him coraled when the hell
rang. Thereupon the referee handed Mehegan the
loaves and fishes, and with them the championship.
Some opposition was shown ; but the winner haii
found favor with the best judges and the majority
of the crowd alike. Anyhow, Wells is no squealer,
and took his defeat like a good descendant of Judas
Makkabi should.
That Richard Arnst had done all that
careful preparation could do to win hack the scull-
ing championship from James Paddon, on Parra-
matta River, on Saturday, was proved by the splen-
did condition in which he finished. Early in the
race the towering Paddon got a length ahead, and
from that moment Arnst newer had a chance. He
rowed a stern chase, and rowed it magnificently, with
a dogged pluck that made onlookers regret there
could not be two winners. Again and again he
spurted, and slightly reduced the increasing gap
between the boats ; then Paddon would bend to it,
increase his stroke, and lift his shell through the
water at greater speed, making good the loss and
adding a little more to his lead. Curiously enough
the beaten man finished much the fresher of the
two ; and had it been a race till one fell overboard
through sheer attrition, Arnst would have won. But
it wasn’t, and Paddon’s superiority was good enough
for the distance. There was nothing spectacular
about the contest. A procession, no matter how hard
the pursuer struggles, is never wildly interesting. In
fact, the sight of a hopeless struggle is liable to be
depressing, and this was a most hopeless-looking
struggle. But it was a mighty important affair to
the men engaged. It puts Arnst practically down
and out as far as championships are concerned, and
also places Paddon in the direct line of succession
to the championship of the world. The conditions
were charming. The course was never kept so clear
before ; the only stranger that eluded the police and
got into the fairway was a dead marine, and, of
course, it had to collide with Arnst’s oar-blade and
throw him out of his swing. The river was floored
over with craft of every kind, huge crowds were on
the banks, and the “gate” on the official steamers
unexpectedly large; while as for the day, it was
ideal for the rowers.
The Parramatta course is three miles 330 yards,
and Paddon covered it in 21min. 41sec. Last year
Barry beat Arnst over the 4J miles Thames course
(Putney to Mortlake) in 23min. Bsec. Later on, he
beat Human over the same course in 22min. 31sec.
On that showing Paddon will have to improve a lot
before he challenges England’s champion with any
chance of success. Arnst, when at his zenith, would
have beaten Paddon’s Saturday effort easily, as in
1911 he defeated Pearce in 19min. 46sec. In 1909
he was even better still —he covered three miles
440. yards, on the Wanganui (M.L.), in 18min. 14
3-ssec. It is early, then, to talk of Paddon as a
world-beater on his showing of Saturday, though, of
course, we do not know what he had in reserve.
The last cheerful travel-round-the-world-
on-nothing crank to be heard from is 11. E. Coulson,
who allegedly bet an Anglo-Indian nothing to £lOOO
that he could bike round the world in two years.
Coulson was not to beg, borrow or steal, but to earn
his own living on land by advertising, lecturing and
selling postcards of himself. At sea he was to work
h'is passage. Last week he howled into Melbourne,
having traversed the 575 miles from Adelaide in 10
days—this frightful time being due to several
smashes. He started from Bombay, and is due to
finish in Colombo. His complete itinerary amounts
to 40,000 miles, and so far he has covered 5581.
Curiously enough it was the stipulation that ™
should work his passage by sea that gave the first
trouble. He found himself at Sourahaya, in Java,
but owing to the prevalence of Asiatic crews he
could not secure a passage to Hong Kong for •>»
days. He rides a Durkopp bike with Continental
tyres. _____

“The Oldest Inhabitant” : The committee
of the V.R.C. owns an historical curio, framed
and exhibited upon a wall of the committee
room, in the Bourke-street offices. It is a
bank note of the face-value of £l —or would
be of that face-value if it were genuine.
Fifty-odd years ago an officer in the Vic-
torian Survey Office invented photo-litho-
graphy. and this is how. It was necessary
often to reduce plans to scale, and to do it
a special lens was imported at a cost of
350 guineas. In the course of experiments
with this lens a Mr. Osborne reproduced a
bank note of the time. It was faithfully
done, and was submitted to Charles Why-
brow Ligar, then Surveyor-General. This
gentleman took it to several bank managers,
who pronounced it a genuine note. When told
that it was a forgery, and that the new pro-
cess made spurious bank-note making as easy
as shelling peas, they were much alarmed.
Mr. Osborne, however, gave them the anti-
dote : “Print your bank notes in colors.”
They saw the need of it, and colored-print
notes at once came into use. A young man
named Henry Byron Moore was then in the
Survey Office, and associated in the experi-
ment ; he had received a practical education
in chemistry and printing, his father having
given his sons a laboratory and printing press
to practise with at home. Moore was asked
by Mr. Ligar to study photo-lithography and
did so, and thus the note came into his
hands A few years later the same Byron
Moore was a Black Wednesday victim, and,
thus losing his public-service job. became
secretary of the V.R.C., and passed the his-
torical note to the club. Mr. Osborne died
in America last year.

The Inky Way.
If Thady O’Kane were alive he would whoop to
know that an old sub-editor of the Northern Miner
at Charters Towers (Q.) has just been elected high-
serang of the Congregational Church, in S. Aus.
North Queensland newspaper offices have a lot of
religion that they have no use for, and youn^
I letcher—Lionel B. —picked up some of it as mining
reporter and sub. Then he tackled theology, the
habit of tackling theology being in the family, and,
after filling a small N.S.W. pulpit or two, turned
(as usual) a very attentive ear to a call when it came
from a bigger tabernacle at Port Adelaide. The boss-
ship of the denomination within the State followed.
