“Locust”: When I was a printer's devil on a
country rag the proprietary arranged for a serial
story from the rapid pen of Grosvenor Bunster, a
well-known literary guy of those days. He began
one, the impressive title of which was “It Runs in
the Blood” ! The characters were—with the excep-
tion of the harmless but necessary butlers, tenants,
farm hands, village maids, etc.—all people of
exalted rank. 1 hardly think 1 dirtied my fingers
setting up type about anyone less than a duke or a
viscount, a countess or a marchioness. After four
or five instalments the distinguished author had an
elbow-lifting accident (which apparently was the
standard disease to which literary men were exposed
in those days), and his copy did not come to hand
Dick Thatcher happened around, and he was com
missioned to keep the story going and to wind it up
decently. Of course, Dick took on the job in a revel
some spirit, and promptly changed the whole plot, in
the manner in which George Bernard Shaw has suc-
ceeded in recent years, turned all the pompous
characters of the first chapters into buffoons, and
inoculated the comic men with solemnity, married
everyone to the wrong people, and wound up with a
“tag” dragging in the title of the story, which was
considered so necessary in those days. The tag ran
in this fashion: “They are married now and happy,
and have gone to live on the beautiful hill near the
slaughter-yard. They have a tiny cat called Flossie,
and whenever a beast is killed at the slaughter-yard,
It Runs in the Blood.”
* * * *
“Chetwynd”: Why ain’t I an actress? Here am 1
making a perfect hobby of nervous breakdown. 1
have lost very valuable valuables mysteriously. 1
have even got married, lost my bulldog and my
highlv-trained ’guana, and not a word in the press
about it. Now, sir, please kindly insert a personal
paragraph (in any column but the “Wild Cat”)
stating that I have lost my ’guana. 1 enclose my
autobiography so that you can put on some trim-
mings. But be sure to mention my name as the
loser, and how fond 1 was of the ’guana and the
dog. If you, sir, should happen to have discovered
my strayed darlings, you can keep the ’guana. But
be sure to mention specially that I (full name) gave
it to you. in my autograph you will see that I have
trained ten ’guanas 1 If necessary, you may mention
this fact casually. Thanking you very much, in anti-
cipation, 1 enclose sixpence for a drink.
BOHEMICS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Imbued with his art is the artist,
And facile of words, the scribe ;
And they let me into their conclaves
As a waif of the lettered tribe.
But being merely a rhymester
That hangs to the Muse’s hem,
They showed me firmly, if kindly,
I wasn’t of caste with them.
1 tinkled my lyre unnoticed ;
I sang in my wildest strain ;
But they didn’t appear to hear me—
Their diffidence gave me pain.
Then I grew aware that a penman
Was shouting against my ear ;
He was bellowing objurgations
Which nobody seemed to hear.
And I heard a decadent writer
Roaring his noxious creed ;
I didn t bother to listen
As nobody seemed to heed.
I glanced at a skilful limner
Depicting the ultra-nude;
As no one took any notice
Doubtless he thought us rude.
For some were talking of sculpture,
Or something absurd like that;
And some were making conundrums
Which fell uncommonly flat.
For no one hearkened a moment
To aught that the other said ;
Each flogged his ridiculous hobby
Until it was almost dead.
My gorge arose with their foolments.
For, vain to the last degree,
They were each so set on their foibles
That they wouldn’t listen to me!
1 tinkled my lyre superbly
(For I cling to the Muse’s hem),
And they stopped their ears with their fingers
As though I was boring them !
So I took me out to the roadstead
Where the men of the world go by,
And I sang to the motley people,
And (some of them) heard my cry.
It was thus I came to my kingdom
In the hearts of the men that know;
And one of them gave me fourpence
And begged me to cease, and go.
They did not question the metre,
They cavilled not at the rhyme,
And I felt I would set them moving
To the lilt of a livelier time;
These men of the stout yard-measure,
These traders, and men in blue,
Would lend an ear to my message
And (some of them) hear me through.
So I sang to them for an sron,
Till my thumb and my throat were sore;
But at last I, myself, grew weary.
So I’ve taken to work once more!
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

A Sydney girl, Eileen Redmond, daughter of Red-
mond, the mining editor of Sydney Telegraph, has
begun to arrive in comic opera, and has brought with
her a fascinating personality and good voice.
She got her chance as Kissy Fitzgarter in ‘‘The
Belle of New York,” and has made good in Maoriland
with the music and lines of that innocuous Bohemi-
enne.
At Bath (Eng.), three of Australia’s old friends are
head-lining on the Palace bill —Alfred Woods, Maud
Williamson (in a morality play) and Fred Russell
(ventriloquist). A local critic says of the Woods-
Williamson playlet that “it is a wonderful dramatic
episode.” Russell, who visited Australia under
Rickards three times, is classed as “the world-
famous.”
“Circle” : I see that Andrew Higginson (the blithered-
about “Higgy” of the stage-dazzled female) has taken
over the Clare Hotel, at \\ augaratta (Vic.). This
transition from beauty actor to Bung pleases me
more than - I can say in plain print. When “Higgy”
was throwing himself about in the savage clothes of
Danilo, the petulant young mash of “The Merry
Widow,” the shrieks of his long-range admirers were
agonising. You couldn’t go out to squander a
social evening without being deafened with Higginson
rhapsodies. Now he has taken a header into the
beerish obscurity of a country pub and there will
be no more of it. At least I hope not. 1 cannot
imagine the flapper and her elder sister making the
same sort of parrot noises over an ex-publican—-
that is, supposing “Higgy” should ever attempt to
come back from the beer engine.
“Macko” of Broken Hill: I went to book seats.
Standing in the crush outside the local booking-office
was a dame with the best part of a rooster’s after-
thought in her hat. As the crowd pressed the feath-
ered female was wedged against a tall youth, who
was considerably annoyed by the persistent tickling
of his nose. Getting tired of blowing the bird’s
rudder first on one side and then on the other, he at
last, in desperation, opened his mouth and bit off
the offending plumes. And the lady pressed on
unconscious of the fact that her three-guinea lid was
reduced to Is. ll^d.
Dear Bulletin, —In his misguided anxiety to prove
that modern bellowdrama is in advance of the old-
time article, “Billy T.” is moved again to extol the
alleged artistic merits of “Within the Law',” and sug-
gests that I cannot see wherein this Yankee produc-
tion is superior to a Charles Darrell outrage. This,
of course, is the cheapest variety of sophistry. It
would be as reasonable to set up William Wa.tson
against Mrs. Hemans to prove poetry is in a
healthier condition now than it was in the early part
of the eighteenth century, when Keats, Shelley and
Byron were at work. To prove “Billy T. s rash
assertions Bayard Vei Her must be shown to compare
favorably, not with Darrell, but with Henry Arthur
Jones and Sydney Grundy. “Within the Law” cannot
possibly hold its own against “The Silver King’ and.
“The Red Lamp.” Coming down the scale, we find
that Drury Lane drama is not what it was, as wit-
ness the show's of the last two years—“ Sins of Society
and “The Marriages of Mayfair.” And when we
sound the depths and drag up a Darrell, he is liable
to appear in a rather favorable light alongside such
moderns as Mrs. Morton Powell and the distinguished
author of “The Queen of the White Slaves.” 'lrusting
I shall not be called upon further to instruct Wil-
liam” in such elementary matters of theatrical his-
tory—l am, yours thirstily, Harrison O.
H H Vincent, who made herds of friends in Sydney
some vears ago as J. C. Williamson’s general manager,
sends" from London, through The Bulletin to each herd
separateiv, individually and collectively a handshake.
He would like to personally deliver the article, but
his health is in the majority on the question, and the
majority says No. On Henry s behalf, therefore, the
Religious Editor will pass the rum bottle at the old
rendezvous on the sixth Friday in the month.
“Vie” ; Last night in my walks abroad I stumbled
on Snowy Baker’s new Melbourne Stadium. It is
an enormous, square, walled-in place of brick, witty
seating for apparently about. 40,000, in the extreme
north-west corner of the city—indeed, beyond the
citv. On the first glimpse it looks utterly out of the
wav ; but it is close to the railroad lines from Spen-
cer-street, and near by there is a siding for docking
railway carriages, with eight platforms capable of
accommodating a great mob. Besides, it doesn t
matter how much a place looks out of the way in
Melbourne. Cooper and Bailey, about 1878, went
out upon the South Melbourne flat. The local entre-
preneurs said it was madness; yet the site answered
so well that thenceforward there was a rush to it.
Chiarini, Wilson, Cole’s American Circus, Fillia,
Fitzgerald and Wirth went there, and now West s,
Spencer’s, the Skating Rink. Hippodrome Glam-
arium and the Snowden have a permanent habitation
on the flat. Then look at the forlorn Alexandra
Theatre, a shocking failure for 15 -years, but elec-
trified by Williamson as Her Majesty’s ; yet he took
it only in a hopeless kind of way, and because Mus-
grove' had mononolised the Now the
Princess’s has fallen all behind Hfer Maj.’s, and the
fashionable Williamson Theatre is to be built oppo-
site.
THE FATAL BLUE-PENCIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Age and Argus, following the lead of
the Melbourne Herald, have cut out theatrical puff-
pars ; and notices (except on Mondays and first-night
criticisms) are cut down to a line, giving the name
of the theatre, the play and the time for the curtain-
rising. The press agents are highly indignant.
Pity me, Gerald Bonehead, leading man
Of the star company of melodrams.
A reptile press has brought me under ban —
No more they paragraph our lies and shams;
The list of prime donne’s chaperons,
The lonely and misunderstood soubrette,
The “block” of the contralto’s infant sons,
The shriek of the big salary 1 get;
The pretty actor’s tale of how he played
Before the crowned heads of Bethnal Green ;
The story of Miss Fleshtint’s Russian maid,
And how “they cheered me in my dying scene” ;
The actress shooting burglars in the night
(Her father is an English clergyman),
The heavy man who street thieves puts to flight,
And all the tales of those who “also ran” —
Tales of lost diamonds, salary romances,
Yarns of the crowded house we had at Nhill;
Of how the Czar and Khedive loved our dances ;
Of Sultans climbing to our window sill;
Of how our mother with us always tours ;
Of how we long for childhood once again,
In the dealt convent on the Scottish moors ;
And how our loneliness is endless pain ;
Of how we golf, and row, and box, and shoot,
When we re in dear old Hingland, resting after
The West End season and “commands” to boot
(“Commands” which moved the King’ to tears
and laughter).
True, other advertisers are neglected,
And paragraphs on sausage find no place ;
And no one cares if drapers are rejected ;
And no one prints the plumber’s lovely face.
None print the tale of grocers’ inner yearnings.
Nor give three scareheads to a baker’s woe,
Nor praise at length the vain ambitions burning
In him who puts the currants in the dough.
But we are great! Cobblers must sole and heel;
Drapers must know the way to sell their rags •
Bakers to bake and thieves the way to steal,
And tailors how to sew the legs of “bags.”
But we are The Profession ! And our frauds,
If they but gain a loud publicity,
May long be undiscovered on the boards!
So cut not out our names, or dead are we!
N.S.W.
MARTIN LUTHER.

CAN 1 OBTAIN A PATENT?
The quickest way to ascertain
this, WITHOUT EXPENSE, is
to write to me describing your invention, enclosing
a small sketch where necessary. Nicety of drawing
is not essential; all that is necessary at first is to
give me YOUR IDEA.
I will at once inform ' you whether the idea is
patent-able, and, if so, give the necessary instructions
for further procedure.
Persons desirous of obtaining really reliable advice
or information regarding Patent matters are cordi-
ally invited to consult me personally or by letter. In
all" cases a careful consideration of their plans, an
honest opinion, and a prompt reply may be
expected.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it ; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —-
the strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What
a relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt
fe>£.
the ear. It fits the ori-
fice of the ear with pre-
cision and comfort. The
division that runs across
the mouth is to allow the
removal and insertion of
the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the he a r ing-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
if you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without
the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order Form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hogskin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of The
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J. P. Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

New Zealand'
IN
BANANA LAND
The Sugar Cane Price Board Bill, which
Rankin introduced to the Bananaland As-
sembly the other day. will put new heart
into the industry, if it passes. It follows
naturally on the increase in wages that the
farm-hands have got. The present base-
price of cane is 13s. a ton, and the wage of
the cane-cutter is Is. an hour. The figures
can’t be reconciled, if the cane cocky is to
make a do of it. The grower knows it —has,
indeed, known it for quite a long time—-but
has always' possessed the vertebra of a
mollusc, and the C.S.R. and kindred spirits
in the milling game have tramped him in
the mud of his own canefield. The Bill which
has come to his rescue provides for compul-
sory conferences between farmer and miller,
and the miller won’t have the thick end of
the stick, unless the chairman happens to
be the sort of person he shouldn’t be. Ran-
kin has behind him 23 members pledged to
support the interests of the cocky, whether
he be cow cocky, mixed cocky or sugar
cocky. They all have sufficient intellect to
vote, and with the help of the party led by
Ryan they may force the Bill through. And,
after all, it shouldn’t nepd much forcing’
It merely provides that a fair price shall be
fixed on the evidence forthcoming; and if
the farmers ask for a price that isn’t fair
because the millers can’t pay it, then they
won’t get it. If the millers object, the only
conclusion will be that they don’t want a
price fixed in the light of reason ; and with
that sort of objection no sane Legislature
can have any sympathy.
About a year ago Queensland, urged by
frenzied individuals who howled about “the
degradation of our womanhood,” and didn’t
want to know about the degradation of our
manhood and the tainting of unborn child-
hood, abandoned the compulsory examination
of Flossie. The “reforms” didn’t include
provision for the extermination of Floss;
it merely put her behind a screen, so to
speak, and let her become a more frightful
menace than she could ever have been when
she was obliged to face the doctors periodi-
cally. The Government is realising now
what a pit of corruption it was induced to
dig. Consequently a proclamation has been
issued announcing that the system of exami-
nation will be resumed; moreover, cases of
venereal disease must be notified, and dis-
eased women will be isolated in a special
hospit: 1 ward. But there won’t be much to
be cheerful about until all the big towns, at
least, are made examination areas, and the
notification of all venereal cases, for all
sexes and at all places, made compulsory.
CIRCUMSTANTIAL EVIDENCE.
LIŢLE WILLIE: “Oh , mat Is that Danie?"
Winton is well-named. In the last 31
months the sunburnt little hamlet has drawn
£25,000 in prize money from Tattersall’s
s;weeps. The first public statue to go up
there will be one of the late George Adams.
Home Secretary Appel—whose kindliness
and straightforwardness even Oppositionists
admit —was moved to indignation the other
day when a deputation told him that neither
the Brisbane Children’s Hospital nor the big
General possessed an isolation ward for
measles and whooping cough, and that,
though the General- took such cases occasion-
ally, the Children’s wouldn’t unless there
were “complications”—such as pneumonia.
One infant had died, the deputation said,
through its sick mother having been obliged
to carry it about in her arms, and there
were other cases of much the same sort.
Appel bubbled over with indignation at the
statement that youngsters with plain
measles had to wait till complications came,
and remariied that if the Children’s Hos-
pital couldn’t provide for such cases it would
be a question whether the big bequest from
Mount Morgan Hall could be subsidised.
How it would improve the situation to shut
off the hospital’s supply of money Appel
didn’t explain. It might be a fair thing
to stipulate for the treatment which Appel
says is necessary ; but to stop supplies, when
presumably there are not supplies enough as
it is, looks a good deal like giving the erring
hospital the excuse to err more than ever.
The Wandering.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wandered last night from the • land we
make pelf in,
To pick up the fairies, with Puck for a
guide;
We went down a way that was winding and
elfin,
To buttercup meadows that nodded in
pride ;
The pixies came out in their finest apparel,
A butterfly wing in each hand for a fan.
And musical fays came along with a carol
To soften the heart of a Terrible Man.
They pelted me down with the sweetest of
posies,
They beat me to earth and commanded me
kneel;
They fastened me then with their fetters of
roses,
And round me they ran in a magical reel.
They summoned Queen Mab, and she came
in her splendor;
She gazed on the captive and told him to
rise—
Oh ! Lucky the man whom she calls to sur-
render,
And prisons eternally there as a prize !
We banqueted then at a mushroomy table,
On bluebells of honey and goblets of dew ;
A tiny bird sang like the bird in the fable —
“A little nest up on a branch in the blue!”
The elves were in fettle and jested un-
wearily,
Tripping for ever on frolicsome toe ;
The trumpeters blew on their trumpets so
cheerily,
“Laugh and be glad, little folk, when we
blow !”
The dance was begun by a fairy of mettle.
Who spun like a top on her heel for an
hour;
An acrobat tiny, a-poise on a petal,
Gave wonderful dives in the deep of a
flower;
The soul of the scene was a petulant pixie.
With quicksilver filling his veins for his
blood,
And ever for mischief, this mannikin tricksy.
He laughed when he shouldn’t, and kissed
when he could.
And round them for ever, and over and under
The merriment rose and the merriment
died ;
The crickets they piped till they blew them
asunder,
And Oberon laughed at the joke till he
cried;
Louder and louder, till over the airy land,
Rang out the roll of the resonant drum—
Gone were the fairy folk, gone was the fairy
land ;
Music and dancers were parted and dumb.
Shall I go questioning: “What have ye done
for us.
Sages so prosy, and weary and gray?
What in return for our loss have ye won for
us?
Where is your fairy band? Nay, brother,
nay!
Give us but eyes, we shall see the land bound-
ing us,
Peopled with Little Folk, Elfin and Fay,
Here in the joy of our children surrounding
us,
Here on our Earth, with her darlings at
play.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 1, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
On Thursday afternoon the Governor and
Lady Macgregor, with the Vision and the
embodiment of the fashion and beauty of
the hamlet as a background, presented the
drums and fifes that Pearl-king Clark has
donated to the 7th battalion of cadets. The
martial event took place at Victoria Bar-
racks, and, after his Ex. and Colonel Lee
had addressed the battalion, tea was poured
by Mrs. Bruche, and the eternal bun went
the old rounds.
The races to-day were spoilt by some
rather heavy rain. All the females skedad-
dled to the stand when the first drops fell,
but after a few minutes two skirted braves
risked their best hats in order to see which
quadruped was likely to bring home the
dividend. One protected a lengthy ostrich-
feathered glorification with a purple silk
sunshade. The other, having no gamp,
calmly betook her millinery bouquet into the
thick of the downpour. There were plenty
more headpieces where that one came from.
Sir William and Lady Macgregor, with
the Foxton aide, arrived on the course about
three o’clock. Dr. Hopkins, Charlie Morris
and Judge Lukin and his wife were drafted
into the vice-regal pen for the afternoon.
Some very glad rags were removed into
the grandstand when the rain began. One
lot was the property of Mrs. Blume, and
another covered Mrs. Isaac Moore. Mrs.
Ralph Clifton looked smart in black and
white, and Mrs. Jack Thomson was as usual
one of the best-dressed women on the lawn.
Two f§tes claimed the attention of the
Macgregor this week. One was the Prohibi-
tion party’s fair at the Temperance Hall,
and the other was at the Kennedy Terrace
Church. The most inspiring feature of the
former was the picture of a fat man strid-
ing along with a megaphone in one hand,
the other being engaged with two or three
chains, on which wriggled several men,
women and children. The title of this stir-
ring work of art was “A Personally Con-
ducted Tour to Hell.” Printed programmes
also were distributed, and in every avail-
able corner were little rhymes and quota-
tions dealing with the currrrrrrsed drink.
When the youthful Rechabites became noisy
while his Ex. was speaking, Chadband
rushed up and said in a horrified under-
tone, “One would think you were in a public
house.” It was a cheerful show, bursting
with brotherly love and bubbling over with
Christian tolerance.
Lockhart Spence, back by the Otranto,
has left his popular lady behind in Fogland.
She won’t return to Sunland until the new
year is glancing round the corner. The
Biscoes, who also came by the Orient liner,
seem to have revelled chiefly in tennis, and
the he-partner waxes enthusiastic over
Maurice McLoughlin’s play.
Arthur Hobbs, a globe-trotting Brisbane-
ite, just returned from England, noted in
John Bull’s island great activity in aero-
nautics, but nearly all the machines used
were of French manufacture. Hobbs also
noted the “primitive agriculture” of the Cold
Land as compared with the Continent, while
Mrs. Hobbs was delighted to find that every
garden in Italy boasted a gum-tree, known
as the “tree of health,” and planted as a
protection against malaria. In the eyes of
Europe the gum is much more characteristic
of Australia than the wattle.
The Otranto bore away a full cargo of
Queenslanders. Mrs. Gerard Gore and
daughter May have sailed to India; Miss
Newton is bound to England for two years ;
and a party of Downs people hurried , „
board at the last minute.
B . r j ld y’ Under-Sec. for Public Work,
and his wife, who have been away since W
March came back to Brisbane this week
-they haven t been all over the world hni
seem to have done a heap of sightseeing
Daughter Mabel and her motor-car f one £
her father’s presents) have been left in
land with relatives.
Litße Una Shanahan (her stage name is
Bna Collins) was married to actor Ham
Trimmer m Melbourne on Monday last
After they have seen the Cup they are going
to tour Australia with a new company Mrs
dimmer is a Brisbane girl, but her'pon*
larity knows no bounds, so to speak.
On Thursday afternoon the Albion P ar k
croquet enthusiasts tea-ed and fed the mem-
bers of the other city clubs. Jolly Mrs
Trotter scooped up two prizes, one for guess’
mg beans in a bottle and the other f or
prowess with a croquet club. There was n»
Prize for the sandwiches and devilled
almonds, which was unfair.
The haggis and the wee bit pipies are
smoking cheerily at the Centennial Hall as
I write. The Scotties are celebrating
Halloween. Dowkin for apples and other
amusements will fill up the time till 10
o’clock, and then the lassies will repair to
some lone spot, armed with an apple and a
mirror, and see what the glass shows them
This old custom is merely an excuse for the
menfolk to have another look in the glass
themselves. Only it isn’t the same kind* of
glass.
The committee of the Children’s Hospital
and several other charitably inclined per-
sons are working hard to arrange a benefit
matinee at His Majesty’s. Percy St. John
and Dan Carroll are lending their aid,
Mayor Doggett and his Mayoress are adding
their municipal weight, and the Vision is
likely to arrange something in the nature of
tableaux. All the picture shows and vaude-
ville artists in the city have promised to
roll the old charity along, and the J.C.W.
firm is being politely approached re lending
the theatre for noddings. Dan Carroll sug-
gests that a sort of Rose Fete would be a
good adjunct, and it is probable that the
wretched citizen who has already been
rushed with Hospital Saturday tags. Ambu-
lance ditto and dead wattle will again be
pursued, this time with stale roses at a shil-
ling apiece.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Georgetown recently art-unioned 4 pig for
the benefit of the district hospital. So suc-
cessful was the gamble that the swine was
raffled five times over, bringing in a net
£BOO to the infirmary. It was then photo-
graphed, and if the Hospital Committee has
any sense of decency it will hang up the
likeness in the institution along with the
portraits of other eminent benefactors.
Yet another result of the foolishness of
crawling up a crooked creek to make a port.
The Kyarra, snorting with indignation at
being brought so far up, and riled at the
impertinence of an ant-like tug which offered
help round the corners, made a swipe at
the insect and sent it to the bottom. Then,
getting sulky at the curses heaped on it, it
left the river, climbed up a sandbank, and for
half an hour defied all the efforts of every-
body to coax it down.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

The ostrich and the sheep, were debated
recently in the Parliament, and
of course a Freetflqdei; got off the gag that
Australia couldn*! produce feathers to equal
the foreign-grown varieties, and that Mrs.
Australia would scorn to wear her country’s
plumes. South Africa exports £3,500,000
wor ( th of feathers every year, and probably
quts several thousands’ worth on its own
Ijead. A heap of good Australian gilt must
go towards making the export figures so im-
pressive. If the Commonwealth could only
buy the stuffing of its wife’s hat from itself',,
instead of sending the cash elsewhere, the
cost of living might go down with a bump.
The cost of living is also largely the cost of
dressing, and man is as much a hat-winner
as he is a bread-winner.
“C.E.’s” note on a vast scheme: —
Cabled that President Shaughnessy, of the Cana-
dian Pacific Railroad, has pronounced in, favor of the
gradual electrification of the 2906 miles- of line that
stretches from Montreal to Vancouver,. Groves, Merz’s
new right-hand man in Melbourne, says it will be
done. There is incalculable water-power in the
Rockies, as in the Nevadas, which furnish San Fran-
cisco with power carried] over 200' miles. Niagara,
supplyipg. Toronto, andi Schwanigan, supplying Mont-
real, wflfi alsq, enter, ipto the Pacific Railway scheme,
w.ifip the, of Quebec. Little alteration
of permanent, way will be needed, and the present
rolling stock will do. The Pennsylvania Railroad is
already being electrified to Pittsburg. The Cana-
dian electrification will begin with the Rockies. Other
vast improvements are proposed to allow of swifter
and more economical freight-haulage. The vice-
president of the railway, who returned last April
from China, reported that an enormous market might
be opened there for Canadian wheat and flour. The
Chinese won’t, he says, eat rice if they can get wheaten
stuff —which is good news, too, for Australia. But
back of the Canadian enterprise I suspect there is
something that isn’t mentioned—the prospect of keen
competition by ships taking the new Panama route
from east to western States, and vice versa.
Douglas Sladen, late of Australia, one of
the seven worst poets anywhere, has been
telling John Bull how to solve his industrial
problems. His proposal is to arm all “free”
laborers, and break strikes by force. He
would establish clubs for “free” laborers
where they could smoke and drink, and view
picture shows, and have rifle practice at
model targets. Furthermore, drilling sheds
must be set up, and military officers provided
to organise, train and drill non-unionists.
It is an excellent programme—if Sladen
wants to see the “free” laborer wiped out; for
the unionists would certainly arm and drill
too. and when the attempt was made to break
strikes by force, the strikers would feel
called upon to break the strike-breakers.
Sladen, by the way, does not advise that Fat
be armed, and drilled, and trained to do his
own dirty work. Fat is force, but only by
proxy.
The commercial ties that bind us to Bull
are weakening. In 1895, out of every
£lO,OOO worth of goods which the Common-
wealth sold abroad, Bull took £7065 worth;
other British possessions £885; and foreign
countfies £2050. In 1912 the figures were:
Bull, ; other British possessions,
£2135; foreign countries, £3BBB. In 1895,
out of every £lO.OOO worth of foreign goods
which we bought abroad, Bull supplied
£7162; other British possessions, £1146;
and foreign countries, £1692. In 1912 the
figures were: Bull, £5876; other British pos-
sessions, £1226; foreign countries, £2898.
We still support Bull with something like
the old-time energy, but Bull has grown
rather tired of supporting us.

What Guns Are Offeree 3
At Your Dealer’s?
FOR a horse or a dog, you of course go to. the
dealer who is known to stock thoroughbreds.
You have confidence in his judgment.
So, too, in sporting goods equipment, you may rely
on that dealer who stocks Remington-UMC rifles and
shotguns. That, in a measure, is proof of his being
well-informed.
Remington-UMC repeating rifles and shotguns are the
last word in breech-loading arms construction. The clean,
trim, thoroughbred lines that make them the sportsmen's first
choice are the result of designing which, among other things,
eliminates the protruding hammer and closes the breech.
You should be informed on these and
other important , points in modern gun-making.
Your name and address on a postcard will
will bring an explanatory booklet.
Remington Arms-Union Metallic Cartridge Co.
Australian Agent, D. D. Bryant, P. O. Box 686, Melbourne

plete and Brake Van Underframes
(6 wheel).
TENDERS addressed to the Secretary,
Department of Home Affairs, Mel-
bourne, will be received up to*'ll a.m. on
Tuesday, 18th November, 1913, for the
manufacture in Australia, supply and de-
livery of:—
Brake Vans (6 'wheel) Complete and
Brake Van Underframes (6 wheel).
Specifications, Plans, etc., will be on view
on and after 21st October, 1913, at the fol-
lowing offices: —

Street, Melbourne; Commonwealth Works
Director, Customs House, Sydney; and
Commonwealth Works Bris-
bane, Adelaide, Perth and Hobart.
The charge for each copy of Specification
will be fl Is., and for each Plan 55., which
must be paid before the document can be
issued.

Brake Vans and Brake Van Underframes,”
and are to be accompanied by the deposit
mentioned in the form of tender.

KALGOORLIE TO PORT AUGUSTA
RAILWAY.
Analytical and Biological Chemist.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
. sons competent to fill the position of
Analytical and Biological Chemist in connec-
tion with the Western Australian Powellised
Sleener Contract. The position will be under
the Kalgoorlie to Port Augusta Railway
Act 1911, which provides that the appoint-
ment will be during the pleasure of the Min-
ister, and may be for any period not extend-
ing six months beyond the date on which the
line shall be declared open for traffic.
Salary. £5OO per annum.
Applicants must be thoroughly capable of
conducting analyses of all classes of materi-
als, and must have a knowledge of timber
and its diseases.
The successful applicant will have to re-
side in Western Australia, and will be imme-
diately responsible to the Engineer-in-Chief
for Commonwealth Railways.
Annlications, accompanied by copies of
diplomas and testimonials, and addressed to
the Secretary, Department of Home Affairs.
Melbourne, will be received until noon on
TUESDAY, NOVEMBER 25. 1933.
The age of the applicant must be stated.
W. 11. KELLY,
for Minister of State for Home Affairs.

Sydney
Business, Robbery, etc.
The Panama Canal is in a shaky region, and twice
already the American engineers have had to examine
that permanent and reproductive work to make sure
it wasn’t cracked or otherwise damaged. The Suez
sand ditch, which has been such a profitable invest-
ment for the British Government, leads a flat, peace-
ful life, and returns large dividends with commend-
able regularity ; but the Panama “water bridge”
threatens to have a jumpy existence.
“Vagabond” (from an English shipyard) : Even
while the papers are printing the usual glowing
accounts of the 18,000-ton Ceramic, and before she
has had time to grow one respectable-sized barnacle,
there washes round something more solid than the
rumor of a new 20,000-ton liner for Australia. Pro-
bably before the last rivet is hammered home the
drawings will be made for a 25,000-tonner, and that
will be chased off the slips by one of 30,000 tons.
This is the day of big ships, and there are good and
plenty reasons for ’em. It has been conclusively
proved that they are more economical than smail
ones. Only the other day the fact was clearly de-
monstrated by experts that 11,000 tons deadweight
can be carried for 20 per cent, more than 6000 tons.
In the face of that, what can you expect but big-
ger ships ? But perhaps there is one thing more we
may expect, or at least hope, and that is that if
we Australians can’t get our old ports made deep
enough, we may use some of the new ’uns that are
deep and ready made.
“Three Stripes” : The epidemic of shoplifting which
Sydney’s stipendiaries despair of putting down would
be considerably reduced if firms increased the num-
ber of their vigilance hands, and, instead of hiding
them away behind peepholes or at the back of bales
of chiffon, decked them in some sort of uniform and
brought them to the front. Most of the “lifting” is
the work of impulse, and the impulse carries all
before it, because no official seems looking. One
visible orderly would be worth a dozen that are
hidden.
The resumption and rebuilding of the Sydney Rocks
has been so badly managed that a number of Chinese
and Hindus have been permitted to occupy houses
or tenements, while former white residents have
been forced out into more or less distant suburbs.
Last week the uproar thereby caused reached the
Treasury, whereupon notice was promptly given to the
aliens to quit. The tinted supplanters are mostly
hawkers, and can live anywhere, whereas the whites
are chiefly employed about the wharves, so the
Ministerial decision is a good one, and would have
been better still if it had been come to earlier.
“C.C.” The old gaol at Berrima (N.S.W.), once
notorious for its “solitary treatment,” will soon be
put to more prosaic use. A Sydney man is installing
machinery in portion of the building, and hopes to
turn Brer Rabbit into (1) Compressed soup ; (2) an
embrocation for rheumatism and burns; (3) a very
strong glue; (4) a poultry food; (5) gelatine; (6
and 7) two varieties of manures; (8) skins; (9)
tails for export to Germany, where they will be
used in toy-making; (TO) profits; (IT) more profits.
Meantime the local cocky runs his thick fingers
through his thin hair, and wonders vaguely what
they’ll be up to next.
“Ahaswer”: One trust has been wiped off the sur-
face of Australia, and the way of it may be useful in
the treatment of others. A dozen years ago, pro-
bably two million school slates were sold annually
in Australia. Then some enterprising business men
cornered the market. At once the twopenny slate
went to sixpence, the threepenny to eightpence, and
the big sixpenny selection or allotment to a shilling.
The Trust made money fat. But just then the Edu-
cation authorities discovered that the use of slates
was unhygienic. Orders went out that slates were
to be abolished, and children from the infant classes
were to be taught to use pen or pencil and paper.
To-day, in Victoria, and probably it is the same in
the other States, youngsters hardly know what a
slate is. It’s doubtful if there are 5000 in the whole
of this State. And a gang of sore-headed kid rob-
bers are cursing interference with Private Enterprise.
Certainly the slate is unhygienic if used in the old
way. I remember a pleasant old-fashioned school-
master of very bygone days—the kind with the faded
tail coat and the soiled, frayed cuffs —expectorating
on a slate and then cleaning it with his shiny elbow
preparatory to laying down the foundations cf a
sum. The whole school expectorated on its slate,
and the girls cleaned off the finished sum with a skirt
or an apron.
* * * *
“Mil”: Pearl-shell, tortoise-shell, trepang and
pearl fishing brought the Commonwealth £433,441
in 1911, and in the four years ending 1911 the value
was close on two millions. Yet the North isn't
scratched; there is scarcely a trader in the Gulf,
and the 80,000 square miles on the Barrier Reef is
as it was when Cook struck it in the Endeavor. The
Gulf of Carpentaria was worked for a period ante-
dating Tasman and Dampier, by Malays who came
down from Macassar on the north-west monsoon and
returned on the sou’-east. In later years the quan-
tity of trepang they declared certainly didn’t repre-
sent their total catch; vet they paid duty on an
average of £BOOO a year. Four years ago the Common-
wealth warned off the aliens, as they were making the
aboriginal do all the work for them, and in payment
debauched him with spirits, horrible diseases and
opium. The Binghi is not permanently out of work,
however, as a co. styled The Torres Syndicate, Ltd.,
was formed the other day and has begun business
with a lugger and a small steamer. Trepang, which
is a small one of the co.’s objectives, is worth from
£45 to £lBO a ton, being bought principally by
China. From it is made the most potent soup on
earth —“the electric soup,” as they call it in
Shanghai
WHEN THE BAILIFFS ARE IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Japanese nation argues that the next big loan
will place China in the bailiff’s hands.
—Cheerful cable news-item.
In olden days when nations brave
Fell upon evil days, they hurled
The fighting men in one huge wave
Against the overwhelming world.
They burned their ships, they sank their hoards,
Their children to the flames they cast,
They turned their wealth to spears and swords
And perished fighting to the last.
But Change runs ceaseless with the suns:
The modern State, at ruin’s marge,
Flies to its armory for guns
And finds—the Bailiff’s Man in charge.
That worthy bloke, with fingers greased,
Points to the empty racks, to show
The posted bill: “ These weapons seized
For moneys owing. —lIvEY MO.”
Then the defenceless horde, in woe
(Amid more solvent nations’ hoots),
Crawls cringing to the conquering foe
And kisses its collective boots
A feat most bitter to the mouth
When Uncle’s client-nations fall.
There is a People of the South
May heed the writing on the wall.
VICTORIA.

AS HIS STOMACH.”
This Persian Epigram is the Real Gauge of a Man’s Life
Persians were a very sagacious people, noted, among other things, for their
deep thinking on life and the things which make up life.
The above epigram shows the wisdom of their thought.
When a man’s stomach is able to furnish new material to the system as fast or
aster than the natural decay of man requires, then such a man lives his fullest and
his best.
When, through wrong living or disease, a man’s stomach begins
o tax the other organs and takes from the blood strength which it

cannot give back in nourishment taken from
food, then begins the death of man, and he
decays fast. The stomach is strong, splendidly
strong, and can stand an untold amount of
abuse and neglect, but when it dies, man dies.
The stomach gives tons upon tons of good,
rich blood every year to the system, and
draws only 6801 b. of nourishment for its own
use. If, however, the food whicii it receives
cannot be turned into blood which is capable
of use by the body, then the stomach receives
no help from the other organs.

Keep Your Tootsies Warm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LIFE’S not all beer and skittles, son,
nor is it cakes and ale ;
The luckiest find troubles as they go ;
No man has walked without regrets along
the safest trail
From cradle-rock to coffin-thud below.
Old Adam, if the tale be true, got up against
his share,
And all his sons and daughters multiform
Have had it as a legacy. Yet why should
we despair?
Pluck up your heart and keep your toot-
sies warm.
He is no friend of mine the man who drags
his sorrows in
And chants a psalm about them in my ear,
And groans about his rotten luck, and makes
a croakful din,
And lets his teardrops mingle with his
beer.
I rise in wrath to say to him : “This is no
place for you ;
Go out and buy a pint of chloroform,
And hasten to the boneyard. While you
perish in the dew
I’ll brighten up and keep my tootsies
warm.”
There is a sad and mournful bloke who
meets me now and then,
And tells me of the doubts that rack his
soul
Concerning whether after death we’ll draft
to Heaven’s pen,
Or down to where the Devil stacks the
coal.
I say to him : “Belov&d friend, I do not care
a cuss;
This world below is sweet and rubiform.
I hope the next has halos; but, If uot, why
make a fuss?
Now, stand the drinks and keep your
tootsies warm.”
“All flesh is grass,” the parson says, by
which he means, no doubt,
That Life’s not worth the game; that it’s
a fraud.
Well, let him call it what he likes (say,
spiritual sauer-kraut),
It offers me the goods that I applaud—
The scent of honeysuckle blown upon the
fainting breeze,
The old cicada’s song, the bees that
swarm,
The falling star that scrawls the sky, the
soughing of the trees;
Why, those alone should keep one’s toot-
sies warm.
And then there’s all the girls we know, and
those we’d like to know,
And here and there a man we’ve grown
to love.
Why, dash it, when I balance up the joy
against the woe,
I’d sooner be on earth than Un Above !
Maybe I’ll never go Above (my sin book
must be filled),
But Frovidence will not hear me per-
form ;
I’m satisfied with what I’ve had, and as for
what I’m billed,
The fight is his who keeps his tootsies’
warm.
N.S.W.
THE SNAKE.
Jessie’s Elopement.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JESSIE MACPIIAIL was Scotch—
and original. The most original
thing she did was to elope with Jack
Meadows and make him think he had eloped
with her. If there were ever a man whom
his friends believed capable of escaping the
wiles of women it was Jack. It was not
because they thought he had a cunning that
could overmatch a woman’s, but because he
was protected by a monomania. To him
women were merely skirted nuisances who
never knew which way to dodge his motor-
bike.
He was twenty-seven when Jessie found
him good in her eyes. Other women had
found him likewise; but they had all come
to hate him. It is said that woman is at
the feet of a man who can do without her ;
but few women can stand being walked
over unseen.
Jack thought of nothing but his powerful
motor-bike. He spun about the country
like a man possessed, acquiring fame for a
reckless daring, and a skill with motors that
led him to some ingenious ideas. These he
put into the hands of Jessie’s father for
business exploitation, and considerable
profits arose. Jessie knew these things as
well as everybody else—or a little better,
because she was Scotch. She was also
Pretty, and knew it; also she knew that the
tact was not of the least interest to Jack.
■A- pretty woman rarely need be daring. But
pretty women rarely fall in love with mono-
maniacs. In her elopement with Jack there
was as much impulsive anger as love. Win-
mug alone preserved her love, which was
fortunate— for Jack.
‘Jack, don’t you think there would be
more pleasure for you if you had somebody
■°.share the joy of your break-neck rides’.”
( h—l s’pose so.”
You’ve invented several things. Why
uot make a comfortable seat over your back
w ueel, and take a friend?”
That’s a good idea, Jessie,” he said
thoughtfully. “But I don’t think I could
to come * It's risky.”
I’d come.”
“The dad wouldn’t let you.”
He needn’t know.”
i( Hut the risk?”
“Are you so poor a mechanic?” The
mechanical risk was all he saw. Jessie
could have boxed his ears.
When he arrived home that evening he
began to work out the idea of a back seat,
to be constructed neatly, strongly ; a pillion
seat. In the course of a week it material-
ised.
One Sunday morning he popped proudly
c? a street corner a discreet half-mile from
Jessie Macpbail’s home, and found Jessie.
khe glanced dubiously at the little seat. It
pad a back, a foot-rest and a cushion, and
it was sideways across the back wheel. But
looked longer at Jack.
, I’m game,” she said. Then she smiled
delightfully.
;;Hp w long have you got?” he asked.
, , . day.” He lifted her into the seat,
and kicking the stand into its hold, he got
astride the saddle.
Hang on, Jessie,” and touching a shiny-
tooking pedal with his feet, the street corner
glided away.
. warm caress of the summer was still
m autumn. Rain the previous night
had washed the air and laid the dust. The
speed-created breeze began to murmur in
their ears, accompanied by the ‘‘phut phut”
ot the twin cylinders, and the jingle of
vibrating metal. They wound in and out
ot the house-crowded streets, the exhaust
smacking back from the walls in sharp,
double echoes, and entered the more scat-
tered suburbs, where the houses were built
back from the street, with gardens before
them ablaze with chrysanthemums. The
houses became further apart, and slipped
behind more rapidly as the open road was
gained, and the smell of the city gave place
to the odor of moist earth and stubble. The
machine chattered joyously under its double
burden, and the road rolled from under like
an endless ribbon. Up climbed the hum-
ming bike, and the city sank behind it,
spreading out, and hiding itself modestly
under a blanket of pearly haze, through
which showed at intervals shadowy domes
and spires, and blocks of tall buildings,
which slowly merged into the mist. At last
all that could be seen of it was a widespread
haze splodged with darker patches of smoke.
Then fences became a streak, telegraph
wires undulated in abrupt ripples, the land-
scape sprang from a point, widened, and
closed into a point, a roar from the engine,
a gusty snarl from the .wheel spokes, and a
shriek were in Jessie’s ears as the wind
rushed past them. The machine bounded in
long leaps. At times it vented an eldritch
scream as a black spot appeared ahead,
grew bigger, and flashed by, in a blurred
image of a man, a horse or a vehicle.
“I believe he’s trying to frighten me,”
thought Jessie. She set her teeth and
closed her eyes. Grains of sand struck her
face through its veil like pellets from a gun.
But there was an exhilarating sense of
pleasure in the thought that his body would
be a fender in event of a smash. She began
to feel tired with nervous strain, but the
humming, roaring, shrieking demon beneath
her leapt on relentlessly. The wind went
through her clothes and chilled her; but
she was grimly determined to face every-
thing rather than let him see she was
scared.
They were still rising, winding in and
out along a narrow shelf of mad. which
always revealed another curve when the
last was rounded, and the whole earth ap-
peared to topple from side to sid" as they
swung in and out. Jack sudderl.v slowed
down. Jessie opened her half-closed eyes
on a broad panorama, sun speckled, yellow,
green and blue ; in the distance nurpie.
“We’re on top of the Divide Jessie, and
we’re going to descend. Yon just hang on
to me.” Before she could plead for a rest
the machine was roaring again. There
was a sinking sensation inside Jessie, like
descending in a lift, which made her speech-
less, partly with anger. She felt like a fly
on a bounding football. She snuggled her
nose into Jack’s linen-clad back, and did not
raise it till she felt the speed slackening.
Before them was an undulating plain,
lagoon-studded, and a winding river in the
foreground. On one side was a many-
spanned. high railway bridge, before them a
low traffic bridge, further on houses and
railway yards. Crossing the bridge Jack
popped along a high, grassy bank, and
stooped. Jumping off, he immediately bent
to look at the machine. Jessie jumped off,
and was reaching for his hair when her own
tumbled about her shoulders. Jack rose
and considered his watch,
“Sixty-five in an hour and a quarter,
Jessie. Not so bad with two.”
“Where are we?”
“Seymour; we’ll be in Bendigo for lunch.
Geelong for afternoon tea. and in Melbourne
for dinner.” Jessie went and sat herself
on the bole of a willow. Jack utterly forgot
her as he hent to inspect the machine again,
though he talked to her volubly about its
details. She happened to glance round and
saw him very carefully examining a short
length of rubber pipe.
“What’s that for, Jack?”
“To take up the vibration.”
“T mean the pipe itself.”
“It’s to supply the engine with lubricating
oil ”
“Would it matter if it broke —much?”
“Rather. Air-cooled engines, you see.
require” But Jessie bad ceased listen-
ing. She was busily thinking when Jack
looked round.
“Shall we go on now, Jess?”
“It’s awfully nice here, Jack. Let’s stay
a b'ttle longer.”
She leant easily against the bole of the
willow, and with half-closed eyes gazed
dreamily at the swirling water. Jack flung
himself full length a little way from her
feet. He watched the water too, for a short
while, then he glanced casually at Jessie’s
feet. His eyes became interested. _ They
were small feet, with finelv-arohed insteps,
covered bv dainty shoes. The silk stockings
vanished in a graceful curve under the edge
of a skirt which just touched her neat
ankles. His glance went along the hidden
lines of her limbs to the waist, swept over
the curve of her bust to the neck —and he
met her eyes, watching him.
“Looking at me as if I were a bit of
machinery, Jack?”
“By gum. Jessie, yes and no!” There
was genuine surprise in his eves.
“Still, it’s the mechanism that appeals—
to you?” There was a ring in her voice
that disturbd him.
“I think if must be. Yet. somehow —stand
up, Jess. She obeyed laughingly, which hid
the savage feeling that really made her eyes
bright. He looked her over with narrowed
eves, impersonally, like an engineer examin-
ing an unusual kind of machine.
“The bike’s a clumsy makeshift to you,”
he said sincerely.
“How long has it taken to make motor-
bikes. Jack?”
“Ten years or so.”
“How long has it taken to make us?”
“By gum, a million or two! But what a
devil of a lot must have been scrapped—

“Where are the scrap-heaps, Jack?”
“Slums, hospitals, gaols and morgues.”
"You’ve forgotten two things.” lie looked

“I never thought of that. Marriage—

On the way to Bendigo Jessie felt uneasi-
ness in Jack. He drove more slowly, and
appeared often uncomfortable. It was not
till she discovered that he was comfortable
when her arm was not round him that she
realised what made him uncomfortable. It
was a very angry girl who had lunch at
Bendigo. When it was over she begged to
be excused for a while. When she returned
her eyes were very bright. He saw her hair
had been recoiled, and her cloak brushed.
A small crowd gathered to see them start.
They moved off amidst the jeers of small
boys, to whom everything new is jeersome.
Fat isn’t in it for conservatism with the
small boy —the elemental child. They made
Jack look distressed, which showed in a fear-
ful restraint in his talk as they hummed
along at a moderate speed. She felt it again
in the uncomfortable wriggle he gave when
she put her arm round him as lie
raised the speed. It was good for
his opinion of himself that he didn’tsee the
vicious look she gave the back of his head,
nor hear the snap of her little teeth close to
the nape of his neck. They entered a stretch
of almost uninhabited country. There was
nothing in sight but sheep and cattle, and
about three miles ahead a green-embowered
cottage. The road was bad and lumpy.
Suddenly Jessie smelt burning oil. it yvas
followed by a slight scraping. Before Jack
could stop, the engine was squealing. Then
everything stopped with a jerk.
“Fired, by gum!” said Jack in dismay.
Then he murmured furiously under his
breath. “Gehenna.” But he did not look
at Jessie.
“Wliat’s the matter, Jack?” she asked ner-
vously.
“Matter!” He bent and suddenly glared
at the short section of rubber tubing. “Some-
body cut it.... Damn those kids.” Jessie
began to cry.
“I believe you told them to do it,” she
sobbed, not daring to look at him. He
glared at her helplessly. “How —how far’s
—Melbourne?” she wailed.
“About 80 miles in a straight line,” he
said sullenly.
“Can’t you mend it?”
“That’s the least trouble. I’ve lost all the
oil, and it will take at least three hours to
free the pistons. He glanced with a des-
pairing kind of hope in the direction of the
cottage, now only a mile away. Then kick-
ing the engine free, he started to push the
machine.
The owner of the cottage was a frowsy
man with chaff in his beard and hair. In
response to a question about oil, he said
“Aw,” which Jack interpreted as “Yes,”
when he also brought out a bottle of com-
mon salad oil. He looked surly when Jack
shook his head. He told them the nearest
township was 10 miles away, and that it
sold no other kind of oil. In desperation
Jack sat down and began to pull the engine
to bits. When it was together again the sun
had set.
“Can you let me have a horse?” asked
Jack.
“Ain’t got one.”
“Can you accommodate this lady for the
night?”
“Ain’t got a missus,” he grunted, in dim
recognition of the conventions. They were
giving Jack a terrible time. He looked as if
he would like to take an axe to his beloved
bike. Jessie looked at him with a white
face.
“I can put you an’ your missus up for the
night,” said the man. Jessie looked shyly
at her feet.
“Don’t run away and leave me alone with
that—that gorilla, Jack.” It was Jack who
was really the more shy.
A week after Jessie was married to Jack
she told her mother what she had done to
the rubber connection. Mrs. Macphail
raised her hands in enormous commiseration,
and took a very cautious peep at her daring
daughter.
“But what took ye, Jessie? Meadows
was nae a marry in’ man.”
“It was that took me, mither.” Jessie
was Scotch in the intimacy of her mother's
bedroom.

W. SABELBERG.
The Wrong Business.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE man within the 10 x 12 office had
his heels on the table. His eyes
watched the curling tobacco smoke
between himself and the ceiling. A knock
came to the door, a timid little rap that
was half an apology. The legs of the
broker rapidly withdrew to their proper
position, the pipe was laid aside for a pen.
and industry seemed to replace idleness as
he called out briskly, “Come in.”
The man who hesitated forward was the
kind of man you would expect to give such
a knock. He was a little, thin, shiny-black-
clothed individual with a napless tall hat
held nervously in both hands.
“Will you spare me five minutes?” he
asked, humbly.
“Certainly,” said the broker. “Sit
down.”
“I am the Australasian Associated Inven-
tions, Limited,” said the nervous little man
as he took a seat bv the side of the table.
"Representative?”
“No, sir; the Australasian Inventions,
Limited.” said the little man, turning his
face full on the broker.
A sudden look of horror came into the
broker’s face. But he overcame his emo-
tion, and said briskly :
“You asked for five minutes, and I pro-
mised you five minutes, and I’ll keep my
word," he said ; “but” —with emphasis—
"not a minute longer. My time is money.
I only ask you to keep your face to the
clock opposite, and keep your business
within the time stipulated. Now, what do
you want?”
The little man turned his face to the
clock, but turned again at the question.
"The clock, sir,” reminded the broker,
with alarm.
“I'm sorry, sir,” apologised the Inven-
tions, Ltd., turning back to the clock. “I
will not trespass longer than I can help,
but I must lose no time in getting to busi-
ness. Sir, I must let you know what lines
I cal’ry. In the first place I will mention
the El Quiros cigar, which is a little gold
mine. Inventor, a Portuguese whom I met
in Colorado. He had invented a tobacco
juice for giving an exquisite flavor and
aroma to poor leaf tobacco, and was thus
enabled to manufacture the cigar I have
mentioned. Actual cost of production,
three-eighths of a penny; price sold across
the counter of the leading bars and tobacco-
nists, two-and-six. Once smoked you will
never smoke any other brand. The profits
to the Australasian Associated is one-and-
eightpence-halfpeany a cigar. Inventor’s
name is Alfonzo Querriero, a poor but
honest man.”
“Profitable,” commented the broker.
"llow much does Querriero get?”
"Five pounds for the formula,” said the
Inventions, Ltd., turning his face to the
broker again.
The broker almost fell back in his chair
tainting, but by a superhuman effort he
recovered his composure.
“Time is passing rapidly. If you do not
watch the clock the interview is ended.”
“My mistake, sir,” said the little man,
weakly. “I ask your pardon. To resume.
The El Quiros cigar is not in it with the
Instrumental Megaphone. The Instru-
mental Megaphone is intended to do away
with brass bands. When we have placed
this truly marvellous invention on the
market there will be no further excuse for
bands whatever. Where 50 to 60 men,
clothed in gorgeous uniforms, now exist,
we shall have a bugler boy wheeling a
little tin box with a trumpet and delivering
the liveliest and sweetest music imaginable.
This amazing invention is the work of a
German I met in a back lane at Antwerp,
and was bought by me for fifteen and six-
pence. Cost of making, nine and two
pence; scrolls of music, two for three
farthings. Sold to Corporations and the
War Offices of the world for five hundred
pounds per machine, and each scroll thirty
shillings.”
“There’s money in that,” said the broker.
“You have a minute left.”
“I was forgetting,” said the Australasian
Inventions with true humility. “As you
say, there is money in it, but my pet inven-
tion is the Wireless Pressman.”
“The what?”
“The Wireless Pressman —do do away
with reporters, proof readers, outside con-
tributors, and all the hacks of literature.
It is modelled on Merceur’s great invention,
only it collects news, writes in the most
approved diction and submits to the linotype
faultless copy. It takes all the bright
thoughts of all people, and the great
thoughts also, so that the unknown geniuses
of the world contribute to the world’s press
without being conscious of doing so. It
reports great speeches, police-couyt news,
dreadful accidents, murders, suicides in the
most readable form. It is a perfect vacuum
cleaner of journalism, nothing escapes it.
Cost of production, fifteen pounds three and
two pence. Price to me for installation, ten
thousand pounds. The inventor is an
Italian named Gracchi, poor but a genius.
I gave him ten pounds for the rights.”
“But how did you get these marvellous
inventions cheap?”
“They were men of genius.”
“But I asked how did they let these mar-
vellous inventions go so cheaply?”
“Sir. I have explained. They were men
of genius.”
The broker thought awhile, and then
said :
“But why Australasian? What has Aus-
tralia done?”
“Australia is to be one of my customers.
I have named it Australasian for the same
reason that in Berlin I am the German
Associated, and in London the British, and
in New York the American. You grasp
me ?”
“I understand.”
“But,” said the Inventions, Ltd., “a still
better business with me is in the shares.
I am limiting the shares to eight at a quar-
ter of a million sterling, for which I guar-
antee ten per cent, per annum. Three
shares are already subscribed —Rothschild,
one ; Rockefeller, one ; Vanderbilt, one” —
“I cannot understand,” said the broker,
looking round his little shabby office. “Why
do you come to me? Do you wish me to
take a share?”
“No, sir.” said the Inventions. Ltd. “My
business with you is purely personal.” He
paused a moment, and two great tears
began to steal down his che'eks. He had
some difficulty to go on, but at length he
proceeded
“I have come to you for threepence for
some milk to save the life of my mother-
less baby.”
The broker drew a small silver coin from
his pocket and said
“Take it. It is all I have, and I wanted
it badly, but take it and —now, go quickly.”
The old man rose and walked to the
door, when the broker said :
“One minute. Before you go, tell me, is
that an invention also?”
“What, the baby?”
“No, the breath.”
“The breath, sir, is. my own, and quite
natural.”
“Do you mean to tell me that you do
not realise the value of that breath of
yours?”
The old man gazed in silent astonish-
ment. and. su’d nothing.
“Man,” cried the broker, with enthu-
siasm, “what an asset! Get rid of the El
Quiros cigar, the Instrumental Megaphone,
the Wireless Pressman, the Starving Baby,
and stick to the breath. Stick to the
breath. It is a whole brewery.”
Then he wiped the perspiration from his
forehead, and rose from his chair and threw
open the office window as wide as it would
go, and leaned out.
ROBERT BROTHERS.
